# Rachel's Journey

*A Novel*

---

# Chapter One

Rachel Mercer’s life was fine.

That was the word she used when people asked. Fine. A useful word. A word that nodded and smiled and closed the conversation before it could wander into uncomfortable territory. Fine meant a job that didn’t make her cry in the bathroom anymore—not often, anyway—and friends who sent her memes at midnight like proof of continued existence. Fine meant a boyfriend who showed up with fries even when she said she wasn’t hungry, which was a lie she told often enough that he’d learned to translate it.

Fine meant rent paid on time, groceries in the fridge, a car that usually started on the first try.

Fine also meant she could go three, sometimes four days without thinking about where she came from.

Literally.

Adopted was the word people used, said with a gentle finality, like it wrapped everything up in a bow and slid it into a drawer. As if it closed a door instead of quietly pointing to another one behind it, unmarked. Rachel had been adopted as an infant. Closed records. Dates, signatures, official seals. A thin place in her history where a story should have been, where something should have pushed back when she leaned on it.

Her parents—the ones who raised her—were good people. Kind in a steady, unshowy way. Middle-class in the sense that meant stability, expectations, a belief that things worked if you did them in the right order. Love had never been the issue. If anything, there had been too much of it, carefully measured and gently applied. Her dad still forwarded articles about tires and car maintenance, as if one day she might wake up interested in rotating her own tires. Her mom still asked about vitamins, about sleep, about whether Rachel was “eating enough real food.”

Most days, the blank spot stayed quiet.

On other days, it turned into something small and hard she carried around in her chest. You could walk with it. Work around it. Forget it was there until you shifted wrong and felt the pressure. Not pain, exactly. More like resistance.

That morning, the pressure showed up while she waited in line at the coffee place near her office.

Three people ahead of her. One behind. All of them deeply invested in milk alternatives. The barista asked a question. The woman in front of Rachel answered like she’d been personally challenged, voice tight with defensive sincerity. An apology followed, practiced and weary. Rachel stared at the menu board even though she could recite it, right down to the seasonal drinks she never ordered.

Her coffee came out the same as always. No follow-up questions. No commentary. That felt like a small mercy.

Outside, the cup warmed her hands as she walked toward the building. Somewhere between the curb and the revolving door, the thought slipped in the way it sometimes did—already settled by the time she noticed it, like it had been waiting.

I’m missing something.

Not a thing you could name. Not a task or an errand or something you could fix by being more organized. More like everything in her life had been assembled correctly and one essential piece had never been included in the box. The instructions insisted it wasn’t necessary, but the whole thing wobbled without it.

Vague thoughts annoyed her more than sad ones. You couldn’t argue with them. You couldn’t prove them wrong. You couldn’t even say them out loud without sounding like someone who captioned photos with lowercase letters and feelings.

Coffee helped. Or at least the ritual of it did.

Six floors up, the office tried very hard to pretend it wasn’t an office. Exposed brick. Plants that required attention. Artwork chosen by committee, all edges sanded down until it couldn’t offend anyone. The elevator smelled faintly of cleaning solution and someone else’s cologne. Rachel liked the building. She liked the security guard who nodded at her every morning like he recognized her as safe, harmless, already accounted for.

Work was work.

Emails stacked up. A calendar filled itself without asking. A message from her manager started with Quick question and went on long enough to require scrolling. Replies were sent. Notes were taken. A sticky note went up on the edge of her monitor because if it didn’t, the thought attached to it would drift away and come back later when she least wanted it to.

At ten fifteen, Mara texted a picture of a dog wearing sunglasses with the caption you at 3pm. Laughter slipped out before Rachel could stop it—too loud, too sudden. She glanced around her cubicle, waiting for someone to look at her like she’d broken a rule. No one did. That could be read a few different ways.

By eleven, she was in a conference room nodding through a conversation about timelines. Notes filled a page. Agreement happened before she’d fully thought it through. Later would deal with it. Later always did.

Her phone buzzed as she left the room.

Evan: Dinner tonight? I can pick up Thai.

The message got more attention than it deserved, like it might contain instructions. Evan was Evan. Kind. Reliable. Comfortable letting Rachel decide things, which some days felt like freedom and other days felt like being the only one holding the map.

Yes. Thank you.

She erased the thank you. Put it back. Added a smiley face that felt polite and slightly dishonest.

Another buzz.

Evan: Cool. You okay?

A small frown pulled at her mouth. She hadn’t said anything strange. No clipped replies. No emotional tells she was aware of.

Yeah. Just busy.

A pause.

Evan: Okay. Miss you.

That landed heavier than expected. Sweet. Familiar. Real in a way she didn’t want to examine too closely.

Miss you too.

Her hands stayed on the keyboard longer than necessary, hovering like there was something else she was supposed to type. The missing feeling brushed past again, quick and light as a draft through an open door.

The bathroom break was mostly an excuse to interrupt her own thoughts.

In the mirror, she looked like herself. Mid-twenties. Brown hair she always meant to do something with and never did. Tired eyes that made her look more serious than she felt. A crease between her eyebrows that showed up when she concentrated and refused to leave, like it had decided to live there.

Cold water helped. A quiet get a grip helped a little more.

The afternoon passed in manageable pieces. Tasks completed. Small annoyances absorbed. Laughter slipped in now and then like cracks in the routine. At five thirty, the laptop shut. A bag went over her shoulder. The elevator ride down included two people who smelled like cologne and one who smelled like new carpet.

Outside, the evening air reset something in her chest. The car unlocked with a chirp. She got in and rested her hands on the steering wheel for a moment before turning the key.

Just a moment. A breath. The transition from work to home.

She'd always done this. Never questioned it.

Traffic moved. The radio played something unfamiliar. Sunlight flared low through the windshield. Evan appeared in her mind carrying takeout bags, knocking with his elbow, smiling like he expected praise. A smile showed up on her face without permission.

Her brain followed it with something less helpful.

If you disappeared, he’d be the one who had to tell everyone.

Her grip tightened on the wheel. Tired explained it. Hungry explained it. An overdramatic brain explained it. The light turned red. The car idled. People crossed the street. The sense of misalignment returned, subtle but insistent, like the wrong soundtrack playing under an otherwise normal scene.

Green.

Pressure built in her ears first—the elevator kind that usually cleared if she swallowed—except this time it didn’t. The air inside the car felt wrong. Thicker. Too close. Her breathing sounded loud in her own head.

The dashboard didn’t help. Speed steady. Fuel low. Radio still playing.

The right lane felt safer, so she signaled and eased over, fingers tingling against the steering wheel as if her nerves were waking up in slow motion. She told herself—out loud, because that usually helped—that she was fine.

Edges sharpened instead. Lane lines too bright. Taillights too red.

The radio cut off mid-note, and for a second she thought her ears had finally given up, because the silence that followed wasn’t just quiet. It was complete. It pressed in from all sides and made her aware of her own pulse.

Rachel opened her mouth to shout and didn’t hear anything at all.

Then the steering wheel was gone.

There wasn’t time to understand that. Just the sick drop in her stomach, hands grasping for something that wasn’t there anymore, her body pitching forward as gravity forgot what it was supposed to do.

Stone came up fast.

The impact knocked the breath out of her. Pain flared through her palms and shoulder as the world slammed back together—sound rushing in, light stinging, air tasting like dust and something faintly burnt.

She gasped and rolled onto her side, coughing, trying to breathe while her heart hammered hard enough to ache.

Cold stone pressed against her cheek.

Pale lines carved into the floor swam into focus, forming a wide circle around her, fractured in places, as if whatever had been drawn there had been pushed too far and hadn’t survived it.

Voices reached her through the ringing.

“No—”

“Oh.”

Rachel forced herself up on one elbow and froze.

Three people stood beyond the circle.

Boots. Practical clothes. A lantern tipped on its side. Papers spilling from a satchel.

They stared at her like she was an equation that didn’t balance.

“Oh,” one woman said. “Oh no.”

Her heart pounded. Rachel pushed up farther.

“Hi,” she said. “Where am I?”

The man blinked. “That’s… a good question.”

The third woman watched her differently, gaze hovering just off Rachel’s outline, searching for something she couldn’t quite see.

Hands open, the woman with the satchel stopped at the edge of the circle. “You’re not hurt, are you?”

A laugh burst out of Rachel, sharp and breathless. “I was driving.”

“Yes,” the woman said, like that explained things.

“You shouldn’t be here,” the third woman said, quieter this time.

“I know,” Rachel said. “But I am.”

The circle stayed broken beneath her feet. Silent. Waiting.

The woman with the satchel opened her mouth, then closed it again. The man shifted his grip on the book, knuckles whitening. No one moved closer.

“This shouldn’t be possible,” the woman with the satchel said.

Rachel looked down at her scraped hands, at the pale lines carved into the stone, at the place where the ritual had failed and somehow succeeded anyway.

“Yeah,” she said. “That seems to be a theme.”

No one laughed.

The quiet stretched. None of them moved to help her up. None of them asked her to leave.

Rachel pressed her scraped palms against the cold stone, feeling the broken circle beneath her, and waited.

Behind her, the doorway stayed open. Waiting too.


---

# Chapter Two

Rachel stayed on the floor longer than she needed to.

Pain explained some of it—shoulder, palms, the reminders left behind by a fall. The rest came from the way the three of them moved around her with deliberate care: voices kept low, steps measured, hands hovering like they were trying to be helpful without making her bolt. Standing felt like a decision. She wasn't ready to make decisions yet.

Something felt wrong, though. Not dangerous-wrong. Just... off.

If she'd been drugged—which seemed like the most logical explanation for appearing in a stone room with three strangers—the world should have felt softer. Blurred. Distant. Instead, everything arrived sharp and clear. Thoughts lined up neatly. Pain registered precise and exact. Even the cold stone beneath her felt impossibly present, every texture insisting on being noticed.

She was more awake than she'd been in months. Maybe years.

That didn't match any story her brain wanted to tell.

The woman with the satchel offered water again.

She held the cup with both hands—steady, careful, like she was handing Rachel something fragile. Ceramic. Warm. Ordinary in a way that felt practiced.

"Thank you," Rachel said automatically, because politeness was a habit and habits were anchors.

The water tasted like water. Cold. Plain. Her throat accepted it gratefully.

The stone pressed through her jeans. She shifted with a quiet hiss. The man noticed immediately and crouched nearby, hovering without touching her, hands braced awkwardly on his knees.

“There’s a bench,” he said. “If that’s easier.”

Rachel followed his gaze. Wooden. Plain. Close enough that getting there wouldn’t turn into a performance.

“I can walk,” she said—and meant it.

Standing took effort, but her balance caught itself faster than she expected. The room tilted for half a second and then settled, like her body corrected before her mind finished issuing the command. The woman stepped forward on instinct and then stopped, catching herself mid-motion.

Rachel clocked that pause. The way she didn't want to crowd her. The way she also didn't want Rachel moving too freely.

She walked to the bench and sat. The wood creaked softly. A folded cloth appeared beside her without ceremony. Rachel used it, wiping dust and grit from her palms, noticing how quickly the sting eased once the dirt was gone.

Names happened, as if everyone collectively decided this was the moment to behave like regular people.

The woman with the satchel went first. "I'm Maelin," she said, voice gentle but firm.

The man followed. "Edrin."

The third woman, the one who'd been watching Rachel with that odd intensity, said simply, "Ilyra."

Rachel repeated them back, tasting the syllables, filing them away. Maelin. Edrin. Ilyra.

"I'm Rachel," she said. "Rachel Mercer."

First name. Last name. Still hers.

Edrin nodded, earnest. "We're really sorry," he said, like repetition might rewind the moment. "This wasn't meant to happen."

“I figured,” Rachel said.

Maelin winced. “Right. Yes.”

They hovered—apologetic, concerned, human.

Which made the situation worse, not better.

Rachel waited for the other shoe. Instructions. A demand. A threat. Something to make this fit the kidnapping model her brain kept assembling.

Nothing came.

Her shoulder throbbed in time with her heartbeat. The room smelled like stone and oil and something faintly metallic. Her eyes kept moving on their own, cataloging exits, distances, body positions, the fact that none of them stood directly between her and the doorway.

"So," she said, because silence was starting to feel dangerous. "What happens now?"

The three of them exchanged quick looks. Ilyra tilted her head slightly. Edrin rubbed his face like he’d already answered this in his head ten times and disliked every version.

“Well,” Maelin said carefully, “first we make sure you’re all right.”

“I think I am,” Rachel said. “Mostly.”

“And then,” Edrin added, “we slow things down.”

Her stomach tightened. “From what?”

“From cascading,” he said, then caught himself. “That sounded—”

“Bad,” Rachel supplied.

His mouth twitched, apologetic. “Yes.”

Her purse was gone. Phone, keys, wallet—everything that tied her to the day she’d been having. Gone with the car. That absence pressed on her harder than the pain did.

Evan would be checking his phone by now.

The thought landed heavy. He would have texted. Once, then again. He worried quietly but thoroughly.

Rachel lifted her chin a fraction. “I need to let someone know where I am.”

Maelin leaned forward at once. “Of course.”

“My boyfriend,” Rachel said. “He’s expecting me.”

Edrin nodded immediately. “That makes sense.”

“Yes,” Maelin said. “Absolutely.”

They didn’t say how.

Rachel let the silence stretch, watching to see if it would turn into a plan.

It didn’t.

Ilyra filled it instead, voice calm in a way that made Rachel's skin prickle. "Time doesn't always behave the way people expect."

Rachel's breath caught. "What does that mean?"

"Sometimes it stretches," Ilyra said. "Sometimes it… doesn't line up. A day here might be an hour there. Or a week. The boundaries don't keep consistent time."

Rachel stared at her. "Boundaries. You keep saying that. What boundaries?"

The three of them went still.

"Where am I?" Rachel asked, voice sharper now. "And don't say a region or a place name I won't recognize. Where. Am. I."

Maelin's hands folded together. "Not where you were."

"I figured that out when I fell through my steering wheel," Rachel said. "But I'm still on Earth, right? This is just—some remote area, or a military facility, or—"

"No," Edrin said quietly.

The word hung in the air.

Rachel's chest tightened. "No what?"

"You're not on Earth," Maelin said gently. "I'm sorry. This isn't your world."

The room tilted. Not like before, not like magic—just the normal way the world tilted when it stopped making sense.

"That's not possible," Rachel said.

No one argued with her.

"That's—" She stood, paced two steps, stopped. "You're saying I'm on another planet? Like, in space?"

"Not exactly," Edrin said carefully. "Not another planet in your universe. Another world entirely. Parallel, maybe, or layered—we don't fully understand the mechanics. But separate."

Rachel's laugh came out sharp and broken. "You don't understand the mechanics of the impossible thing you just told me is real."

"Yes," he admitted.

She wanted to argue. Wanted to demand proof, evidence, something rational. But she'd already fallen through reality once today. And these people, with their strange clothes and careful words, weren't lying. She could feel that much.

"How long has it been?" Rachel asked finally, voice hollow. "Back there. In my—" The word stuck in her throat. "In my world."

Maelin and Edrin exchanged glances.

"We don't know," Edrin admitted quietly. "There's no way to measure it from this side."

Rachel's stomach twisted. Somewhere, in another world—her world—people she loved might think she was dead. Might have already grieved her. And there was nothing she could do about it.

"Is there a way I can send a message?" she asked, voice tight. "Just to say I'm okay."

Maelin's expression softened. Genuinely. "We'll see what we can do. I'm sorry we can't give you better answers."

Rachel nodded, throat too tight to speak.

That answer was a bridge made of fog. Still, she stepped onto it.

Time passed without announcing itself.

Food appeared—bread still warm, soup that smelled rich and comforting. Rachel ate because her body demanded it and because refusing felt like escalation. Her stomach settled.

After that, they asked her to move to a smaller room. Asked, not told. The space was simple: a cot, a table, a cup of water waiting. The door stayed ajar.

"Just until we understand more," Edrin said. "You're free to move around. We just like to know where you are."

Rachel smiled, easy and practiced. "Sure."

The smile came easier than it should have. She was being held—however gently—by people who'd pulled her from her life, and yet some part of her had already started calculating how to make this work instead of how to escape.

That wasn't right.

Even while she smiled, her attention tracked the doorway. The distance to the hall. The sound of footsteps beyond. The way her hearing seemed tuned slightly too high, as if the world had turned its volume up.

Stone and clean fabric made up the room's smell. Rachel sat on the cot and pressed her hands into the mattress, grounding herself in pressure and texture.

Her phone should have buzzed by now.

The thought arrived with uncomfortable clarity. Evan would have texted when she didn't show up for dinner. Then called. Then texted again. By now he'd be pacing his apartment, hands rubbing the back of his neck, that specific restless motion he did when he worried.

He hated uncertainty. Always had. When she'd gotten food poisoning last year, he'd worn a path in her carpet, circling back every ten minutes to check if she needed anything even though she kept insisting she was fine.

"I've got you," he'd said, voice soft and certain, holding her hair back when fine turned into not-fine. "Just let me help."

She'd let him that night. Let him see her small and sick and helpless. And he'd been nothing but gentle about it.

The memory should have been warm. Comforting. Instead it just made her chest ache.

She tried to picture him now—really picture him, the specific way his mouth pulled tight when he was stressed, the gray T-shirt he wore when he was having a bad day—and the image came slower than it should have. Blurrier. Like trying to remember someone through fog.

That wrongness jolted her awake in a way fear hadn't.

How long had it been since she'd thought of him? Hours? Since she'd arrived? She'd been cataloging exits and accepting food and calculating how to survive, and somewhere in all of that, Evan—gentle, patient Evan who'd held her hair back and never asked for anything in return—had slipped to the back of her mind like something she'd deal with later.

The guilt hit fast. Sharp. Real.

Rachel lay back and stared at the stone ceiling, at the uneven pattern of blocks fitted together with a care she couldn't quite name. Her mind kept reaching for a rational explanation and coming back empty.

Sounds drifted through the doorway—voices, footsteps, the quiet continuity of people living their lives while hers hung suspended.

Rachel turned onto her side and faced the door.

Breathing stayed slow. Expression stayed neutral. Thoughts stayed private.

Underneath the fear, something else stirred—warm, present, unmistakably hers.

She pressed her hand against her chest and felt it pulse there, waiting.


---

# Chapter Three

Hunger woke Rachel fully.

It sat low and steady, insistent enough to pull her upright before she had time to think about it. She stayed seated on the edge of the cot for a moment, feet planted, one hand pressed to her chest where that strange warmth had pulsed the night before.

Nothing now. Just her heartbeat, steady and ordinary.

She'd lain awake for a long time after feeling it, that presence beneath her ribs—warm and waiting and impossibly hers. Eventually exhaustion had pulled her under, but her dreams had been restless, full of falling and catching herself mid-air, of reaching for things that moved away from her fingers.

Rachel dropped her hand and listened to the building around her, letting the shape of the morning settle.

Voices carried from somewhere down the corridor. One rose, another answered. A pause. Footsteps crossed stone, slow and unhurried. Wood scraped against the floor, then stopped. The sounds repeated with small variations, a pattern forming without rushing, like the place itself had learned how to breathe.

Rachel stood.

Her balance came easily. Too easily. The ache in her shoulder answered at once when she shifted, precise enough to locate down to the inch. She waited for the fog that usually followed a bad night, the dull drag behind the eyes.

Nothing followed.

The doorway opened onto the corridor without resistance. Rachel stepped through and let the doorframe pass behind her. The floor cooled the soles of her shoes. Light stayed even along the walls, neither brightening nor dimming as she moved, as if the space had decided what it was and didn't intend to change its mind.

She followed the sound of voices.

The corridor widened into a shared room. A long table filled most of the space, its surface already in use. Plates clustered near one end. A pitcher waited beside them, moisture gathering along the glass. Steam rose from a pot near the center, drifting upward before thinning out and vanishing.

Maelin stood at the table, placing plates one by one. Edrin sat on a bench nearby, sleeves rolled, attention fixed on a ledger balanced across his knees. Ilyra leaned against the far wall, weight on one foot, gaze moving between the room and the corridor Rachel had just come from.

Maelin noticed her first.

Her hands slowed, then resumed. "Good morning."

"Morning," Rachel said. She stopped just inside the room, giving herself a second to take it in.

Maelin waited a beat. "You're welcome to eat."

Rachel nodded and moved closer.

Edrin looked up as she reached the table. "Did you sleep?"

"I think so," Rachel said. It felt true enough.

He dipped his head once and returned to the page.

Rachel poured water from the pitcher. The glass clinked softly as she set it down. She drank. Cold spread across her tongue and down her throat, sharp and grounding.

Conversation resumed around her without shifting direction.

Maelin asked Edrin about a delivery. He answered without looking up. Ilyra spoke once, briefly, and Maelin turned toward her immediately before responding. Rachel listened to the timing more than the words, the way attention moved.

She tore a piece of bread free and ate. The crust cracked between her fingers. Chewing took longer than she expected, as if her body wanted confirmation before letting the food disappear.

Her eyes kept moving.

One doorway opened back into the corridor. Another sat partially covered by a hanging cloth. Edrin's bench gave him a clear view of both. Ilyra's position did the same. Maelin worked with her back to the table only when both of the others were facing it.

Rachel set her cup down.

"Did anyone find my phone?" she asked.

Maelin answered without stopping what she was doing. "We looked. It wasn't with your other things."

Rachel nodded and reached for more bread.

Edrin shifted on the bench, adjusting the ledger against his knee. "We'll keep trying."

Ilyra watched Rachel as she ate. Her gaze moved to Rachel's hands, then back to her face, measuring something that didn't have an obvious name.

Rachel noticed the table next. One leg sat shorter than the others. The surface angled slightly toward the far end. Cups clustered closer to Maelin, as if set there out of habit rather than design.

Maelin spoke again. "How are you feeling this morning?"

Rachel swallowed before answering. "Awake."

Maelin smiled faintly. "That's something."

Edrin turned a page.

Ilyra's attention drifted toward the corridor, then returned, sharper than before.

Rachel stayed where she was, eating, listening. Words moved around her without pressing in. The morning continued in small increments--questions answered, objects shifted, footsteps passing the open doorway and moving on.

Rachel rested her palm briefly against the table's edge. Wood met skin. The surface angled away beneath her hand.

She let the moment pass.

Maelin reached for another cup.

She lifted it from the table while speaking, attention angled toward Edrin. Her sleeve caught the edge. The cup tipped.

Water sloshed toward the rim, gravity pulling it down and out. The cup tumbled from her fingers, falling.

Rachel's hand came up without thought.

Stop.

The moment stretched, thin and precise.

The cup hung in the air between Maelin's hand and the floor. Water bulged at the rim, held mid-spill, neither falling nor settling. The drop that should have scattered across the stone simply waited, suspended.

Rachel's breath caught high in her chest. She didn't blink.

Then the room resumed.

The cup continued its fall—but slower now, drifting down like a feather instead of a stone. It touched the floor without breaking, settling upright. The water that should have spilled drew back into the glass as if time had reconsidered.

Everyone in the room had gone still.

Rachel drew her hand back and stared at it. Her palm tingled, warm in a way that had nothing to do with temperature. The sensation felt familiar—disturbingly familiar—like flexing a muscle she'd always had but never known how to use.

Like finding a word in a language she'd never been taught but somehow still understood.

The rightness of it made her stomach turn.

Maelin stood frozen, her hand still outstretched where the cup had been. Her eyes were wide.

Edrin rose slowly from the bench, movements careful, deliberate. The ledger slid forgotten from his lap and scattered across the floor, pages fanning where they landed.

Ilyra's gaze locked on Rachel, unblinking. "What did you just do?"

Rachel pressed her hand against her thigh, trying to erase the warmth. "I didn't touch it," she said, and hated how defensive she sounded.

Maelin's hand slowly lowered. She stared at the cup sitting upright on the floor, water still contained inside as if nothing had happened.

"We saw," she said quietly.

Edrin crouched, gathering the ledger page by page. One corner caught under the bench. He tugged it free and stacked the papers against his knee, movements slow and deliberate.

Ilyra said Rachel's name once. Quietly.

The sound carried across the table and settled.

Rachel swallowed. Her tongue stuck briefly to the roof of her mouth. "It was going to fall."

"Yes," Ilyra said.

Rachel's mouth opened. Closed. Then she straightened, decision forming. "I want to do it again."

Three pairs of eyes fixed on her.

"Rachel—" Maelin started.

"Not a suggestion," Rachel said. The words came out steadier than she felt. "Whatever just happened, it came from me. I need to know if I can control it. You need to know too." She looked between them. "Or were you just going to pretend it didn't happen and hope it doesn't come up again?"

Silence.

"She's not wrong," Ilyra said finally.

Maelin drew a breath. Her hands came together, fingers threading and unthreading. "Are you sure?"

Rachel nodded once. The motion was firm. Decisive.

Edrin finished stacking the ledger and set it down farther from the table than before. He nudged the bench back with his foot, creating space.

The room shifted around Rachel.

Maelin angled her body away from the table. Edrin moved to the side, leaving open space in front of her. Ilyra stayed where she was, weight balanced, attention steady.

Rachel tracked each adjustment.

Her hands curled loosely at her sides. She rubbed her thumb against the side of her finger, feeling a faint tackiness where the table's finish had warmed under her skin. "I didn't want it to spill."

Maelin inclined her head.

Edrin straightened. "We're going to slow things down."

Rachel focused on her breathing. Air moved in. Out. Her pulse steadied enough to count again.

"What does that mean?" she asked.

Maelin answered as she took another step back. "It means you'll have space."

Distance opened without comment. The table no longer pressed at Rachel's side.

Ilyra's gaze softened briefly as Rachel shifted, then settled again.

Rachel rested her palm against the table's edge. The wood angled beneath her hand.

She focused. Tried to remember the feeling—that instinct that had risen when the cup fell. The urgency. The need to stop something bad from happening.

Nothing came.

The table stayed solid beneath her palm. Ordinary. Still.

She waited, hand pressed flat, willing something to happen. A tingle. A warmth. Any sign that whatever she'd done before was still there, still accessible.

The room stayed quiet. Patient.

Rachel's hand dropped. "I can't," she said. "I don't know how I did it the first time."

Maelin stepped closer, voice gentle. "That's all right. We're not in a rush."

"Aren't we?" Rachel's laugh came out hollow. She rubbed her face with both hands. "I need to tell my boyfriend. He's going to be looking for me. My family. My—everyone's going to think I'm dead."

The weight of it hit her all at once. The magic was interesting, frightening, impossible—but Evan was real. And he was out there, somewhere, terrified.

Maelin nodded. "We'll work on that. I promise."

Rachel watched her mouth as she spoke. The pause before the words. The breath afterward.

She didn't press.

The room carried on around her—someone picking up a plate, the pot lid settling back into place, a chair leg scraping before being corrected. Careful movements. Ordinary ones.

Rachel stayed where she was, counting time by the small motions people made when they weren't sure how to proceed.


---

# Chapter Four

Rachel couldn't stop replaying it.

Not the cup itself. The space just before it should have fallen. The slack moment where everything waited. She'd tried once already, right after it happened, but nothing had come. The magic—if that's what it was—had felt as distant as a half-remembered dream.

That had been hours ago.

Now morning light filtered through the windows, breakfast was cleared, and she was still thinking about it. The way time had paused. The way the cup had drifted down instead of falling.

Her fingers pressed together. Released. Pressed again.

Edrin had been watching her fidget for the past ten minutes. Finally, he spoke. "Do you want to try again?"

The question landed carefully, gently.

Rachel looked at him. "I tried yesterday. Nothing happened."

"I know," he said. "But you've had time to think about it now. Sometimes that helps."

Maelin set down the cloth she'd been using to wipe the table. "When it worked—when you stopped the cup—what did you feel?"

Rachel flexed her hand, remembering. "My palm. It was warm. Like... tingling, but not quite. More like a pulse." She shook her head. "I don't know how to describe it."

"That's all right," Maelin said. "Warm is good. That's something to focus on."

"Did you feel anything else?" Edrin asked, leaning forward. "In your chest? Your head?"

Rachel thought back. "My breath caught. High up, like—" She pressed a hand just below her throat. "Like something pulled tight there."

Edrin nodded, making a mental note. "That's the hesitation point. Where you're holding the boundary."

"I wasn't trying to hold anything," Rachel said. "I just didn't want the cup to break."

"That's the instinct," Maelin said gently. "The wanting. The rest followed."

Rachel stared at her hands. "So I need to want something badly enough?"

"Maybe," Edrin admitted. "Or you need to remember what that wanting felt like."

"That's fine," Maelin said, already scanning the table. "We'll keep it small this time. No pressure."

Ilyra didn’t say anything. She moved a cup closer to the edge, then stopped with it half an inch short and glanced at Rachel.

Rachel nodded once.

They cleared a space without announcing it. Plates slid aside. The pot was lifted and set farther back. Edrin steadied the table with his knee while Maelin adjusted the cup.

“Whenever you’re ready,” Maelin said.

Rachel stared at the cup.

She tried to remember the feeling. Not the image. The timing. The breath caught high in her chest. The way her hand had risen without instruction.

She lifted her hand again.

Nothing paused.

She waited, holding her breath, then let it out slowly and tried again. The room stayed ordinary. The cup stayed where it was.

Edrin shifted. “Try not to rush it.”

Rachel closed her eyes.

She pictured the gap. The held drop. The moment that didn't belong to motion. Her hand lifted higher this time, fingers spread, attention narrowing until the table edge pressed hard against her hip.

Something in the air changed.

A pressure built—not outside her, but through her, like her body had become a conduit for something that didn't belong. The warmth she'd felt before flared in her palm, spreading up her arm.

But it felt wrong.

When she'd stopped the cup, the sensation had been warm, yes, but also... light. Quick. Like touching something hot and pulling away before it burned. This was different. The heat kept building, spreading, growing heavier with each breath. It didn't flow through her—it pooled, thick and sluggish, like trying to push syrup through a narrow tube.

Her chest tightened. Not the clean catch of breath from before, but a constricting feeling, like invisible hands squeezing her ribcage.

Then the floor shifted beneath her feet.

Not much. Just enough to notice. Just enough to know it was wrong.

The room tilted.

At first it was subtle—a degree or two to the left, like standing on a boat in gentle waves. Rachel's weight redistributed automatically, body compensating before her mind caught up.

But it didn't stop.

The angle increased. Slowly. Inexorably. Like reality itself was sliding sideways.

Plates scraped against the table, moving on their own, defying their careful placement. The cup that had been sitting innocently now leaned, water climbing one side of the glass as if up had rotated ninety degrees and was still rotating.

Rachel's inner ear screamed wrongness. Her stomach twisted hard, vertigo slamming into her as her balance failed to find purchase. The floor was solid—she could feel it under her feet—but it insisted it was slanted, and her body believed it even as her eyes said everything was still level.

She grabbed for the table edge. Her hand swept through empty air where it should have been.

"Stop," Ilyra said, voice sharp.

But Rachel couldn't stop. Didn't know how. The sensation in her palm had become a fire, spreading up her arm and into her chest where that tight feeling had lodged like a fist. The magic—if that's what this was—wasn't flowing anymore. It was stuck, trapped inside her with nowhere to go, building pressure like water behind a dam.

And she was the dam, and the dam was breaking.

Edrin moved toward her, hand out, then jerked back. "Rachel, you need to—"

The tilt steepened.

Books slid from shelves she couldn't see. Something clattered to the floor in another room. The building itself seemed to groan, wood and stone protesting an angle they were never meant to hold.

Rachel's feet skidded. The stone floor that had been perfectly flat now felt like a slope, like she was standing on a hillside that kept getting steeper. She staggered sideways, arms windmilling, balance completely gone.

Nausea hit like a physical blow. Her vision grayed at the edges. Her stomach heaved, bile rising hot and acidic in her throat.

"Door," she managed, one hand clamped over her mouth. "Outside. Now."

Maelin was already turning. Ilyra had the door open before Rachel finished the sentence.

Rachel stumbled through it.

Earth rushed up under her shoes, uneven but honest. The world snapped back into place as soon as she cleared the threshold, the tilt vanishing so abruptly it left her swaying. One moment gravity was a suggestion her body couldn't follow; the next it slammed back into normalcy with jarring finality.

She bent over, hands braced on her knees, retching. Nothing came up but air and the sour taste of fear. Her stomach clenched and released, clenched again. Sweat beaded cold on her forehead.

The fire in her palm was gone. The pressure in her chest—gone. Like someone had pulled a plug and let it all drain away the instant she crossed out of the building.

Rachel stayed bent over, breathing hard, waiting for her body to believe it was safe.

Cool wind cut across her face, carrying scents she didn't recognize—something green and sharp, like pine but sweeter. Her pulse hammered in her temples. Her legs shook.

Slowly, carefully, Rachel straightened and looked up.

The sky wasn't the right color. 

A pale wash that leaned green near the horizon, thinning as it rose, light spreading through it without glare. Not blue. Not any shade her eyes wanted to correct into something familiar. Just... that. Green-tinged and vast and utterly other.

It should have looked alien. Wrong. Should have triggered some primal rejection in her brain—this is not your sky, not your world, not where you belong.

Instead, some part of her—some part she didn't want to examine too closely—recognized it.

Not the specific shade or the quality of light, but something deeper. The way it settled against her skin felt right in a way Earth's sky never had. The way the air moved through her lungs, carrying that strange sweet-sharp scent, felt like breathing properly for the first time after years of shallow breaths.

She'd spent twenty-six years feeling like she didn't quite fit anywhere. And now, standing under an alien sky on an alien world, having just nearly turned a room sideways with her mind, her body was saying: Yes. Here. This.

The wrongness of that rightness made her stomach twist all over again.

Rachel turned away from the sky, rejecting the thought, rejecting the feeling, rejecting all of it.

Trees ringed the clearing, their trunks familiar in shape—oak-like, sturdy—but carrying broad blue leaves that caught the light in flat planes like solar panels. The color was too saturated to be natural, too vibrant. One leaf tore loose as she watched, drifting down on a wind she couldn't feel, spinning once, twice, before settling near her shoe.

She didn't touch it.

The grass beneath her feet was grass, or close enough. Green, at least. That was something.

Movement caught her eye.

Something crossed the clearing at a distance, low to the ground, moving through patches of sunlight and shadow. Four legs. Fur the color of old copper. The shape of a deer at first glance—long neck, narrow head, careful steps—until it turned.

Rachel's breath caught.

Too many joints in the legs. An extra bend where there shouldn't be one, making the gait fluid but wrong, like watching a video played back at slightly the wrong speed. Each step placed the hoof-like feet a fraction out of sequence with what her brain expected.

It paused near the tree line and lifted its head, testing the air.

The eyes were set too far forward. Predator eyes, not prey. But the body said prey—graceful, delicate, built to run.

Her mind tried to reconcile the contradiction and failed.

The creature regarded her across the distance. Not with fear. Not with curiosity. With simple acknowledgment: You exist. I exist. This is the space between us.

Then it moved on, slipping between the trunks with impossible silence, and was gone.

Rachel stood in the clearing, breath slowing, eyes fixed on the place where it had disappeared. 

That creature belonged here. Moved through this world like it was made for it, contradictions and all. It didn't question the green sky or the blue leaves or the wrong angle of light. It just... existed. Fit.

The way Rachel had never fit anywhere in her entire life.

The way she'd just fit inside that building for one terrible moment, bending reality around her without meaning to.

Her hands had stopped shaking. The nausea had faded to a dull ache. The trapped-fire sensation was completely gone now, leaving only the memory of how wrong it had felt—and the knowledge that it had come from her. That she could do it again, accidentally, at any moment.

Behind her, the open doorway stayed quiet. No one had followed her out. Giving her space, maybe. Or afraid to see what else she might accidentally break.

She didn't blame them.

Rachel took one more breath of the too-sweet air, let the alien sky press against her skin one more moment, then turned back toward the door.

She couldn't stay out here forever. And despite everything—despite the danger, despite the fear, despite having just nearly torn a building apart with her mind—some part of her didn't want to.

The clearing would still be here tomorrow. The wrong sky. The impossible creatures.

But inside were people who'd seen her at her most dangerous and hadn't run.

That had to mean something.

Rachel walked back toward the doorway. Back toward whatever came next.


---

# Chapter Five

Rachel paused at the threshold.

The doorway stood open before her, dark and cool compared to the alien brightness outside. Inside waited answers, probably. Caution. Maybe fear. People who'd just watched her nearly tear reality apart.

She could go back in. Should go back in.

Her feet didn't move.

One more breath of too-sweet air. One more moment under the wrong sky. Her pulse was still too fast, her hands still unsteady, but the trapped-fire sensation was completely gone. The building's interior felt... heavier somehow. Like walking back into pressure she hadn't noticed until she'd left it.

Rachel's hand rose to her chest, pressing where that warmth had been.

Nothing now. Just the steady thump of her heart.

She took a step toward the door.

Maelin appeared in the doorway, silhouetted against the dim interior. She didn't come out, just stood there, patient. Waiting.

"Are you all right?" Maelin asked quietly.

Rachel let out a breath she didn't know she'd been holding. "Are you asking if I'm dizzy or if I'm okay?"

"Both," Maelin said. "Either."

Rachel managed a weak smile. "Dizzy, a little. But not nauseous anymore."

"That's good." Maelin didn't move from the doorway. "Do you need more time out here?"

Did she? Rachel glanced back at the clearing. The blue-leaved trees swaying in wind she couldn't feel. The green-tinged sky that felt more right than it should. The spot where the impossible creature had stood and regarded her with its wrong-placed eyes.

This world was alien. Dangerous. She'd just proven that she was dangerous in it.

And some treacherous part of her didn't want to leave.

"No," Rachel said, forcing the word out. "I should come in."

Maelin's hand lifted, palm up. "You don't have to. If you need space—"

"I don't need space." Rachel stepped forward, close enough now to see Maelin's expression clearly. "I need answers. I need to understand what I just did and why it went so wrong and—" Her voice cracked slightly. "I need to know if I'm going to hurt someone."

Maelin's expression softened. "You won't. Not while we're careful."

"You don't know that."

"No," Maelin admitted. "But I believe it. And I'm willing to help you prove it."

The simple confidence in her voice steadied something in Rachel's chest.

"Inside," Rachel said again, but firmer this time. "I want answers."

Maelin nodded and stepped back, making room. "That's fair."

Rachel crossed the threshold. The air changed immediately—cooler, heavier, that subtle pressure settling back over her skin like a weight she'd briefly forgotten.

Inside, the room had been rearranged.

Not dramatically. Just enough. Chairs pulled back. Objects spaced wider apart. The ledger sat closed near Edrin instead of spread across the table. Ilyra leaned against the wall, arms crossed, posture loose but alert.

They'd cleared the space around where Rachel had stood.

Where she'd nearly torn reality apart.

Rachel stopped just inside the door, staring at that empty space. "You moved everything away from where I was."

"Yes," Maelin said simply.

"Because I'm dangerous."

"Because we don't understand what happened yet," Maelin corrected gently. "And until we do, we're being careful."

Rachel's hands curled into fists at her sides. "I could have killed you. All of you. The building was—I felt it groaning, like it was going to come apart."

"But it didn't," Edrin said.

"Because I ran outside!" Rachel's voice cracked. "Not because I stopped it. Not because I had any control. I just—fled. And what if next time I can't? What if it happens when I'm sleeping, or—"

"Rachel." Ilyra's voice cut through, not unkind. "Sit down before you fall down."

Rachel realized her legs were shaking. She moved to the nearest chair and collapsed into it more than sat.

The room stayed quiet. No one rushed to reassure her. No one told her it would be fine.

"I'm scared," Rachel said finally, voice small. "Of myself. Of what I can do."

"That's smart," Ilyra said.

Rachel's head snapped up. "That's not helpful."

"Wasn't trying to be helpful," Ilyra said. "Trying to be honest. You should be scared. We're scared too."

"Ilyra—" Maelin started.

"No, she's right." Rachel rubbed her face with both hands. "You should be scared. You should probably kick me out. Send me away from here before I—"

"We're not doing that," Maelin said firmly.

"Why not?" Rachel demanded. "I'm a danger to you. To everyone. I don't know what I'm doing and I can't control it and—"

Edrin leaned forward. "And you came back inside anyway."

Rachel stopped.

"You could have stayed outside," he continued. "Could have run. Put distance between us and whatever you think you might do. But you came back. You chose to face this with us instead of alone."

Rachel's throat tightened. "That doesn't make me less dangerous."

"No," Maelin agreed. She pulled her chair closer, slowly, deliberately. "But it means you want to learn. To understand. To control it. And that's what matters."

"You don't know that I can," Rachel whispered. "Learn to control it, I mean."

"We don't," Ilyra said bluntly. "But we're going to try anyway."

Rachel looked between them, searching for doubt, for fear, for any sign they were just being polite before they found a way to get rid of her.

She found determination instead. And something that looked almost like... solidarity.

"Why?" Rachel asked. "Why help me? You didn't ask for this. For me."

Edrin leaned forward, forearms on his knees. "Because you didn't ask for this either. And because—" He hesitated, then continued. "Because leaving you to figure it out alone would be cruel. And dangerous. For everyone."

"Practical and ethical," Ilyra added with a half-smile. "My favorite combination."

Despite everything, Rachel felt a laugh try to escape. It came out more like a sob.

Maelin reached for the pitcher and poured water, then slid the glass across the table. It stopped well short of Rachel's hands.

Rachel stared at it. "You're not handing it to me."

"Not yet." Maelin's fingers stayed loose around the cup. "When you're ready."

Rachel understood. They were giving her control. Letting her decide when to reach for it, when to trust herself not to accidentally... what? Shatter it? Freeze it mid-air?

She took a breath and reached for the glass.

Her hand didn't shake. The glass stayed solid. The water stayed water.

She drank it slowly, tasting the cold, the slight mineral quality. Normal. Ordinary.

When she set it down, nothing tilted. Nothing pushed back.

Her shoulders lowered a fraction.

"Okay." Her voice came out steadier than expected. "What do we do? How do we make sure I don't... do that again?"

"We go slow," Edrin said. "We watch for warning signs. The sensations you felt—the heat in your palm, the pressure in your chest—those are tells. You learn to recognize them before they build."

"And if I can't?"

"Then you get outside," Ilyra said practically. "Away from enclosed spaces. Away from things that can break."

"That's your safety plan?" Rachel asked. "Run outside?"

"For now," Maelin said. "Until we understand more."

Rachel rubbed her temples. "This is insane. I'm insane. This whole situation is—"

"Hard," Maelin finished. "And frightening. And deeply unfair. But not insane."

"What about my life?" Rachel asked. The words came out sharper than she intended. "My job. My boyfriend. Everyone who—" Her voice broke. "How long has it even been? For them?"

The three exchanged glances.

"We don't know," Edrin admitted. "Time doesn't align between worlds. It could be hours. It could be longer."

Rachel's stomach dropped. "So Evan might think I'm already dead."

No one contradicted her.

Maelin looked down at her hands. "We can't send messages between worlds. I'm sorry. That's not within our abilities."

Rachel pressed her palms flat against the table, focusing on the solid wood, the grain beneath her fingers. "So I'm just... stuck here. With powers I don't understand and can't control, in a world that isn't mine, while everyone I know thinks I'm—" She couldn't finish the sentence.

"You said you can't send messages," Rachel said slowly. "But you could send me back. Eventually. When you figure out how. Right?"

The silence stretched too long.

"Right?" Rachel repeated, voice rising.

Edrin's expression turned pained. "It's... complicated."

"Uncomplicate it."

He exchanged a glance with Maelin, who nodded.

Edrin chose his words with visible precision. "The summoning created a connection between you and this world. A resonance. That's why you can do what you do—the magic isn't something you're casting, it's something you're... conducting. From here."

Rachel's chest tightened. "Okay. And?"

"And the longer you're here, the stronger that connection becomes," he continued. "The magic isn't just happening randomly—it's happening because you're becoming part of this place. The world is recognizing you as... belonging."

The word hit like a punch.

"But I don't belong here," Rachel said. "I belong back home."

No one responded.

"The resonance goes both ways," Ilyra said finally, her usual bluntness softened. "The more this world pulls you in, claims you, the harder it would be to send you back."

"How much harder?" Rachel demanded.

"We don't know," Maelin admitted. "We've never tried to reverse a summon before. But the magic you're developing—it ties you here. Every day you stay, every time you use it, even accidentally... the threads get stronger."

Rachel's hands were shaking again. She pressed them flat against the table. "So there's a time limit. On getting home."

Edrin's jaw tightened. "We think so. But we don't know how long. Weeks, maybe. Possibly months."

"Or less," Ilyra added. "Depends on how fast you're integrating."

Rachel laughed, the sound hollow and broken. "So I have to choose. Learn to control this power—which might take weeks or months—or try to get home before I'm stuck here forever. And I don't know which is possible or how long I have before the choice is made for me."

"We'll find a way," Maelin said. "To do both. To give you both options."

"You don't know that," Rachel whispered.

"No," Maelin admitted. "But we're going to try anyway."

The honesty of it—the refusal to offer false comfort—steadied something in Rachel's chest.

She looked at each of them in turn. Maelin, determined. Edrin, guilty and earnest. Ilyra, blunt but present.

Three strangers who'd accidentally kidnapped her and were now promising to help fix the unfixable.

"Okay," Rachel said finally. "Then we work fast. Together. And we figure this out before I run out of time."

Edrin nodded.

Maelin's expression softened with something like relief.

Ilyra uncrossed her arms. "First step: you need rest. Can't control magic if you're ready to fall over."

Rachel wanted to argue. Wanted to demand they start immediately, that every wasted moment was a moment closer to being trapped.

But Ilyra was right. Her hands were trembling with exhaustion. Her thoughts felt fuzzy at the edges.

"Fine," she said. "But tomorrow we start. No delays."

"Tomorrow," Maelin agreed.

Rachel stood, legs unsteady but holding. The room didn't tilt. Nothing shattered.

Small victories.

Rachel looked at each of them in turn. "Thank you. For not giving up on me. Or running away."

"We don't run," Ilyra said with a slight smile. "Not our style."

Rachel found herself almost smiling back.


---

# Chapter Six

Rachel woke to movement.

Not footsteps. Paper. The soft rasp of a page being turned, then turned back again. She lay still and listened to it repeat, the sound threading through her half-sleep until it pulled her the rest of the way awake.

Edrin was already up.

He'd taken over the long table near the windows. Books were stacked in uneven piles, some open, some marked with scraps of paper folded into the spines. A shallow bowl held bits of chalk and a metal compass she didn't recognize. He was reading from one volume while flipping back through another, eyes moving as if the text continued across bindings.

Rachel stayed where she was, watching him work.

He noticed her attention and looked over, blinking as if he'd forgotten she was there. "Sorry. Did I wake you?"

"No," she said. "I think this place did."

He smiled at that, distracted. "The March never really sleeps."

She waited until he turned back to the table before swinging her legs over the side of the bed. The stone floor felt cool and solid under her feet as she stood and crossed the room, stopping a few steps from the table.

"What are you doing?"

"Looking for anything that resembles what you did," he said. He tapped a margin with his finger, then frowned and slid the book aside. "So far, it's mostly things that don't fit."

"That's reassuring."

"It should be," he said. "It means you didn't break anything old."

"Just something new."

"Possibly."

Ilyra was in the doorway again, arms folded, watching them with the same expression she'd worn the night before. "You're already thinking about trying again."

Edrin didn't deny it. "Thinking isn't the same as doing."

"Looks close from here."

Maelin came in behind her with a tray, setting it down on the edge of the table. Bread, fruit, something warm in a small cup. She didn't interrupt the conversation, just made the food available and took a seat nearby.

Rachel tore off a piece of bread. "You keep talking about the March."

"The Near March," Edrin said absently. "It's the unstable region to the east of here—where boundaries break down. There are others farther out, but this one's closest to the settled routes. We maintain this outpost at its edge to monitor it."

"That doesn't mean anything to me."

He nodded. "It's a place where things brush up against each other. Worlds, I mean. Not gently."

"That's comforting."

"It isn't meant to be."

Maelin watched Rachel chew, then said, "We didn't expect to pull a person through. That part is… unusual."

"How unusual," Rachel asked.

Edrin hesitated. "We've never done it."

Ilyra snorted. "He means no one we know has."

Rachel swallowed. "So you're new at this."

The conversation felt comfortable. Easy in a way that made no sense. She'd known these people for what—two days? Three? And yet sitting here with them, eating bread and discussing accidental summoning, felt more natural than dinner with Evan ever had.

That thought landed cold and sharp.

Evan, who'd known her for two years. Who brought her food when she was stressed and never pushed when she went quiet. Who loved her.

And these three strangers—her kidnappers, technically—already felt more like home than he ever had.

What did that say about her?

"We've studied it," Edrin said, pulling her back. "Theory, mostly. Observations. Secondhand accounts."

Rachel looked at the books again, grateful for the distraction from her own thoughts. "Those don't look secondhand."

"They are," he said. "Someone else's notes. Someone else's failures."

Maelin leaned back in her chair. "My mother told me a story once. About a village near the old crossings. She said a woman there could stop things without touching them. Just for a breath. Just long enough to step out of the way."

Rachel froze. "What happened to her?"

Maelin's mouth tightened slightly. "No one knows. The stories don't agree."

"That's not helpful," Rachel said.

"No," Maelin agreed. "But it's the only thing I've ever heard that sounds even close."

Edrin stopped flipping pages. "That's not in the records."

"My mother didn't keep records," Maelin said.

Silence settled over the table. Rachel became acutely aware of her hands, resting flat against her thighs. She lifted one slightly, then let it fall again.

"You want me to try," she said.

Edrin nodded. "Only if you're willing."

"I am," Rachel said. "But I don't want to guess."

"That's the problem," Ilyra said. "We're all guessing."

Maelin leaned forward. "We can stop. There's no requirement that you do anything today."

Rachel shook her head. "If this is happening anyway, I'd rather it happen on purpose."

They cleared the table more out of habit than necessity. Rachel stood where she had before, feet planted carefully, as if the floor might remember her last mistake.

"Tell us what you're thinking," Maelin said.

"The pause," Rachel said. "Not the fall. The space right before."

She lifted her hand slowly.

For a moment, nothing happened.

Then the air thickened. Not visibly. Just enough that Rachel felt it resist her fingers, like moving through water. The table creaked, a low sound that raised the hairs on her arms.

Ilyra straightened. "That's enough."

Rachel dropped her hand. The pressure vanished.

Edrin had reached for his pen, then stopped himself and set it down. "That wasn't the same as before."

"No," Rachel said. "It felt heavier."

The room shifted, barely. Just enough to make the cup rattle against the table.

Ilyra swore quietly. "We need to stop."

Maelin didn't argue. She was already standing.

Edrin ran a hand through his hair. "This isn't behaving the way it should."

Rachel looked at him. "Should."

He exhaled. "We're out of our depth."

That hung there.

"We don't have to call him yet," Maelin said. "We could wait. Observe."

Rachel's head snapped up. "No."

They all looked at her.

"No," she repeated, steadier this time. "You just told me you're out of your depth. That this is 'not behaving the way it should.' You want to wait and observe while I—what? Accidentally tear reality apart?" She stood, hands flat on the table. "Who's got answers? Who can actually help?"

Edrin's shoulders slumped slightly. "Arathen. My mentor. He taught me most of what I know."

"Then call him," Rachel said.

"And he taught you to stop when things go sideways," Ilyra added quietly.

Edrin didn't argue with that.

Maelin looked at Rachel, something like respect crossing her face. "Calling him changes things. It makes this… official. Other people will know about you."

Rachel considered that. The books. The stories. The way the room had tilted, just a little, like a warning. The timer ticking down on whether she could ever go home.

"Then we call him," she said firmly. "Because right now I've got magic I don't understand, a world that's trying to claim me, and a countdown on getting back to my life. I need someone who knows what they're doing. And apparently, that's not you three."

The bluntness hung in the air.

"No," Edrin admitted. "It's not."

Edrin stood, pushed his chair back with care, and crossed to the shelf along the interior wall. His fingers moved across spines slowly, not searching so much as confirming they were still there. When he stopped, it was on a thin volume wedged between two heavier books. He eased it free like the shelf might resist if he rushed.

The cover was plain brown cloth, worn pale at the corners. One edge had been repaired with thread that didn't match.

Maelin straightened before Rachel fully registered why.

From the doorway, Ilyra let out a short breath through her nose. Not a laugh. Not quite.

Rachel watched all three of them react, then looked back at the book. It didn't look like anything. If it had been sitting on her coffee table back home, she would have picked it up, flipped it open, and put it back down without a thought.

"What?" she said finally. "Is that the call-him book?"

Edrin didn't correct her. He carried it to the table and set it down carefully, fingers lingering on the cover as if he were reminding himself it was real. "It's a paired volume."

"So," Rachel said, leaning forward, then stopping herself halfway. "Letters appear? Like magic mail?"

"You write here," he said, opening it and smoothing the first pages with his palm. "He receives it there."

"How long does that take?"

Edrin didn't answer right away. He adjusted the cloth marker, frowned at the page, then said, "It depends."

"On what?"

"Distance. Conditions." He lifted his hand and Rachel saw faint impressions in the paper, old writing pressed deep into the fibers. "On him."

Rachel leaned back. "Does it always work?"

"When it matters," Maelin said.

Rachel turned to her. "That wasn't the question."

Maelin met her eyes. "It was my answer."

Rachel let that sit. "So this matters?"

Maelin reached for the pitcher instead of answering, poured water, and set a glass closer to Rachel's side of the table. The sound of it touching wood landed heavier than a yes or no.

Edrin turned to a page marked with a narrow strip of cloth. The paper there was cleaner, less bruised by use. He took a small tin from his pocket and opened it. The smell reached Rachel first—metallic, sharp, like crushed coins and leaves.

"That's ink?" Rachel asked.

"It is."

He picked up the pen, then stopped, the nib hovering. Not frozen. Thinking.

"Before I write," he said, "I want to be clear."

Rachel folded her arms. "Clear is good."

"I'm going to write what we know. And what we don't." His eyes flicked to her, then away. "And I'm not going to guess about your world."

Rachel nodded. "Okay."

Maelin said quietly, "And you can stop us."

Ilyra pushed off the doorway and came closer, boots quiet on stone. "You can also tell him yourself, if you want?"

Rachel opened her mouth, then closed it. "I don't know what I'm allowed to say."

"That's not a trick question," Ilyra said. "That's the problem."

Edrin exhaled and dipped the pen. Ink stretched thin before breaking. He waited again.

Maelin said his name.

He looked up.

She nodded toward Rachel, then toward the chair nearest the table.

Edrin slid it out with his foot. Rachel took it without ceremony. From there the table felt farther away. Safer. Her knee bounced once before she caught it.

"What are you writing?" she asked.

"An opening. A description. A request."

"Start with 'we messed up,'" Ilyra said.

"That's implied."

"Make it less implied."

"Not like that," Maelin said, already smiling.

Rachel let out a breath she hadn't realized she'd been holding. "Can I see it first?"

"Yes." Immediate.

He grabbed a scrap of paper instead and wrote quickly. When he slid it across, Rachel read it, then read it again.

**Arathen—We attempted a cross-boundary summons and received an unanticipated subject. We need your guidance.**

"That makes me sound like a loose animal," Rachel said.

Ilyra snorted.

Edrin grimaced. "It's an opening line."

"It makes me a thing."

He flipped the paper without arguing and rewrote.

**Arathen—We attempted a cross-boundary summons and brought through a person. Her name is Rachel.**

Rachel read it more slowly this time. "Better. But add something else."

Edrin looked up, pen hovering.

"Tell him I'm cooperating," Rachel said. "Tell him I want to understand what's happening to me. And—" She hesitated, then pushed forward. "Tell him there might be a timer on sending me back, so if he has answers, we need them soon."

Edrin met her eyes, then nodded and added the lines.

Maelin said, "Thank you," and meant more than the words.

Edrin wrote it into the book. The ink sank unevenly, dark at the edges. He paused often, not to think, but to wait.

"You're telling him about the hesitation?" Ilyra asked.

"Yes."

"And the tilt?"

"Yes."

"And the resonance effect?" Rachel added.

Edrin nodded. "I have to. He needs to know everything."

"And the fact that—"

"Ilyra," Maelin said.

Ilyra stopped, hands lifted. "Okay."

Rachel swallowed. "What fact?"

Edrin didn't answer. His pen hovered.

"That's a face conversation," Maelin said. "Not an ink one."

Rachel didn't like it, but she understood. Some things couldn't be written down. "Fine. But he needs to know I'm not passive in this. I'm trying to work with you."

"I'll make that clear," Edrin promised.

When Edrin finished, he didn't close the book right away. He let the page sit, then closed it gently.

"What now?" Rachel asked.

"Now we wait."

"How long?"

Edrin glanced at Maelin.

"We'll know."

Rachel pressed her lips together. Trust a clock she couldn't see.

Ilyra moved first. "We're not staring at it."

Maelin stood, set cups out, tore bread, poured tea. Someone sat. Someone stood again. Steam curled and thinned.

Rachel picked up fruit, set it down. "Okay," she said. "Normal conversation. On purpose."

"Yes," Maelin said.

Rachel looked at Edrin. "What were you like before all this?"

"Before?" He frowned. "I studied binding theory."

"That still sounds like summoning."

"It isn't," he said, then sighed. "It's the structure underneath. The part where answers exist before language."

"You always talk like that?" Rachel asked.

"Only when he's nervous," Ilyra said.

Edrin shot her a look. "I don't—"

"You do," Maelin said. "Less now."

Rachel smiled. "I worked in logistics. Scheduling. Operations. I fixed things before anyone noticed they were broken."

Edrin nodded. "You managed systems."

Rachel pointed at him. "That. You just did my job."

"Then we'd get along," he said.

"I would've made you label everything."

"I already do."

Later—after tea had cooled and been reheated—Maelin asked about Evan.

Rachel hesitated. "He's a physical therapist."

"That sounds useful," Maelin said.

"It is," Rachel said. "He fixes people. Bodies, I mean. He's really good at it."

Ilyra leaned against the wall. "How's he when he's angry?"

Rachel blinked. "Quiet."

"Quiet how?"

She thought about it. "He apologizes. Even when he shouldn't. He starts asking questions like he's trying to solve the argument instead of being in it."

Ilyra nodded slowly. "Fixer."

"Yes," Rachel said. "He'll take the blame just to keep things moving."

Maelin's voice was gentle. "That can be kind."

"It is," Rachel said. "It's also exhausting."

Later still—after Evan drifted away and came back sideways—Rachel asked Ilyra how she knew so much about injuries.

Ilyra shrugged. "My aunt ran a clinic near one of the river routes. People came through half-broken and lied about how bad it was."

"You learned healing there?" Rachel asked.

"Because no one else would," Ilyra said. "You patch first. You ask later. Sometimes never."

Maelin watched her carefully.

Ilyra went on, voice rougher now. "People don't tell the truth about pain. Bodies do."

Rachel found herself nodding, understanding settling deep. Back home, conversations like this—real ones, past the surface—had felt like work. Performance. She'd measured every word, calibrated every response, constantly aware of how she appeared.

Here, she'd just asked. And Ilyra had just answered. No performance required.

The ease of it should have comforted her.

Instead, it felt like proof of something she didn't want to acknowledge: that she'd been performing her entire life, and only now, in this impossible place with these strangers, had she stopped.

The book stayed on the table.

No one touched it.

Then Edrin lifted his head.

Maelin froze.

Ilyra went still.

"What?" Rachel asked.

Edrin crossed the room and opened the book.

The writing appeared slowly.

**Received.**

Then:

**Do not attempt replication again.**

Rachel leaned forward before she meant to.

More lines followed.

**Is she stabilized after the hesitation?**  
**Describe the environmental response in exact terms.**  
**Where are you holding her?**

"Holding?" Rachel said.

"He means location," Edrin said automatically.

The last line took longer.

**I am coming. Do nothing until I arrive.**

No one spoke.

Rachel stood. The bench scraped as she moved.

"I'm going outside," she said.

She didn't wait for permission.

As she moved toward the door, that persistent thought returned: Every hour here makes it harder to leave. The resonance was pulling her deeper into this world with each breath, each step, each moment of belonging. The timer was ticking, and she could feel it in her bones now—not as urgency, but as inevitability.



---

## Chapter Seven

Rachel was still outside when she heard the door.

Not the sound itself—this place had doors that sighed and settled and did their best to be quiet—but the way the air behind her changed, like someone had stepped into the space and carried a different temperature with them.

She didn’t turn right away.

She stood under the wrong sky and let her breathing even out, because her body kept trying to sprint ahead of her brain. Because her brain kept trying to sprint ahead of her life. Because the green-leaning light made her feel faintly sick if she stared at it too long.

Footsteps came closer over the dirt. Unhurried. Not cautious in the way Maelin had been cautious, like she was trying not to startle a wounded animal. These steps belonged to someone who assumed the ground would hold.

“Rachel?”

It wasn’t Maelin.

Rachel turned.

Four people stood at the edge of the clearing instead of three.

Edrin was there, shoulders tight, hands folded like he didn’t know what to do with them anymore. Maelin stood a half-step behind him, steady. Ilyra was to the side, posture loose, eyes sharp, watching everything that wasn’t the person in front of her.

And between them—slightly forward, like he’d wandered into the right spot by accident—was a man Rachel hadn’t seen before.

Older than the rest. Not old-old. Old enough that the lines at the corners of his eyes looked like they belonged there, like they’d been earned doing something he liked. His hair was dark with silver threaded through it, pulled back without care and still somehow tidy. His coat was worn the way a useful thing gets worn, not the way an expensive thing gets damaged.

He looked at Rachel like she was a puzzle he wanted to solve, and like the puzzle might bite him if he did it wrong.

“Hi,” he said, as if greeting her outside under a wrong-colored sky was a normal thing to do. “I’m Arathen.”

Rachel waited for someone else to say something. No one did.

She glanced at Edrin.

Edrin nodded once, the way people nod when they’re relieved and ashamed at the same time.

Rachel looked back at Arathen. “You came fast.”

Arathen made a small face. “Fast is relative. You should see how slowly the March likes to cooperate when you’re in a hurry.”

Ilyra’s mouth twitched.

Arathen noticed it and looked pleased with himself, like he’d just gotten a point in a game only he was playing. Then his eyes went back to Rachel, and the playfulness didn’t vanish so much as shift aside, making room for focus.

“Are you alright?” he asked.

Rachel’s hands curled. Uncurled. “I don’t know. I’m not hurt.”

“That’s a good start.” He walked a few steps closer, stopping far enough away that he wasn’t crowding her. He didn’t look at the sky until he was beside her, and when he did, he looked at it like he’d seen this shade before and didn’t like what it implied.

“Pretty,” he said lightly.

“It’s wrong,” Rachel said.

Arathen nodded. “Yes.”

He turned back toward the others. “Inside, please. Let’s not do heavy conversations in the open unless we have to.”

Ilyra moved first. Maelin followed. Edrin hesitated, eyes flicking to Rachel, then to Arathen.

Arathen caught the hesitation and sighed. “Edrin. You did the right thing calling me.”

Edrin's shoulders dropped a fraction. "We might have waited too long."

"Or too short," Arathen said. "Depends on what happens next."

They walked back inside.

Rachel felt it the moment she crossed the threshold—a wrongness in the air, a pressure building like static before lightning. The room's boundaries were shifting, destabilizing, responding to too many people with too much awareness all in one space.

"Something's—" she started.

The air rippled. Books on the shelf trembled. The water in the pitcher began moving in ways water shouldn't, climbing up the sides in defiance of gravity.

"Rachel," Arathen said sharply. "Can you feel it?"

"Yes," she gasped. The pressure was building behind her eyes, in her chest. The boundaries of the room were too thin, pressed too close together. She could feel them starting to tear.

Her hands moved without conscious thought, reaching toward the disturbance. The warmth flared in her palms—familiar now, almost comfortable. She found the tear, the place where boundaries had started to separate, and smoothed it. Like pressing wrinkles from fabric. The pressure eased.

The water in the pitcher settled. The books stopped trembling. The air cleared.

Rachel swayed, suddenly exhausted.

Arathen caught her elbow, steadying. His eyes were wide, calculating. "You just stabilized a boundary fluctuation. Instinctively. Without instruction."

"I didn't think about it," Rachel said. "I just felt it was wrong and—"

"Fixed it," Arathen finished. His expression had shifted, concern mixing with something that might have been awe. "Edrin's reports didn't mention this level of sensitivity. You can feel boundary stress before it manifests?"

"I don't know," Rachel said. "Maybe? Yes?"

The room felt different with Arathen in it—not rearranged, just claimed. And now he'd seen what she could do when she wasn't even trying.

Arathen set his bag near the table. He did not touch the book.

“Alright,” he said, rubbing his hands once. “Tell me what happened. In your words.”

Rachel sat. Maelin sat close. Ilyra leaned against the wall. Edrin hovered.

Rachel talked. About the summoning. The fear. The glass. The pause. The pressure.

"When you stopped the glass," Arathen said gently, "what were you thinking?"

"Don't spill," Rachel said. "Don't break."

"A prevention instinct," Arathen said. "Interesting."

He was watching her with that same intensity—not unkind, but thorough. Like he was cataloging details that didn't quite align with his expectations.

"You're measuring me," Rachel said.

"I am," Arathen said calmly. "Not you. What you're carrying." He paused, head tilting slightly. "Though the line between those two things might be thinner than we'd like."

Something in his tone made Rachel's pulse quicken. "What does that mean?"

"It means I'm not making assumptions yet," he said evenly. "But Rachel—what you did shouldn't have been possible for someone from a world without ambient magic. The fact that it was..." He let the sentence hang, unfinished, like an invitation to fill in the blank herself.

Rachel felt safer.

Rachel felt watched.

Arathen laid out rules. No testing alone. No new summoning. Familiar spaces only. Rest.

Rachel asked about home.

The word caught in her throat. Home. Which home? The apartment she'd rented? The city where she'd lived? The world she'd left?

Or this place, where the air recognized her and magic moved through her like breathing?

Arathen deferred, and Rachel found herself almost relieved.

Later, when Rachel asked what she was, he said, "You're Rachel."

Boxes, he said, were dangerous.

Rachel thought about the box she'd built around herself back there. Fine. Functional. Small enough to fit anywhere, unobtrusive enough that no one looked too close. 

She'd lived in that box for twenty-six years and called it a life.

When the conversation slowed, Rachel asked to go outside again.

Arathen allowed it. With Maelin.

Outside, the sky was still wrong.

Rachel stood under it, breathing, the light pressing softly against her eyes the way it had before. Maelin stayed close without touching, close enough that Rachel could feel the heat of another person without having to acknowledge it.

Behind them, the door clicked as it settled back into place.

Rachel tipped her head up. The thin, green-leaning light moved across the leaves overhead, the color refusing to correct itself no matter how long she stared.

Maelin's voice came quiet beside her. "Ready to go back in?"

Rachel shook her head. "Not yet."


---

## Chapter Eight

Rachel woke already tired.

Not the earned kind—the kind that came after long days or late nights—but something that settled in her bones like sediment. She'd slept, as far as she could tell. The room had stayed dark and quiet. Her body had gone still. But rest hadn't followed.

She lay there longer than necessary, staring at the ceiling and tracing the pattern where stone met wood. A hum lived under the floorboards, faint but persistent, like a pulse that didn't quite belong to her.

Except it did now. More than yesterday. More than the day before.

The building was awake, and she could feel it. Not like sensing movement in another room, but like feeling her own heartbeat. The distinction should have bothered her more than it did.

Every day she spent here, the resonance grew stronger. She could feel herself sinking roots into this place, or it sinking hooks into her—she wasn't sure which. Maybe both.

The timer was ticking. How many days had it been now? Five? Six? Each one made the connection deeper, the path back narrower. Soon—maybe already—it would be too late to return even if they found a way.

And the terrifying part was how little that thought bothered her.
