She didn't know how she knew that. The knowledge arrived without explanation, settling into certainty before her mind could question it.

When she finally stood, nothing changed. No pressure gathered in response. No awareness tracked her movement. That absence should have steadied her. Instead it felt like the space before a verdict, when the silence meant someone had already decided.

Rachel moved down the hall without choosing a destination and found herself at the outer door, hand resting against the latch. The air pressed differently here—thicker, weighted, as if the walls leaned inward when she came too close to leaving.

She opened the door.

Outside, the sky stretched pale and wrong, that faint green wash she couldn't correct no matter how long she stared. Light filtered through leaves too broad and too blue, their veins pressing close to the surface like something preserved under glass. A sound drifted from the tree line—almost birdsong, almost familiar, before it fractured into something that didn't map to memory.

Rachel stepped into the clearing.

The pressure eased at once, loosening around her shoulders and ribs like a grip she hadn't known was there. Air moved differently here. Freer. Less observed.

She walked without purpose or plan, letting her feet choose direction. Her breathing slowed. Her pulse steadied. The wrongness didn't correct itself, but it stopped asking her to notice.

She stayed longer than she meant to—long enough that counting time became irrelevant, long enough that her attention softened at the edges.

Then she felt it.

Not movement. Not sound. Just awareness, distinct and undeniable, that something behind her had noticed her absence.

Rachel stopped.

She turned back toward the building. The structure stood unchanged, stone and wood settled exactly where they'd been. But the air near the threshold looked different now—denser somehow, like heat gathering without warmth. She took a single step back toward it.

The sensation eased in response.

Her stomach tightened.

"You felt it too."

Rachel turned. Maelin stood several paces away, hands loose at her sides. Close enough to reach if needed. Far enough to let Rachel choose whether to close the distance.

Rachel swallowed. "The building. It knows I left."

Maelin's gaze tracked the space between Rachel and the door, measuring something Rachel couldn't name. "Knows," she repeated quietly. "Not notices?"

Rachel took another step toward the threshold. The pressure softened again, settling like recognition. "It's counting," she said. "Keeping track."

Maelin exhaled slowly, the sound carrying weight. "Then we don't test that alone."

"I know." Rachel meant it. She stepped back again, feeling the air reshape itself around her, familiar now in a way that made her skin prickle.

The door opened.

Arathen stood in the frame as if he'd been there all along, attention fixed somewhere past them both.

"Well," he said. His voice stayed level, clinical. "That clarifies something."

Rachel's hands curled. "You were watching."

"Yes."

She waited for an apology. None came.

"Of course," she said.

His gaze shifted to her, brief and assessing. "If it helps, I didn't cause the response."

"It doesn't," Rachel said.

He nodded once, as if that answer satisfied him more than reassurance would have.

They returned inside together. The air closed around Rachel as soon as she crossed the threshold—not painful, but deliberate, as if invisible hands had reached out to confirm her return. Arathen shut the door. The latch settled with a quiet click that felt too final.

Rachel stopped in the center of the room, hands flexing at her sides. The wrongness pressed closer here. Not threatening. Just present, like a question waiting to be asked.

"I need to do something," she said. Her voice came out steadier than she felt. "With my hands. Something normal."

Arathen watched her for a moment, gaze moving across her face in that way he had—measuring, but not unkind. Then he nodded once. "The kitchen is yours."

"Alone," Rachel added, then caught herself. "If that's—I just need to think while I work."

He considered that. A small crease appeared between his brows. "Stay clear of the outer wall. Nothing heavy. And if something feels off, say so immediately."

Rachel's jaw loosened slightly. The impulse to argue faded. He wasn't trying to control her. He was trying to keep her safe. The distinction mattered more than she'd expected.

"All right," she said. "Thank you."

His expression softened just slightly. "Of course."

Ilyra shifted in the doorway, watching but giving her space. Edrin glanced up from his book, concern crossing his face before he caught himself and looked away.

Rachel moved to the kitchen.

The counter sat lower than her apartment had been, the angle wrong enough to notice. She reached for a pan and felt the weight settle unexpectedly in her palm—heavier than it looked, the balance off-center. The knife she lifted wanted to roll in her grip. She adjusted without thinking, fingers finding the right pressure through habit alone.

Heat built slowly beneath the pan. Too slowly. She stood waiting, palm resting against the stone counter, feeling that persistent hum travel up through her arm.

"You move like someone who learned efficiency under pressure," Arathen observed from somewhere behind her. Not judging. Just noticing.

Rachel didn't turn. "I learned to cook when I was too tired to care what it tasted like. As long as it was food and it was fast."

A pause. "That's a particular kind of tired," Maelin said softly.

Rachel cracked an egg—or something like one—into a bowl and frowned at the color. Pale yellow leaning green. Wrong, but not alarmingly so. She stirred it anyway.

Edrin hovered at the edge of her peripheral vision, attention caught between his forgotten book and her hands. "That pan doesn't heat evenly," he blurted out, then winced. "The left side takes longer. I can find you a better one—"

"Edrin," Arathen said gently.

Edrin flushed. "Right. Sorry. I'll just—" He retreated to the table, looking miserable.

Rachel glanced over her shoulder at him. "It's okay. I'm used to uneven pans. My old stove was worse."

Edrin's expression eased slightly. He nodded, grateful.

Maelin sat with a cup cradled in both hands, steam rising between her fingers. She watched Rachel for a moment, then said quietly, "Your shoulders."

Rachel paused mid-stir. Realized they'd climbed toward her ears again. She forced them down, felt the muscles protest. "I do that when I'm concentrating. My boyfriend—" She stopped, the present tense suddenly wrong. "Evan used to point it out."

Maelin's mouth curved with sympathy. "He sounds observant."

"He is." Rachel stirred slower after that, letting the rhythm settle into something steady. "He'd hate this. All of it. The not knowing."

"You're worried about him," Maelin said.

Rachel opened her mouth to agree, then stopped. Was she? Constantly?

She tried to remember the last time she'd actively thought about Evan—not just mentioned him in conversation, but actually pictured him, worried about what he was going through. Hours ago? Yesterday?

The realization hit cold: she'd gone an entire day without thinking about him. An entire day of learning magic and reading impossible maps and eating meals with people who'd kidnapped her, and Evan had been—what? Background noise? An obligation she'd remember eventually?

"Yes," she said, but the word tasted like a lie. "Constantly."

The food came together without her full attention—automatic motions her hands remembered even when her mind wandered. She slid portions onto plates and passed one toward Maelin without looking.

Maelin accepted it, silent for a moment. "You do that without thinking."

Rachel glanced at her. "Do what?"

"Take care of people," Maelin said. "Make sure everyone has what they need before they know to ask for it."

Rachel's hands stilled. The observation sat heavier than it should have. "I worked in operations," she said. "You notice things. It's just habit."

Arathen's attention shifted, sharpened with understanding. "Operations," he repeated thoughtfully.

"Logistics. Scheduling." Rachel plated another portion and handed it to Edrin, who accepted it with a quiet thank you. "I kept systems running. Fixed problems before anyone noticed they existed."

"That explains a great deal," Arathen said, and something in his voice made it sound almost like respect.

Rachel turned to look at him. "Explains what?"

He accepted the plate she offered him. "Why you read the map so clearly. Why you sense boundaries. You've spent years learning to see where things might break before they do."

The words landed softer than she'd expected, recognition rather than judgment.

They ate together after that—not rushed, but not quite relaxed either. Rachel remained standing, one hip braced against the counter, but it felt less like guarding and more like she simply didn't know where else to be. Edrin ate with focus, clearly hungry. Ilyra leaned against the far wall, and after a few bites looked up and said, "This is good. Thank you."

The simple acknowledgment loosened something in Rachel's chest.

Maelin moved between them with water, refilling cups, and when she passed Rachel she touched her shoulder briefly. Just once. A small gesture that said you're one of us now.

Rachel found herself almost smiling.

"If I go outside again," Rachel said, breaking the silence, "what happens?"

Maelin and Ilyra both looked toward Arathen.

He set his mug down with care. "That depends."

"On?"

"On whether the boundary keeps responding." His tone stayed even, clinical. "And whether the response escalates."

Rachel's stomach tightened. She turned toward the window, drawn by instinct or avoidance—she couldn't tell which. Her palm found the sill, cool stone under warm skin. Beyond the glass, the sky held its wrongness without apology. Light moved across the clearing in patterns that didn't belong to any sun she recognized.

"You're very calm about all of this," she said.

"I've been afraid before," Arathen said from behind her. "Panic doesn't improve outcomes."

Rachel closed her eyes briefly. Opened them. The view hadn't changed. "That's not reassuring."

"No," he agreed. "It isn't."

Silence stretched, filled only by the building's persistent hum. Rachel stayed at the window, listening to it pulse beneath her palm, feeling the space around her contract and release like breathing.

The hum had bothered her at first. Now it felt like a heartbeat she'd been missing her entire life.

That thought should have scared her. Should have sent her running for the door, demanding they find a way to send her back.

Instead, she just stood there, palm pressed to stone, and accepted it.

"I'll cooperate," she said quietly. "With whatever you need me to do."

The words came easy. Too easy. Like she'd already decided to stay long before her mind had caught up with the choice.

"For now," Arathen said.

She glanced back at him. Found him watching her with that same measured attention, neither kind nor unkind. Just observing.

"For now," she echoed.

The building hummed beneath her palm. Rachel closed her eyes and let it.


---

## Chapter Nine

Arathen didn't call it a test.

That omission made Rachel trust the whole thing less.

He stood near the table with his hands loose at his sides, weight shifting occasionally, occupying space the way water fills a container—without force, but completely. His attention moved across the room in careful sweeps, cataloging details she couldn't guess at.

Rachel watched him over the rim of a mug gone lukewarm. The ceramic pressed against her palms, grounding her to something solid even as the building's hum threaded up through the floor and into her bones. She'd stopped trying to ignore it. The sound—or feeling, or whatever it was—had become background, constant as her own pulse.

Ilyra leaned against the wall beside the doorway, arms folded, posture deceptively casual. Her eyes tracked Arathen's movements with the focus of someone waiting for a signal. Edrin hovered near the shelves, a book open in his hands but unread, gaze flicking between Arathen and Rachel like he was following a conversation no one had started yet. Maelin sat close—not touching, but near enough that Rachel felt the heat of her presence, steady and deliberate.

Arathen's gaze settled on Rachel's face. Not her hands. Not the mug. Her.

"We're going to try something," he said. "Small."

Rachel waited. The room held its breath with her.

"How small," she asked.

"Controlled," he said. "Measured. Something we can stop if it moves the wrong direction."

No one laughed. No one relaxed.

Rachel set the mug down. The sound of ceramic meeting wood landed heavier than it should have, as if the table had been waiting to acknowledge the contact.

"What are we doing?" she asked.

"Observing," Arathen said. "You, primarily."

Rachel's jaw tightened. "That's not an answer."

"It's the most accurate one I have." He pulled a chair out and sat, the motion unhurried. "I need to see what happens when you try something on purpose instead of by reflex."

The distinction settled cold in her chest.

"You can stop at any point," Arathen said. "The moment it feels wrong, we stop. I mean that."

Rachel swallowed. "And if I can't stop it myself?"

Maelin shifted beside her, close enough that Rachel could feel her warmth. "Then we will," she said quietly. "You're not doing this alone."

Edrin set his book aside completely, giving Rachel his full attention. "We're right here."

The simple assurances steadied Rachel more than elaborate promises could have.

Arathen stood and moved the table—not far, just enough to create space. The scrape of wood against stone felt loud in the quiet. Chairs followed, pushed back to widen the working area. The room rearranged itself around his movements, expectant now but not hostile.

"We'll use something simple," he said. "Something that can't hurt anyone if this goes wrong."

Rachel's first thought was glass—the cup, the pause, the way time had bent around her intention. The memory arrived sharp and unwelcome.

"Not glass," she said.

"Good instinct," Arathen said, and the approval in his voice was genuine. He reached into his bag and produced a wooden bead, small and dark, smooth from handling. "This was my daughter's. It's survived worse than a fall."

The personal detail caught Rachel off guard. Made the object less clinical, more human.

"It rolls," he said, holding it up for her to see. "Nothing else matters."

Rachel stared at it. Such a small thing. But he'd chosen something he cared about. Something that said I trust you.

He set it on the table with care. The bead sat there, ordinary and patient.

"I'm going to roll this toward the edge," Arathen said. His voice stayed level, but kind. "Stop it before it falls. That's all."

Rachel's stomach tightened. Her hands felt too warm, too aware of themselves. She nodded.

Edrin leaned forward slightly, ready to move if needed. "You can do this," he said softly.

Ilyra caught Rachel's eye and gave a small nod. Not pressure. Just presence.

Arathen rolled the bead.

It moved faster than she'd anticipated. Her body responded before thought could intercede—instinct ahead of decision. The room contracted around her focus, everything peripheral falling away until only the bead remained, wood against wood, motion against stillness.

Stop.

The word didn't leave her mouth. Didn't need to.

The bead reached the table's edge and held.

Not balanced. Not teetering. Just suspended, half over empty air, as if gravity had been asked politely to wait.

Rachel's breath caught high in her chest. She hadn't moved. Hadn't touched anything.

The air around the bead looked different—thinner somehow, like the space itself had been scooped hollow.

"Good," Arathen said, his voice careful and pleased. "Very good."

Rachel's pulse hammered in her ears. "I did that. I actually did that."

"You did," Maelin said, and Rachel could hear the smile in her voice even as her eyes stayed wide with wonder.

Ilyra exhaled slowly, and when Rachel glanced at her, she saw something that might have been pride. "Clean work."

Edrin looked like he wanted to say something—mouth opening, closing, opening again—before he finally managed, "That was remarkable."

The praise felt strange. Foreign. But not unwelcome.

"Let it go now," Arathen said gently. "You can release it."

Rachel stared at the bead. "How do I—"

"However feels natural," he said. "There's no wrong way."

"What am I even doing?" Rachel asked, staring at the suspended bead. "Is this time? Space? What am I actually controlling?"

Arathen considered his answer carefully. "You're manipulating the boundary between motion and stillness. Not stopping time itself—more like... creating a pocket where normal rules pause. A hesitation in reality."

"That's what the room tilt was," Edrin added quietly. "You pushed against gravity itself because gravity is just another boundary—between up and down, falling and floating."

Rachel's stomach turned. "So I could accidentally..."

"Yes," Arathen said simply. "Which is why we're being careful."

Her instincts screamed to drop everything, to never touch this power again. But she trusted them now, at least a little, and that made the difference.

She exhaled and loosened something she couldn't name—a grip that existed nowhere in her body but everywhere in her intention.

The bead dropped. Hit the floor. Rolled under the table with a hollow sound.

Rachel's knees went soft. Not enough to buckle. Just enough to notice.

The exhaustion she felt wasn't normal tired. It was the kind that came after using muscles you'd forgotten you had—muscles that had been atrophied, waiting, her whole life.

Using her abilities felt like waking up. Like finally breathing deep after years of shallow breaths. Like flexing a limb that had been bound too tight and discovering it was stronger than she'd ever imagined.

The rightness of it terrified her more than the magic itself ever could.

Maelin's hand found her elbow, steadying. "I've got you."

"I'm okay," Rachel said, but she didn't pull away from the support. Didn't want to. Back home, she would have. Would have smiled and insisted she was fine and retreated to process alone.

Here, she let herself be held.

"You are," Maelin agreed, squeezing gently before letting go.

Arathen was watching her with an intensity that made Rachel's skin prickle. Not clinical observation—something deeper. Like he was seeing past what she was doing to what she was.

"What?" Rachel asked, unnerved by his focus.

He blinked, the intensity fading behind his usual measured calm. "Nothing. You're adapting faster than I expected, that's all." He nodded once. "Again."

Rachel's throat went dry. "Again?"

"Once more."

Edrin looked stricken. Opened his mouth. Closed it without protest.

Arathen retrieved the bead, movements unhurried, and placed it back on the table. "This time," he said, "don't try to replicate what you did. Just observe."

The instruction settled wrong in Rachel's chest.

He rolled the bead.

Rachel reached for the pause again, focused on the bead's motion—

And her power spilled over.

She felt it happen—the control slipping sideways, affecting not just the bead but everything within her focus. Including Arathen.

He froze mid-breath.

Not completely. Not like the bead. Just slowed—seventy percent, maybe less—but enough that Rachel saw his eyes widen in delayed surprise, saw his hand moving toward her with dream-like sluggishness.

"No!" Rachel gasped, releasing everything immediately.

Arathen stumbled forward, momentum restored too quickly. Maelin caught him before he could fall, and he steadied himself with visible effort.

"I'm sorry," Rachel said, the words tumbling out. "I didn't mean—I was trying to just pause the bead but it—" Her hands were shaking. "Are you okay? Did I hurt you?"

Arathen held up a hand, breathing carefully. "I'm fine. Just... disoriented." He sat down heavily. "That was... unexpected."

"You paused him," Edrin said, voice thin with shock. "You caught a person in temporal field. That shouldn't—demons can't do that. We're supposed to be immune to—"

"I'm not a demon," Rachel interrupted. "Maybe that's why—" She stopped. Looked at Arathen. "I could have hurt you. What if I'd frozen you completely? What if I couldn't restart you?"

"But you didn't," Arathen said, though his voice wasn't entirely steady. "You caught yourself. Released immediately. That's good instinct, Rachel."

"It's dangerous instinct," Rachel countered. "I keep telling you—everything I do is dangerous. To all of you. What if next time I can't stop it?"

"Then we'll be more careful," Maelin said firmly. "Set better boundaries. Literal ones." She looked at Arathen. "Should we establish safety protocols? Keep physical distance during tests?"

Arathen considered, then nodded. "Yes. And Rachel—" He met her eyes. "That wasn't failure. That was information. Now we know your power can affect people, not just objects. Now we know to be careful about proximity and focus."

"Now we know I'm even more dangerous than we thought," Rachel said quietly.

The room went silent. No one contradicted her.

Because they couldn't.

The instruction settled wrong in Rachel's chest, but she nodded anyway.

He rolled the bead.

Rachel reached for it instinctively—and the difference struck her at once.

The pressure gathered faster this time, heavier, as if the room had grown impatient with practice. She felt resistance where before there had been only compliance, like pressing against something that pressed back with equal force.

Stop—

The bead jerked mid-roll, then froze.

Too low.

It hung below the table's edge, suspended at an angle that made her eyes ache to follow.

Rachel's stomach turned over.

The air around the bead looked wrong—thin and cold, like something had carved out a hollow and forgotten to fill it back in. The space itself seemed wounded.

Ilyra straightened sharply. "Arathen."

"I see it."

Rachel's breath came shallow. "What did I—"

"You didn't do anything wrong," Maelin said, but the uncertainty in her voice undermined the reassurance.

Edrin stared at the empty space surrounding the bead, his face pale. "The displacement is visible."

"Rachel," Arathen said. His voice cut through without rising. "Stop."

She looked at him, confused. "It's already—"

"I'm not talking to the bead."

The words landed like cold water.

Rachel understood then. She was still holding on. Still gripping that nameless thing inside her chest, clenching it tight as if releasing it would tear something fundamental loose. The pressure built with nowhere to go, trapped and growing.

Arathen moved closer—not crowding, just positioning himself between her and the table, breaking her line of sight.

"Look at me," he said.

Rachel dragged her gaze away from the bead. Met his eyes.

Steady. Patient. Waiting.

"Breathe," he said.

She did. In through her nose. Out through her mouth. The way she'd learned in some forgotten wellness seminar, the kind of breathing that was supposed to fix things.

The pressure eased. Barely. Just enough.

The cold hollow around the bead softened at the edges.

"Good," Arathen said quietly. "Now let go."

Rachel released.

The bead fell. Hit the floor with a sound that felt muffled, wrong. It rolled slowly, as if moving through something thicker than air, before coming to rest against the table leg.

Rachel's pulse pounded in her temples.

Arathen watched the bead until it stopped completely. When he looked back at Rachel, concern had replaced clinical interest. "How do you feel?"

"Shaky," Rachel admitted. "That was harder."

"I know." He moved closer, studying her face. "Any pain? Dizziness?"

"No. Just tired."

Edrin looked stricken. "We should stop. That's enough for today."

"More than enough," Maelin agreed, already moving to Rachel's side. "You need to rest."

Ilyra's arms folded across her chest, but her voice came out gentler than usual. "That was brave. But Edrin's right. We're done."

"How much danger is she in?" Edrin asked Arathen directly, the words tumbling out. "If this keeps escalating—"

Arathen raised a hand, calming. "We proceed carefully from here. Smaller steps. More time between attempts." He looked at Rachel. "Nothing you're not ready for."

Rachel found herself grateful for their concern, even as part of her wanted to prove she could keep going. "What if I need to do it again? In an emergency?"

"Then you will," Arathen said. "And we'll be here to help." He retrieved the bead from the floor, rolling it gently between his fingers. "For now, we learn from what just happened instead of pushing forward."

Rachel's attention drifted back to the space where the bead had hung suspended. The air there looked normal now, but the memory of its wrongness lingered.

Maelin's hand rested briefly on her shoulder. "Come sit down. I'll make tea."

Rachel nodded, suddenly aware of how much she wanted exactly that—to sit, to have someone care for her in small ways, to not be alone with what she'd just discovered she could do.


---

## Chapter Ten

They didn't leave right away.

Rachel had expected urgency—a plan formed quickly, bags packed, the door closing behind them before the morning finished settling. That was how things worked when people realized they'd miscalculated. But the hours stretched instead, time moving without purpose or direction. The house breathed around them, patient and watchful, as if it had decided they weren't finished yet.

Rachel sat on the floor near the table, back pressed against one of the legs, knees drawn up. She hadn't chosen the spot consciously. It had simply become where she was, listening to the scrape of chair legs as Edrin rearranged furniture for reasons he didn't articulate. Small movements. Adjustments that might have meant something or nothing.

Ilyra stood in the doorway, arms folded, gaze distant. Counting something internal—breaths or heartbeats or decisions. Maelin moved through the space washing things that were already clean, hands occupied with purpose where her mind couldn't settle. Arathen remained at the window, attention fixed on the clearing beyond as if waiting for the landscape to offer answers.

No one spoke.

The silence pressed in from all sides, thick with unspoken decisions.

Rachel realized she was waiting for someone to tell her what they'd concluded. What verdict they'd reached about her. About what came next.

The realization bothered her more than the quiet did.

"Is this the part," she said finally, voice steadier than she felt, "where someone explains what happens now?"

Ilyra glanced toward Arathen. He didn't turn from the window.

"We're still deciding," Maelin said. Her tone stayed gentle, careful.

Rachel's jaw tightened. "You're waiting for something."

Edrin stopped moving chairs. "We are," he said, then flinched as if he'd spoken out of turn.

"For what?" Rachel asked.

He hesitated, fingers curling against the chair back. "For you to… stabilize."

The word sat wrong in her mouth. Rachel wanted to laugh—felt the impulse rise sharp and bitter—but swallowed it instead. "I don't think that's happening."

Ilyra's expression stayed flat. "He means the magic. Not you."

"That doesn't help," Rachel said.

Arathen finally turned from the window. His gaze settled on Rachel with that same careful attention he'd given the bead—measuring not her worth, but what existed in this moment. When he spoke, his voice carried a weight she hadn't heard before.

"You're right," he said. "This isn't sustainable."

Rachel's chest tightened. "You mean me."

"I mean all of this," he said. "The waiting, the uncertainty. What we're asking you to carry." He moved away from the window, closer to the group. "You deserve better than being held in suspension."

The acknowledgment caught Rachel off guard.

Maelin moved closer and crouched nearby, arms resting on her knees. Her eyes found Rachel's. "We're not keeping you here. Not like this. We know you have a life you were pulled from. People who need to know you're alive."

"But you can't send me back," Rachel said.

"Not yet," Maelin admitted, and the regret in her voice sounded genuine. "But we're not giving up on finding a way."

Something in Rachel's chest loosened slightly at the honesty.

Rachel found a loose thread on her borrowed sleeve. Pulled it once. Stopped herself before she unraveled more. "I keep feeling like I walked into the middle of a conversation," she said. "Like you all know the context and I'm just guessing."

"That's not inaccurate," Ilyra said.

Rachel looked up at her. "Then tell me what I'm missing."

Edrin shifted uncomfortably, his book forgotten on the table behind him. "We were attempting something. A summoning."

"I know that part," Rachel said.

"You don't know why," he said.

She pushed herself upright, folding her legs beneath her, facing them properly now. "Then maybe you should tell me."

The room held its breath. Even the building's hum seemed to quiet, as if waiting.

Arathen spoke first. "We don't typically involve unwilling participants."

Rachel's jaw tightened. "And yet."

He acknowledged that with a slight nod. "And yet."

"We needed help," Maelin said. Her hands folded together, fingers interlacing and releasing in small, repetitive movements. "With something we didn't know how to handle alone."

Rachel frowned. "Help with what?"

Ilyra's expression stayed flat. "That's where it gets complicated."

"Try me," Rachel said.

Edrin rubbed at his temple, fingers pressing hard enough to leave marks. "The March has been expanding. Slowly, but measurably. Areas that were stable five years ago are fragmenting now. Trade routes collapse. Settlements evacuate. People disappear into boundary fractures."

"We've been trying to understand why," Maelin added. "To find a way to stabilize it. But the March doesn't follow normal rules. It's not just unstable—it's actively destabilizing everything around it."

"Like a wound that won't heal," Ilyra said flatly. "Gets worse if you don't treat it."

Rachel watched them carefully. "So you tried to summon something to fix it."

"Someone," Maelin corrected quietly. "Not something."

The distinction landed cold in Rachel's chest. "You were summoning a demon."

The word fell heavy into the silence.

No one denied it.

Edrin's voice came quiet. "A specific one. Keleth. She's a boundary walker—someone who exists between worlds by nature. We thought if anyone could understand what's happening to the March, could tell us how to stop it..."

"It would be someone who lives in those in-between spaces," Arathen finished.

Ilyra's arms remained folded. "Demons understand boundaries better than most. Keleth especially. She's helped stabilize fractures before, according to the records."

"But you got me instead," Rachel said.

"Yes," Edrin admitted. "And we still don't know why."

Rachel stared at the floor, at the grain of the wood, at anything but the people watching her process this. Something shifted inside her—not quite fear, not quite anger. Something older and less defined.

"Who were you trying to reach?" she asked quietly.

Edrin opened his mouth, clearly wanting to answer.

Arathen raised one hand—barely a movement, gentle. "We can tell you," he said. "But perhaps not all at once. You've learned a great deal in a very short time."

Rachel's gaze lifted to him. "I can handle it."

"I know you can," Arathen said, and he sounded like he meant it. "But handling something and needing to handle it are different things. We owe you the truth. We also owe you the space to receive it at a pace you choose."

The distinction made Rachel pause. He wasn't refusing. He was offering her control.

"Soon though," she said. "I need to know."

"Soon," he agreed. "You have my word."

Edrin looked between them, then nodded. "Whenever you're ready, Rachel. We'll tell you everything."

Rachel pressed her palms flat against the floor, seeking stability in the solid, unchanging pressure of wood and stone.

"Did you know the summoning might fail?" she asked.

"Yes," Edrin said without hesitation.

"Did you know it might bring someone else? Someone who wasn't what you wanted?"

Silence answered her.

The absence of denial settled heavier than words could have.

Rachel nodded slowly. "All right."

The acknowledgment didn't carry forgiveness. Didn't carry understanding. It simply marked the limit of what she could process before something inside her fractured.

"But you still haven't told me," she said, voice dropping lower, "why this is happening to me specifically."

No one spoke.

Rachel's chest constricted. "I didn't have this before. I didn't feel—" She stopped, fingers curling against the floorboards. "I wasn't like this."

Maelin started to move closer. "Rachel—"

"No," Rachel said. The word came out harder than she'd intended. "I need to understand this part. I need to know why."

Her gaze fixed on Arathen. "You keep looking at me like you've already figured it out."

Arathen's eyes closed briefly. When they opened again, his expression had lost its measured calm, revealing something rawer beneath.

"I don't know everything," he said. "I wish I did."

Rachel's throat closed. "Then tell me what you do know. Please."

He stepped away from the window and, after a moment's hesitation, sat down across from her. Eye level. Equal.

"Magic doesn't manifest uniformly across worlds," he said. Each word came carefully, but not cold. Like he was handling something precious and breakable. "Some places hold it loosely. Others compress it until it becomes dense, resistant. This world pushes back against those who don't belong."

Rachel swallowed. "And I belong?"

"Most people who cross from your world to this one experience it as obstruction," he continued gently. "Exhaustion. A constant sense of being pushed away by something they can't see."

"But not me," Rachel said.

Arathen held her gaze, and his expression was kind. "You experience it as recognition. Like coming home to a place you've never been."

The room shifted around her—or perhaps she shifted and the room stayed still. Rachel couldn't tell which.

She wanted to laugh. Felt the impulse rise bitter and sharp in her throat. "That's not possible."

"It's rare," he said. "But you're not the first person this has happened to." The assurance seemed meant to comfort. "You're not alone in this."

"You're saying this world gave me something."

"I'm saying," Arathen corrected quietly, "this world allowed something that was always part of you. Something that had no room to exist where you were."

Rachel's hands formed fists against her thighs. "What does that mean?"

He hesitated, choosing his words carefully. His eyes tracked across her face like he was reading something written there in a language only he could see. "You told us you were adopted. That you'd always felt like something was missing, like you didn't quite fit anywhere."

Rachel's breath caught. "How is that relevant?"

Arathen's expression softened. "Because people who exist between worlds often come from... fractured origins. Not broken. Fractured. Split between what was and what could be. Your adoption left a gap in your history—a place where your story should have been solid but wasn't. That kind of incompleteness..." He paused, and for a moment something flickered across his expression—recognition, maybe, or suspicion carefully contained. "It can make someone permeable to other possibilities."

"You're choosing your words very carefully," Rachel said, watching him back.

"I'm trying not to make assumptions," Arathen said. "Not yet. But Rachel—the way magic moves through you, the way this world recognizes you... it's not typical. Even for someone who crosses boundaries accidentally."

"What are you not saying?" Rachel asked.

Arathen's mouth pulled into something that wasn't quite a smile. "That I have theories. And that I'd rather wait until we have more information before I share them."

Maelin spoke softly. "It means you weren't wrong there. The world was. You were always meant for somewhere with more space."

The gentleness in her voice nearly undid Rachel.

"So I was broken there," Rachel said, voice wavering. "And I'm fixed here."

"No," Edrin said urgently, leaning forward. "You were never broken. You were—" He struggled for the word. "Compressed. Like trying to exist in a space too small to hold you. That missing piece you felt? It wasn't something you lost. It was something you never had room to grow."

Rachel's throat closed. The missing piece. The gap she'd carried her whole life, the one she'd tried to fill with being fine, being useful, being small enough that no one would notice the hollow space inside her.

"Did you know that when you summoned me?" she asked.

"No," Edrin said, and there was real pain in his voice. "I swear, Rachel, we didn't know. But it explains why the working reached you instead of Keleth. You were—you are—someone who exists between worlds. Just like she does. The summoning found someone with the same... resonance."

Ilyra's voice came quieter than usual. "We didn't mean to pull you here. But maybe you were always meant to find your way to somewhere like this."

Rachel stood. The motion felt too abrupt, too uncontrolled. She took two steps and stopped, hands shaking.

"You're telling me," she said, "that I came here and suddenly I could do things I was never supposed to be able to do. That some part of me was just... waiting."

Maelin stood as well, moving to Rachel's side. "Rachel. It's not about what you were supposed to be. It's about what you are."

"I wasn't this person yesterday," Rachel said. Her voice cracked despite her effort to keep it level. "Yesterday I was—"

What? Fine? Functional? A person who fit, even if the fit was uncomfortable? Who had a boyfriend and a job and a life that made sense on paper even if it felt hollow?

Yesterday she'd been someone who belonged nowhere and had made peace with it.

Today she was someone who belonged here, in this impossible place with these impossible people, and that should have been wonderful except—

Except what did it say about her that she fit better with strangers in a foreign world than she'd ever fit with people who'd known her for years?

What did it say about her that some small, guilty part of her didn't want to go back?

"I don't know how to be this person," she said finally.

Arathen stood too, but kept his distance. "You don't have to know yet."

Rachel pressed both palms against her eyes, breathing too fast, too shallow. "I hate not understanding." What she really hated was understanding too well.

"I know," Maelin said, and then she was there, hand resting between Rachel's shoulder blades. Not demanding. Just present. "I know this is frightening."

Rachel's breath hitched. She hadn't cried yet—not through any of this—but she felt dangerously close now.

Edrin's voice came soft and earnest. "We'll help you figure it out. You don't have to do this alone."

Arathen's tone shifted, more formal. "I want you to meet someone. Someone who understands what it's like to exist between worlds. Someone who's built a life doing exactly that."

Rachel lowered her hands slowly, and Maelin squeezed her shoulder once before stepping back.

"Someone like me," Rachel said.

Arathen's expression grew thoughtful, measuring. "Someone who shares your... particular relationship with boundaries. Her name is Sivara. She lives in Thornhaven, a trading town about a week's travel from here. Maybe more, depending on conditions and what we encounter along the way."

"And she can help me control this?" Rachel asked.

"She can show you how she manages her own abilities," Arathen said. "How she's lived among humans for years without incident."

Rachel frowned. "Why wouldn't she live among humans?"

A beat of silence.

Edrin cleared his throat. "Because she's a demon." The words came out like a confession.

Rachel's stomach dropped. The word hit different than she expected—not scary movie, not hellfire and brimstone. Just... other. Definitively other.

"You're taking me to meet a demon," she said flatly.

"A boundary walker," Maelin corrected gently. "Like Keleth, the one we were trying to summon. Sivara exists in the spaces between worlds naturally. She understands the magic you're developing because it's similar to her own."

"Similar," Rachel repeated. Her mind was racing. "You think I'm like a demon."

Arathen measured his words. "We think you're manifesting abilities that shouldn't be possible for someone from your world. Sivara can help us understand why. And more importantly, she can teach you to live with them safely."

Rachel wanted to laugh. Wanted to scream. Instead she just stood there, processing the fact that she was being taken to meet an actual demon because her rescuers thought she had something in common with one.

"Does she know we're coming?" Rachel asked, grasping for practical questions.

"No," Arathen admitted. "We'll need to approach carefully. Sivara values her privacy and her integration into human society. She won't appreciate strangers showing up unannounced."

"But you think she'll help me anyway."

"I think she'll be curious enough to hear us out," Arathen said. "And once she sees what you can do, once she understands..." He paused. "She'll have questions. Just like I do."

The way he said it made Rachel's skin prickle. Like he was watching her even now, cataloging details, forming theories he wasn't ready to share.

"Okay," she said finally. "Okay. When do we leave?"

"Tomorrow," Arathen said. "If you're ready."

"We should go sooner," Rachel said, surprising herself. "Every day I'm here, the resonance gets stronger. You said it yourself—there's a timer on this. If Sivara can help me understand what's happening, help me figure out if I even can go back—" Her voice caught. "Then I need to meet her. Soon."

Arathen studied her for a moment, then nodded. "You're right. Tomorrow then. No delays."

Rachel closed her eyes briefly. Opened them. The world remained unchanged, but she felt steadier somehow. More in control.

"I'll never be ready," she said. "But let's go anyway."

Maelin smiled at that. "That's usually how it works."


---

## Chapter Eleven

Packing was not a simple matter.

Rachel had expected efficiency—decisions made quickly, belongings sorted into clear categories of necessary and dispensable. Instead, the morning unfolded in careful negotiations and silent reconsiderations, each person moving through their own internal calculus of what could be carried and what had to be left behind.

Edrin had taken over the table immediately. Books spread before him in stacks that grew and shrank as he reconsidered their necessity. His hands moved between volumes with a kind of restless precision, fingers tracing spines as if touch could determine essential from merely useful.

"You can't carry all of those," Ilyra said. Her tone stayed level, factual.

Edrin's jaw tightened. "These are reference texts."

"Choose three," she said.

"That's not sufficient—"

"Then choose five," Ilyra said. "But you're carrying them yourself, and we're walking."

Edrin stared at the books as if the weight of the decision matched the weight of the volumes. Finally, reluctantly, he moved one thick tome from the keep pile to the discard pile. The motion looked like it cost him something.

Rachel watched from the floor near the far wall, legs folded beneath her, hands empty. She'd been sitting there long enough that the position had grown uncomfortable, but standing felt like intruding on their preparations.

She had nothing to pack.

The realization had arrived gradually—the others sorting through belongings, making choices, while she sat with nothing to sort, nothing to choose. Everything that had defined her previous existence had vanished with the car. Phone. Wallet. Keys. Photos of Evan. The small, accumulated proof that she'd been someone, somewhere.

She tried to mourn those things. Tried to feel the loss as heavy as it should have been.

Instead, what she felt was something closer to relief.

No phone meant no carefully curated social media presence. No photos of brunch she hadn't enjoyed. No text threads where she'd performed enthusiasm for plans she'd dreaded. No calendar notifications for obligations she'd accepted because refusing felt harder than attending.

The life she'd lost had been so small. So carefully controlled. So exhaustingly maintained.

And she hadn't even realized it until now, when all those carefully maintained pieces were gone and she could finally breathe.

She looked down at her borrowed clothes. Even these weren't hers, and somehow that mattered less than it should have.

"We're terrible hosts," Maelin said suddenly, and Rachel looked up to find her approaching with a bundle of fabric. "You've been here days and we never thought—" She shook her head. "These should fit. They're practical for travel, and they're yours to keep."

The emphasis on that last word made Rachel's throat tight.

"Thank you," she managed.

Maelin knelt down beside her rather than handing the clothes from standing. "I wish we had more to offer. Some of your own things."

"It's not your fault I don't have anything," Rachel said.

"It's our fault you're here at all," Maelin replied gently.

Ilyra appeared with a worn pack and set it beside Rachel. "This was mine when I first started traveling. It's good. Sturdy. It'll carry what you need."

Rachel stared at it. "You're giving me your pack?"

"I have another," Ilyra said. "Better one, actually. This one deserves someone who'll appreciate it."

The casual generosity undid something in Rachel's chest. "I don't know what to say."

"You don't have to say anything," Ilyra replied.

Rachel stared at it for a moment before tucking the clothes Maelin had given her inside. The simple act of having something to put away, somewhere to put it, steadied her more than she'd expected. She folded each piece with more care than necessary, attention fixed on the ordinary task.

"Shoes," Ilyra said, looking at Rachel's feet. "Those won't last a day on the road."

Rachel looked down at her work flats, still somehow intact despite everything that had happened. They belonged to a different life. A different person. "I know."

"We'll find something in the first town," Ilyra said. "It can't wait longer than that."

Rachel nodded.

Arathen moved to the table and began unwrapping something from protective cloth. The fabric fell away to reveal a map—not paper, but something that looked aged and preserved, surface marked with lines that seemed to shift as the light changed angle.

"This guides us," he said.

Edrin straightened, attention immediately focused. "You're bringing that."

"Yes."

"That's—" He stopped himself, then continued more carefully. "That's not commonly traveled with."

"No," Arathen agreed. "It isn't."

The map spread across the table as Arathen weighted the corners. Lines emerged slowly, as if the surface needed time to remember what it was meant to show. Rachel found herself drawn closer, pulled by something she couldn't name.

The symbols resolved into clarity as she looked at them—not gradually, but all at once, as if they'd been waiting for her attention specifically.

She frowned, leaning closer.

"That route is broken," she said, pointing to a line that curved through the eastern section. "You'll want to avoid it."

Edrin moved beside her immediately, following her gesture. "It's marked as continuous on the map."

"I know what it shows," Rachel said. "But it's wrong. The route is interrupted. Like something pulled it apart or—" She tilted her head, trying to understand what she was seeing. "Like it's trying to exist in two places at once and failing at both."

Ilyra leaned in to look where Rachel pointed. "That passes near the Near March. If it's destabilizing..." She trailed off, concern evident.

Rachel glanced up at her. "I keep hearing about this March. What is it?"

Maelin's hands folded together at her waist. "It's a place where the boundaries between worlds grow thin. Things don't settle properly there. Land shifts. Roads appear one day and vanish the next."

"So, actively dangerous," Rachel said.

"Unpredictable," Maelin said. "Which can be worse."

Rachel looked back at the map, finding other routes, tracing them with her finger. "This way looks more stable. Longer, but solid."

Edrin had been studying both the map and Rachel's face. "You're reading the conditional layers," he said, wonder creeping into his voice. "Routes that exist only sometimes, depending on factors most people can't even see."

Rachel looked at him. "There are layers?"

"The map shows multiple routes that overlap and change based on conditions," he explained. "Current weather, magical density, time of day, proximity to boundaries. It usually takes trained cartographers hours to parse them all out."

She looked back at the map. "They just look... different to me. Like different colors, almost, though that's not quite right."

Arathen was smiling slightly. Not his usual measured expression—something warmer, surprised. "That's a remarkable gift."

"It's useful," Ilyra agreed, sounding impressed. "Might keep us alive."

Rachel felt her face warm at the praise. "I'm just reading what's there."

"No," Edrin said earnestly. "You're reading what's there in a way most people can't. That's valuable, Rachel. Really valuable."

Arathen's eyes lingered on her for a moment, something thoughtful crossing his expression. Then he looked away, back to the map. "More valuable than you know."

The acknowledgment settled strange but not unwelcome in her chest.

Arathen rolled the map carefully, securing it. "Rachel, we should discuss your abilities before we leave."

Rachel's stomach tightened. She'd been so focused on the practical aspects of travel—packing, routes, shoes—that she'd almost let herself forget about the power that could tilt rooms sideways.

Almost.

"What about them?" she asked, trying to keep her voice steady.

"On the road, we won't have the same controlled environment," he said. "Different spaces, different pressures. You'll need to be aware of what triggers the sensations."

Rachel's hand drifted to her palm, remembering the warmth. "The heat. The tightness in my chest."

"Exactly." Arathen set the map aside. "If you feel either building, you tell me immediately. We stop, you step away from the group, and you let it dissipate."

"What if we can't stop?" Rachel asked. "What if we're on a narrow road or—"

"Then I'll help you ground it," he said simply. "But the goal is recognition before escalation. You've done it once successfully with the bead. You can do it again."

"That was in a room," Rachel said. "With you right there. What if—"

"Rachel." Maelin's voice was gentle but firm. "We're not going to let you face this alone. That's the entire point of traveling together."

Rachel nodded, throat tight. "What if I hurt someone while I'm sleeping? The tent—what if I—"

"The tent has its own stability," Arathen said. "It's not part of normal space in the way the building is. If anything, it might insulate against your abilities rather than amplify them."

"Might," Rachel repeated.

"Might," Arathen agreed. "But we'll be careful. And Rachel—you've already shown more control than you give yourself credit for. The bead test wasn't luck."

Rachel wanted to believe that. Needed to believe that.

"Okay," she said. "I'll tell you if I feel anything. The heat, the pressure—any of it."

"Good," Arathen said. "We'll practice during rest stops. Small exercises. Nothing dramatic."

They returned to their preparations after that—quieter, more deliberate, but with an additional weight Rachel could feel pressing on her shoulders. Edrin made his final selections, each book placed in his pack with visible reluctance for those left behind. Ilyra checked straps and weight distribution with practiced efficiency. Maelin wrapped something in cloth and tucked it carefully into the bottom of her pack where it wouldn't shift.

Rachel watched them work, then looked down at her own pack. One borrowed set of clothes. No personal objects. No accumulated history. Everything that had marked her as herself—photos, documents, small objects carried more for sentiment than function—all of it gone.

She should have felt lighter.

Instead, the absence weighed more than possessions ever had.

The afternoon sun angled lower through the windows, casting long shadows across the floor. They weren't ready yet—wouldn't be ready for hours still—but the moment had shifted. The building's hum had changed, anticipation threading through it.

Rachel closed her pack and set it beside the others.

Maelin paused in her own packing and looked over. "You all right?"

"No," Rachel said. "But I will be."

Maelin nodded, accepting that.


---

## Chapter Twelve

They left later than planned.

Rachel had anticipated a clean departure—a moment when they'd close the door behind them and step deliberately onto the road. Instead, it happened gradually, in fragments. Someone returned for forgotten supplies. Someone else debated the sun's position and what that meant for their timing. By the time they actually left, the departure felt less like a decision and more like an inevitable drift.

The road stretched narrow before them—packed earth worn smooth by years of passage, edges blurred where grass encroached. It looked more like a suggestion than a definitive path, as if the land hadn't fully committed to the idea of roads.

Rachel adjusted her pack for the third time in as many minutes. The straps sat wrong no matter how she positioned them, weight pulling at her shoulders in ways her body didn't know how to accommodate.

"Stop adjusting," Ilyra said without looking back, but her tone was gentle. "You're making it worse."

Rachel's hands stilled. "It doesn't fit right."

"It won't," Ilyra said. "Not for days. Your body needs to learn the weight."

Rachel left the straps alone, though every instinct screamed to keep trying to fix it. The weight settled into an ache that started immediate and promised to grow worse.

Maelin fell back until she walked beside Rachel. "The first day is the hardest," she said sympathetically. "Your body doesn't know what you're asking it to do yet."

"When does it get easier?" Rachel asked, trying not to sound as pathetic as she felt.

"Eventually," Maelin said, which was kinder than honesty required. "You get stronger. Your shoulders stop screaming. You learn how to walk differently."

"How long is eventually?"

Maelin's smile held sympathy. "Sooner than you think when you're in it. You're tougher than you know."

Rachel wanted to believe that.

They walked in loose formation, each person settling into a natural position. Ilyra moved ahead, attention scanning the road with the learned focus of someone who knew which details mattered and which could be ignored. Edrin followed several paces back, consulting the map periodically before looking up to confirm what he'd read against what he saw. Arathen drifted—sometimes beside Rachel, sometimes behind, his position shifting without apparent pattern.

Rachel's feet found rhythm in the uneven ground. Her shoulders ached under the pack's weight. Sweat gathered at the small of her back.

It was the most physically present she'd felt in years.

Back home, she'd driven the same route to work every day, mind already at her desk before her body arrived. She'd moved through a world of climate control and ergonomic chairs, of careful distance from discomfort. Her body had been transportation for her mind, nothing more.

Here, every step registered. Every breath counted. She was aware of her body in a way that should have been exhausting but instead felt like finally inhabiting herself.

The Near March didn't announce its proximity.

Rachel had expected something visible—a boundary marker, a shift in vegetation, some clear indication that they'd entered a different region. Instead, awareness crept in gradually. The air grew denser, not oppressive but present in a way it hadn't been before. Sound behaved differently here. A birdcall began and faded too quickly, cut short as if the air itself had absorbed it. Their footsteps seemed to vanish before the sound could travel.

Rachel's frown deepened. "Something's different here."

Ilyra glanced back over her shoulder. "Different how?"

"The air," Rachel said, pressing a hand to her chest. "It's heavier. Like it's—watching? No, that's not right. It's waiting for something."

Edrin's attention snapped to her immediately, and he looked almost excited. "You're sensing the boundary? Already?"

"I think so," Rachel said uncertainly.

Arathen adjusted his pace until he walked between them, his expression thoughtful. "That's the March's edge. Most people don't notice it until they're standing right at the threshold."

Rachel's unease deepened. "Should I be able to feel it from this far away?"

"It means you're sensitive to the boundaries," Arathen said. "Which is useful, even if it's uncomfortable."

"We should trust her instincts," Maelin said from behind them. "If it feels wrong to her, it probably is wrong."

They didn't move any nearer to it. Edrin consulted the map more frequently, muttering about boundary shifts and stability gradients. Then Rachel stopped walking and pointed to their left.

"That direction," she said. "The pressure is lower there. Safer."

Edrin looked up from the map, frowning. "The marked route continues right."

"I know what the map says," Rachel said. "But the road to the right—it feels wrong. Unstable. Like walking on ice that's starting to thin."

Ilyra studied the terrain in both directions. "The left looks rougher. More overgrown."

"It is," Rachel said. "But it's solid. The other way..." She shook her head. "Something's wrong with it."

"That's good enough for me," Ilyra said, and turned left without hesitation.

The others followed, and the pressure eased almost immediately, like stepping out of a room where the air had grown too close.

Edrin stared at the map, then looked up, then back down. "It changed," he said quietly, something like awe in his voice. "The route just updated to match what Rachel sensed."

"The map knows when someone who can actually read it is present," Arathen said, a hint of a smile in his voice. "It's showing her the truth instead of the approximation."

Rachel focused on walking after that, letting the praise settle warm in her chest. They trusted her. That mattered more than she'd expected.

After an hour, her legs burned.

After two, her shoulders ached.

She didn’t complain. Mostly because everyone else looked like this was normal.

They stopped when Maelin said they should, not because anyone argued. Packs came off. Water was shared. Rachel drank more than she meant to and immediately regretted it.

“So,” she said, wiping her mouth. “How far did we go?”

Edrin checked the map. “Less than we planned.”

“Of course.”

“But farther than we would have if we’d taken the other bend,” he added.

Rachel nodded, strangely pleased.

They ate something small and unremarkable. Rachel couldn't tell if it was meant to taste that bland or if her mouth was just tired.

Arathen stood and brushed crumbs from his hands. "Rachel, while we have a moment."

She looked up at him, already knowing what he meant. Her stomach tightened.

"Just something small," he said. "To keep the sensation familiar."

Rachel set down her water and stood, legs protesting. The others gave them space without being asked, drifting to the edges of their rest area. Creating a buffer zone.

In case something went wrong.

Arathen picked up a small stone from the roadside. "Stop this before it hits the ground. Like the bead."

He tossed it gently.

Rachel's hand came up instinctively. She focused on the warmth, the memory of control, the clean catch of breath she'd felt when it worked before.

The stone slowed.

Not stopped—not suspended in mid-air like the bead had been—but it drifted down like it was falling through honey instead of air, giving her time to catch it in her palm.

The warmth in her hand flared briefly, then faded.

"Good," Arathen said, and he sounded genuinely pleased. "That's progress. You're not fighting it anymore."

"It felt easier," Rachel admitted, surprised. "Less... stuck."

"Because you're not panicking," he said. "Fear makes it pool instead of flow. The more you practice in calm moments, the better you'll handle urgent ones."

Rachel turned the stone over in her hand. It was just a rock. Ordinary. But she'd changed how it fell, bent the rules of gravity around it, and it had felt almost... natural.

That should have been terrifying.

It was. But it was also starting to feel like hers.

"Again?" Arathen asked.

Rachel nodded.

They practiced three more times before Ilyra called that they needed to move. Each time, the warmth came quicker, the control smoother. By the last one, Rachel caught the stone mid-air and held it there for a breath before letting it drop into her hand.

Then something changed.

Rachel felt it like pressure against her awareness—not physical, but present. The sensation of being watched, but not with eyes. Someone was observing her through boundaries, their attention brushing against her like fingertips through fabric.

She froze, the stone dropping from her hand.

"What's wrong?" Arathen asked immediately.

"Someone's watching," Rachel said quietly. She turned slowly, trying to locate the source. "Not here. Through boundaries. I can feel them."

Arathen went very still. His own awareness extended outward, searching. After a moment, his expression darkened. "I don't sense anything."

"They're there," Rachel insisted. The presence lingered at the edge of her perception—curious, assessing, not hostile but definitely intentional. "They're... far away. Not physically close. But watching through boundary space somehow."

"That's advanced technique," Arathen said quietly. "Remote observation. Most demons can't do it at distance." He looked at Rachel with concern. "The fact that you can sense it when I can't—that's significant."

"Is it dangerous?" Maelin asked, hand moving toward her pack.

"Not immediately," Arathen said. "But it means someone knows about Rachel. Someone powerful enough to track her across boundary regions." He paused. "And skilled enough to hide from me while doing it."

The presence withdrew suddenly, like a door closing. Rachel felt its absence as clearly as she'd felt its arrival.

"They're gone," she said.

"But they found you," Ilyra said grimly. "And they'll be back."

Arathen's jaw was tight. "We need to move faster. If someone's tracking Rachel, they might not be content with just watching."

As they shouldered their packs and continued walking, Rachel felt different. Not confident—she wasn't that foolish—but less afraid of what lived inside her.

And more afraid of whoever was interested in it.

The watcher's presence had felt analytical. Clinical. Like she was a specimen being studied.

She didn't like it at all.

As they walked again, the world opened up in small ways. A farmhouse set back from the road. A pair of travelers heading the opposite direction who nodded without stopping. Someone’s laughter drifting from somewhere Rachel couldn’t see.

This wasn’t wilderness.

That unsettled her more than she expected.

By late afternoon, the pack's weight had become nearly unbearable. Rachel's shoulders burned with each step, the straps cutting into muscle that hadn't known this kind of sustained strain existed. She was calculating how to ask for a rest without revealing how badly she needed one when Ilyra slowed.

"We stop here," she said.

Relief flooded through Rachel so intensely it felt almost like pain.

As they left the road, a thought struck her with unexpected force: What day was it?

She tried to count back. The summoning had been—what, Wednesday? Thursday? She'd lost track somewhere between the second test with Arathen and the packing. How many days had it been? A week? More?

Back home, Evan would have filed a missing person report by now. Would have called everyone she knew. Her parents would be frantic. Mara would be organizing search parties. Her coworkers would be whispering theories.

And here she was, walking down a dirt road in another world, more concerned about whether her pack straps were adjusted correctly than about the people who loved her tearing themselves apart with worry.

How long had it been for them? With time moving differently between worlds, they could have been searching for weeks. Months. They might have already held a memorial service for her.

The guilt hit hard enough to steal her breath.

And underneath it, worse than the guilt—the knowledge that even if they found a way to send her back tomorrow, the resonance had already grown so strong she wasn't sure she could leave. The connection deepened with every step she took in this world. Every meal shared. Every moment of magic used.

The timer wasn't just ticking anymore. It might have already run out.

They left the road, moving into a shallow grove of trees that offered cover without feeling enclosed. The ground looked level enough, clear enough.

Maelin crouched to examine the earth, pressing her palm flat against it. "This will do."

Edrin began unstrapping his pack, movements economical despite his obvious fatigue.

Rachel let her pack slide from her shoulders with a groan she couldn't quite suppress. The sudden absence of weight made her feel unbalanced, as if she might float away without it anchoring her. She stretched carefully, wincing as her spine protested.

"I don't see anywhere to shelter," she said, looking around the grove. "Are we sleeping under the stars?"

"Not quite," Ilyra said, already unpacking. "Watch."

She pulled out what looked like ordinary canvas—aged and folded small, unremarkable in every visible way. Rachel watched as Ilyra and Maelin began setting it up with practiced efficiency, poles appearing and clicking into place with motions too quick to follow. The structure that emerged looked impossibly small.

Rachel stared at it. "Please tell me that's not for all five of us."

"It's bigger than it looks," Maelin said with a small smile.

Rachel circled it once, trying to make the dimensions work in her mind. They didn't. "I don't understand. There's no way we all fit in there."

"Magic is strange about space sometimes," Arathen said, watching her process this. "The inside doesn't always match the outside."

Rachel stopped walking. Looked at him. Looked back at the tent. "That's..."

"Impossible?" Maelin suggested gently.

"I was going to say terrifying," Rachel admitted.

"It's safe," Maelin assured her. "I've slept in this tent hundreds of times. It's actually more stable than most buildings."

Rachel moved closer, then hesitated at the threshold. Through the opening, she could see light that shouldn't exist, space that shouldn't fit.

"Take your time," Arathen said. "It's disorienting the first time."

Rachel took a breath and stepped through.

The interior unfolded around her—not gradually, but all at once, as if space itself had been waiting for her to accept the invitation. A central room opened before her, far larger than the exterior could contain. Walls hung with fabric that somehow felt permanent. A low table. Shelves already stocked with supplies. Doorways suggesting other rooms beyond.

"Oh," Rachel breathed out. "Oh, this is—"

"Impossible?" Ilyra said, stepping in behind her. "Yeah. But useful."

Rachel took a few steps inside, touching the wall to confirm it was solid. "How does this work?"

"Very old magic," Edrin said, entering last and setting his pack down with a thump. "Folded space. Most people can't make these anymore."

"This is incredible," Rachel said, and found she meant it. The terror had given way to wonder. "I mean, it still violates everything I thought I knew about physics, but it's incredible."

Maelin smiled at that, pleased. "I'm glad you like it. You'll have your own space—there's a small room in the back that's quiet."

The thoughtfulness of that—of making sure Rachel had privacy—struck her unexpectedly hard.

"Thank you," she said, and her voice came out softer than she'd intended. "All of you. For—" She gestured vaguely. "For not leaving me behind. For including me."

"You're one of us now," Ilyra said matter-of-factly. "Where else would you be?"

Rachel's throat tightened. She dropped her pack and let herself lean against the wall, suddenly aware of how exhausted she was—not just physically, but emotionally.

Maelin's hand found her shoulder. "First day is done. You did it."

"We did it," Rachel corrected quietly.

Maelin squeezed gently. "Yes. We did."

Outside, light began to fade between the trees, shadows lengthening across ground they'd covered. Inside, the tent maintained its impossible steady presence, indifferent to the progression of time beyond its walls.

Rachel found a bench and sat, letting the exhaustion finally settle fully into her bones. Her body catalogued the day's damage—blisters forming, muscles strained past familiar limits, a headache building behind her eyes from concentration and sun.

Around her, the others moved through their evening routines with practiced efficiency. Edrin sorted through his pack, checking that nothing had shifted wrong. Ilyra examined her boots for damage. Maelin began unpacking what they'd need for the night.

Normal actions. Ordinary preparations.

Except nothing about this was ordinary.

Rachel looked around the impossible space—walls that shouldn't exist, light from nowhere, people who'd become something like family in less than a week.

"Get some sleep," Ilyra said, already settling into her own space. "Tomorrow's another long day."

Rachel nodded and lay back, staring at the curved ceiling.

Outside, the road waited. Inside, her eyes stayed open.


---

## Chapter Thirteen

The tent didn't settle around Rachel the way shelter should.

She'd expected arrival to feel like rest—a moment when her body recognized safety and allowed itself to stop. Instead, the space remained stubbornly temporary in her mind, warm and quiet but refusing to register as destination. Her awareness kept reaching beyond the impossible walls, tracking the road outside, measuring the distance they'd covered against the distance still ahead.

She sat on the edge of her assigned bed, examining one sock with more attention than it warranted. Was it damp? Or had her foot simply changed shape after hours of walking? The mattress adjusted beneath her when she shifted, responding in a way that felt wrong—too attentive, as if the bed itself was watching.

Across the central space, Ilyra worked at a piece of wood with a small knife, the blade moving in short, irregular strokes. No visible goal shaped the carving. The sound repeated with enough irregularity that Rachel noticed its absence as much as its presence.

Maelin stood near the cooking area, holding a jar and reading its label. She read it twice, then set it back without opening it. Her hands moved to another container and repeated the process.

Edrin sat at the low table, book closed in front of him, fingertips resting on the cover as if to prevent it from opening on its own.

Arathen leaned in the doorway, gaze fixed on nothing in particular, attention turned inward or outward—Rachel couldn't tell which.

The silence pressed in from all sides, thick with unspoken thoughts.

Rachel broke it. "The town we're heading toward," Rachel said, breaking the silence. "What should I expect?"

Maelin looked up, setting aside the jar she'd been examining. "Are you worried about it?"

Rachel shrugged, trying for casual and not quite managing it. "I don't know what I'm walking into."

Edrin set his book aside, giving her his full attention. "It's a trading town. Mixed population. People are used to seeing travelers, so you won't stand out too much."

"Will they know what I am?" Rachel asked. "Will they be able to tell?"

"Tell what?" Ilyra asked.

Rachel pulled her knees closer. "That I don't belong. That I'm from somewhere else. That I can do things I shouldn't be able to do." She paused, then added, "Speaking of which—this tent. It's made of magic, right?"

Arathen's attention sharpened slightly. "It is."

"So what happens if I—" Rachel gestured vaguely. "If my powers react to it? If I accidentally try to pause or tilt something that's already folded through impossible space?"

"That's a good question," Arathen admitted. "The tent exists in a stable pocket. It shouldn't interact with your abilities the way normal space does."

"Shouldn't," Rachel repeated.

"We're fairly certain," Edrin added, then caught himself. "Actually, we're mostly certain based on theory, but we've never had someone with your specific abilities sleep in it before."

Rachel's eyebrows rose. "That's not as reassuring as you think it is."

"If something feels wrong, you wake us," Maelin said firmly. "Immediately. Don't try to handle it alone."

Rachel nodded, then pressed her palm flat against the tent's floor. The surface felt solid enough, but there was something underneath—a subtle hum, different from the building's pulse but similar in quality. Like two notes played in harmony instead of unison.

Her fingers spread wider against the fabric. "I can feel it. The magic. It's..." She searched for the word. "Singing. Almost."

Arathen leaned forward with interest, that same intense focus from before. "And does it feel hostile? Unstable?"

Rachel concentrated, letting her awareness extend into that hum the way she'd been practicing with the stones. But this time, something shifted. The magic didn't just respond to her touch—it recognized her. Reached back, almost, like greeting like.

Her breath caught. "No. It feels... settled. Like it's been this way so long it forgot it was supposed to be impossible." She pulled her hand back quickly, unsettled by the sensation. "I think we're okay. But I'll tell you if that changes."

Arathen's eyes hadn't left her face. "You felt something else just then. What was it?"

Rachel hesitated. How did she explain that the magic had felt... familiar? Like meeting a distant relative and recognizing your own eyes in their face?

"Nothing," she said. "Just—it's strange, that's all. Feeling magic like it's alive."

Arathen's expression suggested he didn't entirely believe her, but he let it go. "That's all we ask." Something in his tone made it clear he'd filed the moment away for later consideration.

Maelin moved closer, settling beside Rachel. "You belong wherever you are. You're not less real just because you started somewhere different."

"But I don't know how to be new here," Rachel admitted. "Back home, I was good at blending in. Good at making myself small and useful and easy to ignore. But here, everything I do feels loud."

"Because you're not trying to disappear anymore," Arathen observed gently.

Rachel looked at him, startled by how precisely that landed.

Edrin leaned forward earnestly. "The people in this town—they're not going to judge you. And if anyone gives you trouble, they'll have to go through all of us first."

"We don't let people hurt our friends," Ilyra added, and the simple declaration of friendship made Rachel's eyes sting.

"I just don't want to mess this up," Rachel said. "Don't want to be the reason something goes wrong. I have a talent for that."

Maelin's expression softened with understanding. "Rachel, you've been here less than a week and you've already saved us from taking a compromised route, read a map that most trained scholars can't parse, and handled powers that would send most people into shock. You're not the liability you think you are."

"You really think that?" Rachel asked quietly.

"I know that," Maelin said firmly. "We all do."

The tent held them in comfortable closeness—not quite silent, but filled with the kind of quiet that came from people who'd begun to trust each other. Five people in an impossible space that somehow felt exactly right.

"What actually happens when we reach the town?" Rachel asked. "Best guess?"

"They'll be curious," Arathen said. "Probably cautious. We're asking them to harbor someone who crosses boundaries in unusual ways. That makes people nervous."

"So we're hoping for tolerance," Rachel said.

"We're hoping for understanding," Maelin corrected. "And if we don't get it from the town, we'll go somewhere else. You're not negotiable."

The fierceness in her voice surprised Rachel. "You barely know me."

"We know enough," Edrin said. "You're patient when we fumble explanations. You make breakfast without being asked. You warn us away from danger. Those aren't small things."

"Also you haven't run screaming yet," Ilyra added. "Which shows remarkable judgment."

Rachel huffed out something close to a laugh. "The bar is low, huh?"

"The bar is exactly where it needs to be," Ilyra replied.

Rachel lay back on the bed, staring at where the ceiling met the wall in a seam that curved impossibly. The tent didn't feel temporary anymore. It felt like shelter. Like safety.

"I'm afraid," she admitted quietly. "Of what happens next. Of who I'm becoming." She paused, then forced out the rest. "Of not knowing if I'll ever get home. Of not knowing if I even want to anymore."

The admission hung in the air like a confession.

Maelin's hand found Rachel's shoulder. "That's reasonable. All of that is reasonable."

But it wasn't, was it? What kind of person chose a world of magic and danger and impossible spaces over the safe, known life they'd left behind? What kind of person felt more at home with kidnappers than with a boyfriend who loved them?

The answer settled cold in Rachel's chest: the kind of person who'd been drowning slowly for twenty-six years and had only just learned what air tasted like.

"I should want to go back," Rachel said. "Evan is there. My job. My whole life. I should be fighting every minute to get back to them."

The emotions rose sharp and fast—guilt, grief, confusion—and with them came a familiar warmth spreading through her palm. Rachel's breath caught. The pressure building in her chest, that telltale tightness—

She recognized it now. Caught it before it could pool and trap itself.

Rachel pressed her hand flat against the bed and breathed deliberately. In through her nose. Out through her mouth. Let it flow instead of holding it. The warmth pulsed once, twice, then faded like a candle blown out.

The tent stayed stable around them.

"But you're not," Ilyra said. Not a question. If she'd noticed the brief surge of magic, she didn't mention it.

"No." Rachel's voice barely rose above a whisper, but steadier now. "I'm not. And I don't know what that makes me."

"Human." Arathen's voice was quiet but certain. "It makes you human."

He met her eyes across the dimming space, and Rachel realized he had noticed. Had seen her catch it, control it, let it go. The slight nod he gave her was acknowledgment and approval both.

"We're afraid too," Edrin added. "Of what we've set in motion. Of whether we can help you the way you need. But we're less afraid when we're together."

Arathen's voice carried weight despite its softness. "Found family is like that. You choose each other. And then you keep choosing."

Rachel closed her eyes, letting the words settle. The light began to fade—not abruptly, but gradually, as if the space itself had decided day was finished. Around her, the others prepared for sleep. Small sounds of settling. Fabric shifting. Breath evening out.

"Thank you," Rachel said into the dimming space. "For choosing me."
