"Right back at you," Ilyra replied, and something in her voice said she meant it.

Rachel lay there as the others' breathing deepened into sleep. She thought about Evan. Tried to picture his face with the clarity he deserved—the specific way his mouth pulled when he smiled, the gray T-shirt he wore on bad days.

The image came slower than it should have. Blurrier than Maelin's or Edrin's or even Ilyra's.

She'd known them less than a week.

She'd known him two years.

Rachel pressed her palm against her chest, feeling her heartbeat, and tried not to think about what that meant. About what she'd already started choosing without choosing.

About what it said about her that when she pictured home now, she saw folded canvas and impossible space and three people who'd kidnapped her and somehow become the ones she couldn't imagine leaving.

The missing piece she'd felt her entire life—that gap she'd carried through coffee shops and office buildings and dinner with Evan—it wasn't missing anymore. Or maybe it had never been missing at all. Maybe she'd just been in the wrong place for it to exist.

Here, in this impossible tent with these impossible people, she was whole.

And that truth was more terrifying than any magic she'd discovered.

If they found a way to send her back tomorrow, she'd have to decide.

She wasn't ready for that choice. Wasn't sure she'd ever be.

But deep down, underneath the guilt and the fear and the confusion, she already knew what she'd choose. Had been choosing it, without choosing, from the moment she'd stopped the glass from falling.

Rachel closed her eyes and listened to breathing that wasn't hers.

Outside, the road went on without them.


---

## Chapter Fourteen

The tent came down easier than it had gone up.

Rachel watched Arathen work, hands moving through a sequence that looked like folding laundry but felt like dismantling architecture. The impossible space collapsed into itself—not violently, just deliberately—until what remained was a square of canvas no larger than a dinner napkin.

"That shouldn't be possible," Rachel said.

"No," Arathen agreed, tucking the folded tent into his pack. "It shouldn't."

Around them, the others moved through their own morning routines with practiced efficiency. Edrin rolled his bedding tight and secured it with leather straps. Maelin checked their water supply, redistributing weight between packs. Ilyra knelt by the dead fire, burying the ashes and scattering leaves over the disturbed earth until no trace of their camp remained.

Rachel stood uselessly in the middle of it all, holding her borrowed pack and feeling like an imposter playing at adventure.

"You're doing it again," Ilyra said without looking up.

"Doing what?"

"Standing like you're waiting for permission to exist." Ilyra rose, dusting her hands. "You're part of this. Act like it."

Rachel's face warmed. "I don't know what to do."

"Then ask," Maelin said, not unkindly. "Or watch and learn. But don't hover like you're apologizing for taking up space."

The words should have stung. Instead, they settled like an invitation.

Rachel looked around, assessing. The packs were almost ready. The camp was nearly erased. What was left?

"Food," she said. "We need to eat."

"Smart," Edrin said, pausing his packing to glance at her. "We have trail rations, but they're meant to supplement, not sustain. We'll need to hunt or forage today."

"I can help with that," Rachel said, then immediately second-guessed herself. Could she? She'd never hunted in her life. Never even been camping, really, unless you counted the one corporate retreat where they'd stayed in cabins with Wi-Fi.

Arathen's eyes found hers across the clearing. "You might be more help than you think."

Something in his tone made her pulse quicken. He knew. Of course he knew. He'd been planning this.

Twenty minutes later, they broke camp as the light shifted from green-gray to something almost golden. The forest around them felt alive in a way that woods back home never had—not threatening exactly, but aware. Watching.

Rachel walked between Maelin and Edrin, her pack settling into a rhythm against her shoulders. The borrowed boots Ilyra had found in the last town were stiff but functional, and after an hour of walking, her feet had started to remember how to move over uneven ground.

"There," Ilyra said quietly, stopping so suddenly that Rachel nearly walked into her.

Everyone froze.

Ilyra pointed ahead and to the left, where the undergrowth thinned near a stand of silver-barked trees. At first, Rachel saw nothing. Then movement—a flicker of brown, low to the ground.

A rabbit. Larger than the ones back home, with ears that swiveled independently and fur that seemed to shift color depending on the angle of light. It was nibbling at something green, nose twitching.

Edrin's hand moved slowly toward the bow strapped across his back.

The rabbit's head snapped up.

"Wait," Arathen said softly. He was looking at Rachel. "Let her try."

Rachel's stomach dropped. "I've never—"

"You don't need to kill it," he said. "Just stop it. Give Edrin time to aim."

The rabbit was already moving, haunches tensing to bolt.

Rachel's hands went cold. "I don't know if I can—"

"You can," Arathen said. Not a question. A statement.

The rabbit leaped.

Rachel's hand shot out—pure instinct, no thought—and the world caught.

Not completely. Not like the cup, where everything had frozen solid. This was different. Gentler. The rabbit hung mid-leap, legs extended, but it was still moving—just impossibly slowly, like it was pushing through water instead of air.

Rachel's breath came short and fast. The warmth was there in her palm, familiar now, but controlled. Flowing instead of pooling. She could feel the boundaries of what she was doing—not stopping time, but bending it, stretching the moment until it lasted longer than physics allowed.

Edrin moved. Smooth. Practiced. Drew the bow. Aimed.

Released.

The arrow flew true.

Rachel let go.

The world resumed. The rabbit dropped, already dead before it hit the ground. Quick. Clean.

Rachel stood there, hand still outstretched, heart hammering.

"Good," Ilyra said. Just that. But she sounded impressed.

Maelin squeezed Rachel's shoulder as she passed, moving to help Edrin retrieve the kill.

Arathen hadn't moved. He was watching Rachel with that same intensity from before—measuring, cataloging. But beneath the observation was something else. Satisfaction, maybe. Or confirmation.

"How did that feel?" he asked.

Rachel looked down at her hand. The warmth was already fading, leaving only a pleasant tingle. "Different from before. Not as... heavy."

"Because you're learning control," Arathen said. "Learning to modulate. That's remarkable progress for someone who discovered their abilities less than two weeks ago."

There it was again. That emphasis. That careful choice of words that suggested he was thinking something he wasn't saying.

"It felt easy," Rachel admitted quietly. "Is that bad?"

Arathen's mouth pulled into something that wasn't quite a smile. "That depends on your perspective."

Before Rachel could ask what that meant, Maelin called out, "Two more ahead. Small clearing, thirty paces north."

They moved as a unit now, quieter, purpose focused. Rachel found herself falling into the rhythm without thinking—watching where she stepped, breathing shallow, moving when they moved and stopping when they stopped.

The clearing opened up ahead. Two more rabbits, these larger, grazing near a fallen log.

This time, no one had to tell her what to do.

Rachel reached out—not with her hand, but with that sense she was starting to recognize. The one that felt like touching something intangible, like running her fingers through water that shouldn't exist.

The rabbits slowed.

Not frozen. Not even obviously stopped. Just... delayed. Moving at half-speed, then quarter-speed, their awareness of danger arriving too late to matter.

Edrin took one. Ilyra, bow suddenly in hand, took the other.

The world snapped back.

Rachel swayed, exhaustion pulling at her knees, but Maelin was already there, steadying her.

"I'm okay," Rachel said.

"I know," Maelin replied. "But you're allowed to not be."

They field-dressed the kills with an efficiency that spoke of long practice. Rachel watched, queasy at first, then increasingly fascinated. There was no waste. No cruelty. Just necessity transformed into sustenance with respect and skill.

"You're not squeamish," Edrin observed, glancing up from his work.

"I thought I would be," Rachel admitted. "But it feels... I don't know. Honest?"

"It is," Maelin said. "More honest than buying pre-packaged meat and pretending it appeared by magic."

Rachel almost laughed at that. Here, the magic was real. The meat was earned.

By the time they found a suitable campsite—a clearing near a narrow stream where the water ran clear and cold—the wrapped meat had settled into a comfortable weight in Ilyra's pack. The rabbits roasted over a fire that Ilyra coaxed from damp wood with impressive patience. The smell made Rachel's stomach clench with hunger so sharp it almost hurt.

When had she last been truly hungry? Not snack-hungry or skipping-lunch-because-of-meetings hungry. Actually hungry, where food became something necessary instead of something scheduled.

Edrin portioned the meat carefully, making sure everyone got equal shares. Rachel accepted hers and bit in without waiting for it to cool. The heat stung her tongue, but the flavor—smoky and rich and real—made her not care.

"Slow down," Ilyra said, amused. "It's not going anywhere."

"Sorry," Rachel mumbled around a mouthful.

"Don't be sorry," Maelin said. "Be hungry. You earned it."

That stopped Rachel mid-chew. Earned it. Because she'd helped. Because her powers had been useful instead of terrifying.

She looked across the fire and found Arathen watching her again, expression unreadable in the flickering light.

"What?" Rachel asked.

"You're smiling," he said.

Rachel touched her face. She was. "Is that bad?"

"No," Arathen said quietly. "It's good. You should smile more."

Something in his tone made her chest tighten—not painfully, just aware. She looked away, back to her food, and tried to ignore the warmth that had nothing to do with the fire.

Later, after the meal was finished and the bones were buried and the fire had burned down to coals, Rachel sat with her back against a tree and watched the sky darken through unfamiliar constellations.

She should miss home. Should be thinking about Evan, about her apartment, about everything she'd lost.

Instead, what she felt was this: satisfaction. Competence. Belonging.

And beneath it all, a quiet guilt that she was starting to ignore.

"You're thinking too loud," Ilyra said from somewhere to her left.

Rachel huffed. "Is that possible?"

"With you, apparently."

Rachel let the silence settle before speaking again. "I used to have panic attacks about being useless. About not contributing enough at work, at home, in my relationship. Like I was always one mistake away from people realizing I didn't actually belong anywhere."

"And now?" Maelin asked gently.

Rachel looked at her hands—the same hands that had stopped time, that had made the hunt possible, that had helped feed the people around her. "Now I stopped two rabbits mid-leap so we could eat dinner. And it felt like the most natural thing in the world."

"Because it was," Arathen said. He'd been so quiet she'd almost forgotten he was there. "Natural for you, at least."

There it was again. That implication.

Rachel turned to look at him. "What aren't you telling me?"

Arathen met her gaze steadily. "A lot of things. But most of them are just theories until we reach Sivara. She'll have better answers than I can give you."

"But you have suspicions," Rachel pressed.

"Yes."

"Are they bad?"

Arathen considered that. "They're complicated. And potentially difficult to accept. But bad?" He shook his head slowly. "I don't think so. Different, certainly. But not bad."

Rachel wanted to push, to demand he tell her everything he suspected. But exhaustion was pulling at her now—the good kind, earned through movement and magic and purpose.

"Tomorrow," she said.

"Tomorrow," Arathen agreed.

Rachel pulled her borrowed blanket tighter and let her eyes close. Around her, the others settled into their own sleep. The forest breathed. The stream whispered. The stars wheeled overhead in patterns that had never mapped her home.

And for the first time since the summoning, Rachel fell asleep without dreaming of the life she'd left behind.



---

## Chapter Sixteen

The town arrived gradually—not with walls or gates, but with evidence of habitation seeping into the landscape like water finding its level. First came the cleared spaces where trees had been persuaded to grow elsewhere. Then stumps worn smooth by years of use as sitting places, resting places, places to set down what you carried. A path that had been walked often enough to become permanent.

Rachel noticed the transition. Noticed when the forest became managed, when wildness gave way to intention.

"Merridan's Cross," Edrin said, adjusting his pack. "Small trading post. We can resupply here."

Rachel's stomach tightened without clear reason. People. Strangers. The first time since the summoning that she'd need to exist around anyone who didn't already know what she was.

What she might be.

Maelin fell into step beside her, close enough that their shoulders nearly touched. "You'll be fine."

"I wasn't worried," Rachel said automatically, then caught herself. "That's a lie. I'm worried."

"Good," Maelin said. "Means you're paying attention."

The town materialized properly as they rounded the last bend—a collection of structures that seemed to have negotiated their placement with the forest rather than imposing themselves upon it. Wood and stone, practical and worn, arranged around a central square where people moved with the unhurried purpose of daily routine.

But the wood was wrong. Rachel noticed it immediately—the grain ran in spirals instead of straight lines, and the color shifted from gray to pale green depending on the angle of light. The stone wasn't quite stone either, or not any stone she recognized. Too smooth. Too uniform. Like it had been grown rather than quarried.

The sky above the square held that same faint green wash she'd never quite adjusted to, making the afternoon light feel perpetually on the edge of storm without ever delivering. Rachel counted maybe thirty buildings. Smaller than she'd expected. Larger than she'd hoped.

Ilyra moved ahead without discussion, her posture shifting into something more deliberate. Scout mode, Rachel had started calling it in her head. Edrin stayed close to Arathen, their conversation dropping to murmurs Rachel couldn't parse from this distance.

"First time's always strange," Maelin said quietly. "Being around people who don't know your story."

Rachel glanced at her. "How many times have you done this?"

"Enough to know the feeling doesn't go away." Maelin's mouth curved slightly. "You just get better at pretending it does."

They entered the square, and Rachel felt the shift immediately. Eyes found them—not hostile, just assessing. New people meant questions. Meant disruption to routine, however minor. 

A woman carrying baskets slowed her pace to watch them pass. The baskets were woven from something that looked like wicker but gleamed faintly, catching light like metal. Two men near a cart stopped their conversation mid-sentence. The cart's wheels were carved from a single piece of that wrong-grained wood, wearing smooth but not splintering.

Smells hit Rachel in waves—bread baking, but with an undertone that was almost citrus. Smoke from cook fires that smelled like pine and something sweeter, almost like vanilla. And underneath it all, that persistent herbal scent she'd started associating with this world, sharp and green and alive.

A child ran past chasing something that looked like a cat if cats had six legs and ears that swiveled independently. The creature chirped—actually chirped, like a bird—before disappearing between buildings.

Rachel kept her hands loose at her sides and tried to look like someone who belonged anywhere.

It didn't work.

She could feel it in the way attention slid over the others and caught on her. Edrin looked like a scholar. Maelin like a traveler. Ilyra like someone you didn't want to cross. Arathen like someone important trying not to be.

Rachel looked like someone who didn't fit her own skin.

"Ignore it," Maelin murmured.

"They're staring."

"They're curious. It's different."

"Doesn't feel different," Rachel said.

Arathen led them to a building marked with a hanging sign Rachel couldn't read—symbols that meant nothing to her but that the others recognized immediately as a general store. The door stood open, propped with a stone worn smooth on one side.

Inside, the air smelled like dust and dried herbs and something metallic Rachel couldn't identify—not iron or copper, but something that sat at the edge of those familiar scents without quite matching either.

Shelves lined the walls, packed with goods arranged in an order that made sense to someone but not to her. Jars held things that might have been grains or might have been something else entirely—one variety had a faint iridescent sheen, another seemed to shift colors as Rachel's angle of view changed. Fabric bolts in colors that didn't quite match Earth's palette—a blue that leaned too purple, a red that verged on orange but not quite. Rope coiled in bundles, but the fibers looked almost crystalline in places, catching the light coming through windows made of something clearer than glass should be.

A woman stood behind a counter near the back, her hands moving through the routine of inventory without looking up. She wore her hair in a braid that incorporated small beads—wooden, Rachel thought at first, until one caught the light and shimmered like abalone shell.

"Arathen," she said, voice carrying the weight of recognition.

"Merra," he replied, and Rachel heard warmth there. History. "It's been too long."

The woman looked up then, and her expression shifted through several emotions in quick succession—surprise, pleasure, something more complicated—before settling on professional welcome.

"Two seasons at least." Merra's eyes moved past Arathen to the rest of them, cataloging, assessing. They stopped on Rachel. Stayed there a beat too long. "You're traveling with a larger group than usual."

"Circumstances changed," Arathen said, easy and unrevealing.

Merra's attention lingered on Rachel for another moment before she seemed to physically pull it away. "What do you need?"

Edrin moved forward with a list he'd been maintaining since they'd left the outpost. His voice fell into the rhythm of negotiation—quantities, qualities, prices that meant something in a currency Rachel didn't understand. Maelin drifted toward the herb section, attention caught by something she recognized.

Rachel stood near the door and tried to be unobtrusive.

It didn't work.

She could feel Merra's awareness like a physical thing, kept carefully at the edge of her vision. Not looking directly. Not asking questions. But aware. Hyperaware.

Ilyra materialized beside Rachel, voice low. "She's nervous."

"Of me?"

"Of something." Ilyra's eyes tracked the shopkeeper with professional interest. "Could be you. Could be that we showed up at all. Hard to say."

Rachel watched Merra measure something into a cloth bag, movements precise and slightly too quick. "I'm making her uncomfortable."

"You're new," Ilyra said. "New makes everyone uncomfortable."

But that wasn't it. Rachel could tell. She'd spent twenty-six years reading rooms, calibrating her presence to make others comfortable, shrinking herself into acceptable shapes. She knew the difference between wariness of strangers and wariness of something else.

Something wrong.

Maelin called her over, gesturing to a display of travel supplies. "These are good," she said, holding up a pair of gloves. "Better than what you have."

Rachel accepted them, grateful for the distraction. The leather was soft, well-made, with reinforced palms. "How much?"

"Let Arathen handle the money," Maelin said. "We'll settle accounts later."

Rachel turned the gloves over in her hands, trying to feel normal. Trying to be normal. A person buying gloves in a shop. An ordinary transaction.

She moved toward the counter to add them to their collection of supplies.

Merra looked up as Rachel approached. Their eyes met.

The woman went still.

Not frozen. Not afraid exactly. But something in her expression shifted, retreating behind professional courtesy like a door closing softly but definitively.

Rachel set the gloves on the counter. "These too, please."

"Of course." Merra's hand reached for them.

Rachel reached at the same time.

Their fingers didn't touch—Rachel pulled back instinctively—but Merra flinched anyway. A small movement, quickly controlled, but unmistakable.

The shopkeeper took the gloves and added them to the growing pile, her movements brisk and final. "That's everything?"

"Yes," Arathen said, producing coins from a pouch Rachel hadn't noticed before. "Thank you, Merra."

"Safe travels," Merra said, and she meant it as dismissal.

They left.

Outside, the afternoon had progressed into something heavier, light thickening toward evening. The square had emptied somewhat, though Rachel still felt eyes tracking their movement.

"Well," Edrin said, too bright. "That went well."

"Did it?" Rachel asked.

No one answered.

They found an inn at the edge of the square—larger than the other buildings, with a sign depicting a tree Rachel didn't recognize. The trunk spiraled in on itself, branches spreading in mathematical patterns that looked almost fractal. The wood of the sign itself had that same spiral grain, and the paint used for the image had a slight luminescence, visible even in daylight.

Arathen negotiated rooms while the others waited in what passed for a common area. The floor was made of fitted stones that weren't quite level—intentionally, Rachel realized, creating subtle patterns in the surface that guided foot traffic without being obvious about it. A few locals occupied tables near the far wall, their conversations dying when Rachel's group entered, then resuming at lower volumes.

One man had eyes that caught the light strangely—not quite cat-like, but reflecting more than human eyes should. Rachel tried not to stare, reminded herself that "human" might mean something different here, might include variations she'd been raised to think impossible.

Rachel sat with her back to the wall and watched Maelin organize their new supplies with methodical efficiency. Edrin had pulled out a small journal and was making notes about something. Ilyra stood near the window, attention fixed outside with the same focus she'd shown in the shop.

"You're thinking too loud again," Ilyra said without turning.

"Am I?"

"You want to ask why the shopkeeper reacted that way."

Rachel's hands curled against her thighs. "Yes."

"Could be a dozen reasons," Ilyra said. "Could be she sensed something about you. Could be she's had bad experiences with people who look at her the way you do."

"How do I look at people?"

Ilyra finally turned. "Like you're expecting them to see through you."

The observation landed with uncomfortable accuracy.

Arathen returned with keys and the information that they'd have two rooms for the night. "We leave at first light," he said. "Rest while you can."

Rachel accepted her share of dried fruit from Maelin's distribution and ate without tasting it. Around them, the inn settled into evening routines—food served, conversations rising and falling, the particular quality of sound that came from strangers sharing space.

She'd done this a thousand times in her old life. Restaurants. Coffee shops. Airports. Being around people who didn't know her, didn't need to know her, who existed in parallel without intersection.

It had never felt like this.

Like she was performing humanity instead of living it.

Later, in the room she shared with Maelin and Ilyra, Rachel lay on the narrow bed and stared at ceiling beams that caught shadows in their grain—that spiral grain that seemed universal in this world's wood, as if all trees grew twisted. The mattress beneath her was stuffed with something that wasn't quite straw, softer and with a faint herbal scent that wasn't unpleasant but wasn't familiar either. Through the window, she could see two moons rising—one larger and pale silver, the other smaller and faintly blue, both casting overlapping shadows that made the night outside look layered, dimensional in ways that hurt to look at too long.

Maelin's breathing had evened into sleep. Ilyra sat near the window, keeping watch in her particular way—not actively looking, just aware.

"Does it get easier?" Rachel asked quietly. "Being around people who don't know?"

Ilyra was silent long enough that Rachel thought she might not answer.

"No," she said finally. "But you get better at carrying it."

Rachel closed her eyes and tried to believe that would be enough.

Outside, the town breathed around them. Ordinary people living ordinary lives, unaware that something not-quite-ordinary lay awake in their inn, wondering what it meant that she'd never felt more like an imposter than when trying to be herself.



---

## Chapter Seventeen

They left Merridan's Cross before the town fully woke, when morning existed as intention rather than fact. The larger moon had set but the smaller blue one still hung near the horizon, casting its peculiar light across rooftops and making shadows pool in strange directions. Rachel's new boots felt stiff against her feet, leather that hadn't yet learned the shape of her stride—leather from some animal she couldn't name, dyed with something that left faint metallic undertones against the brown.

The gloves stayed tucked in her pack—not needed yet, but there, proof of transaction, proof of belonging to this world's economy however tangentially.

Proof she'd made a shopkeeper uncomfortable just by existing near her.

The thought followed Rachel out of town and into the resumed forest, persistent as the rhythm of walking. The trees here were different from the ones near the outpost—taller, with bark that had a faint ridge pattern like scales, and leaves that were blue-green instead of the yellow-green she'd started accepting as normal. Something flew overhead—too large to be a bird, wings making a leathery sound—and Rachel made herself not look up, not confirm what kind of creature cast that shadow.

Twice during the morning's walk, they'd passed through thin places—spots where boundaries pressed too close, where reality felt stretched. Both times, Rachel had felt the wrongness and had instinctively smoothed it as she passed. Not consciously. Not with effort. Just a small adjustment, like straightening a picture frame in passing. The boundaries had settled, stabilized.

Arathen had noticed. She'd seen him glance back at the corrected spots, then at her, expression thoughtful but unsurprised.

She was doing boundary work constantly now. Small adjustments. Unconscious maintenance. Her presence alone was helping stabilize the spaces they walked through.

By midday, the stiffness in her boots had progressed to blisters. Rachel didn't mention it. Edrin was limping slightly—his own boots wearing unevenly, creating pressure where pressure shouldn't be. Maelin's breathing had taken on a quality that suggested her pack's weight had found some limit. Even Ilyra moved with more care than usual, attention tracking the path ahead with the focus of someone whose body had reminded her it had boundaries.

They were all tired. All sore. All human.

Except Rachel wasn't sure that word still applied to her the way it once had.

"There," Arathen said, gesturing ahead to where the forest opened into a clearing. "We'll stop for the night."

The relief was collective and immediate.

The clearing was ringed with flowers Rachel had no names for—waist-high stalks topped with blooms that opened and closed with visible movement, responding to their footsteps with slow contractions like breathing. The grass beneath felt springier than Earth grass, each blade thicker, more succulent. When Edrin started clearing space for the fire, the cut grass released a smell like mint crossed with something citrus-sharp.

Camp assembled itself through practiced routine. Ilyra disappeared to scout the perimeter, moving between trees whose bark seemed to shift color as the light changed—responsive, Rachel realized, to temperature or touch or something else. Edrin gathered wood with the particular single-mindedness of someone grateful for a task that required no conversation, selecting fallen branches that still showed that characteristic spiral grain. Maelin set about organizing sleeping arrangements with quiet efficiency.

Rachel helped where she could—holding things, fetching things, being useful in ways that didn't require expertise she didn't have. Her boots had rubbed her heels raw. She'd deal with it later. After. When dealing with it wouldn't slow anyone else down.

The fire, when Arathen coaxed it to life, felt like permission to stop moving. The wood burned with small pops and sparks that were faintly green—something in the sap, Rachel assumed, some chemical composition that didn't match Earth trees. The smoke smelled clean, almost medicinal. To let the day's aches announce themselves properly. Rachel lowered herself to the ground near the flames—ground that was still warm from the day's sun, holding heat in a way that suggested the soil itself had different properties than Earth soil—and tried not to wince at her body's complaints.

Maelin sat beside her without preamble, a small tin already open in her hands. "Feet," she said.

"They're fine."

"Rachel." Not unkind. Just final.

Rachel pulled off her boots and immediately regretted several life choices. The blisters had progressed past theoretical into visceral reality.

Maelin made a small sound of sympathy. "New boots always do this." Her fingers were gentle, applying salve that stung before it soothed. "You should have said something earlier."

"Everyone was tired."

"Everyone's always tired when traveling," Maelin said. "That's not a reason to hurt in silence."

Rachel didn't have an answer for that. Or she had too many answers, none of which felt appropriate to voice. She'd spent twenty-six years hurting in silence because speaking up felt like demanding more space than she'd earned.

Edrin returned with an armload of wood that seemed excessive until Rachel realized he'd gathered enough for the whole evening. He arranged it with the same methodical attention he brought to his books, creating a structure that would feed the fire gradually, efficiently.

"Thank you," Arathen said, and Edrin's shoulders straightened slightly under the acknowledgment.

Ilyra emerged from the forest like she'd been assembled from shadows, her return marked more by presence than sound. "Clear," she reported. "Nothing tracking us, nothing ahead that shouldn't be."

"Good," Arathen said. He'd been preparing something in a small pot over the fire—tea, Rachel thought, or something similar. The smell was sharp and herbal, not quite familiar.

They ate trail rations that tasted like efficiency rather than flavor. No one complained. Food was fuel here, not entertainment. Rachel found herself grateful for the simplicity of it, the absence of performance.

As the light failed and the fire became the center of their world, the conversation drifted from practical matters to something less defined. Edrin asked Ilyra about a scar on her wrist—thin and white, visible when her sleeve pulled back. She told a story about a river crossing gone wrong, her aunt's clinic, a choice between infection and amputation that had been closer than anyone wanted.

"But you kept it," Maelin said.

"My aunt kept it," Ilyra corrected. "I just held still and tried not to die."

The story led to others. Maelin's mother teaching her to navigate by stars that weren't always visible. Edrin's first failed summoning, minor and embarrassing, that had convinced him he'd chosen the wrong field of study until his mentor had explained that failure was just information arriving painfully.

Rachel listened, absorbing the histories that shaped the people around her. Their casual mentions of things she didn't understand but could feel the weight of. This was how people became real—not through dramatic revelation but through accumulated detail, the small stories that built into lives.

"What about you, Rachel?" Edrin asked, and the attention shifted toward her with the gentle inevitability of tide.

Rachel's mind went blank. What stories did she have? What details built into a life worth sharing?

"I don't have anything interesting," she said finally.

"That's not what I asked," Edrin said, smiling slightly. "I asked about you, not about interesting."

Rachel looked at the fire and tried to find something worth offering. "I used to organize the office supply closet at work. Every few months, I'd just... reorganize it. Make sure everything had a place, make sure everything was in its place."

"That sounds peaceful," Maelin said.

"It sounds compulsive," Rachel corrected. "I did it because I couldn't control anything else. Couldn't fix the actual problems. But I could make sure the pens were sorted and the paper was stacked and everything was exactly where it should be." She paused. "No one ever asked me to. I just needed something to feel manageable."

The confession sat in the space between them, more revealing than she'd intended.

Edrin nodded slowly. "I understand that. The books. That's my version. When things feel impossible, I organize information. Make it systematic. Containable."

"We all have our version," Arathen added. He'd been listening more than speaking, attention moving between them with that particular quality of awareness he carried. "Different shapes, same need."

Ilyra poked the fire with a stick, sending sparks up into the dark. "Mine's sharpening blades. Even when they don't need it. Especially when they don't need it."

The acknowledgments settled something in Rachel's chest. They'd each found their own way to manage the unmanageable. She wasn't alone in that need.

Maelin shifted, drawing her knees up. "Tell us a story, Arathen. One of the old ones."

"Which kind?" he asked, though something in his tone suggested he already knew.

"Boundary walkers," Maelin said, and Rachel's attention sharpened. "The ones who exist between."

Arathen's expression grew thoughtful, firelight catching in the silver threads of his hair. "Those are complicated stories."

"Those are the ones worth telling," Maelin said.

He considered this, then nodded slowly. "Alright." He settled back, finding a position that suggested this might take time. "The first thing you need to understand is that boundaries aren't walls. They're more like... membranes. Permeable. Some things can cross them naturally. Most things can't."

Rachel's hands curled against her knees.

"Humans," Arathen continued, "exist firmly on one side. Rooted in their world, their rules, their reality. Demons exist on the other side—or sometimes in the spaces between realities. They're not bound the way humans are. They can move through boundaries like water finding cracks."

"Are they dangerous?" Rachel asked before she could stop herself.

Arathen's eyes found hers across the fire. "That's the wrong question. Anything can be dangerous. The better question is: are they inherently harmful? And the answer is no. They're just... other. Different. They exist according to different rules."

"But people fear them," Rachel said.

"People fear what they don't understand," Maelin said. "And demons are fundamentally difficult to understand from a human perspective."

Edrin leaned forward. "Some demons choose to live among humans. They learn to navigate human rules, human expectations. They integrate."

"Like Sivara," Rachel said.

"Like Sivara," Arathen confirmed. "Boundary walkers who've made a choice to exist in human spaces, who've built lives here. They're not pretending to be human—they're just... being themselves in a human context."

Rachel's throat felt tight. "How do you know if someone's a demon? Can you tell?"

Arathen weighed his answer. "Sometimes. There are signs. The way magic responds to them. The way boundaries recognize them. Sometimes it's just a feeling—like recognizing a fellow traveler on a path only you can see."

The fire crackled. No one spoke.

"What makes someone a demon instead of a human?" Rachel asked, voice steadier than she felt. "Is it about what they can do? What they are? Where they're from?"

Arathen's attention hadn't left her. She could feel the weight of it, the calculation behind his careful answer. "It's about essence," he said finally. "About what you are at your core, beneath everything else. Humans are rooted. Demons are fluid. Humans need stability. Demons need boundaries to exist between."

"That sounds lonely," Rachel said.

Maelin's expression turned thoughtful. "It can be. That's why finding others like you matters. Why community matters, even for beings who don't need community the way humans do."

Rachel stared at the fire and felt something inside her shift. A recognition she wasn't ready to name.

Arathen's voice carried weight despite its softness. "Demons aren't made. They're born into the wrong place. Or the right place, depending on your perspective. They just are. And sometimes they don't know it themselves until something happens that reveals what they've always been." Rachel knew he was speaking directly to her now, through the medium of story.

The silence stretched. Somewhere in the forest, something called—not quite birdsong, not quite anything Rachel could categorize.

"I'm tired," she said finally, the only truth she could manage.

Arathen stood, brushing off his hands. "Get some sleep. Tomorrow's another long day."

Rachel stood carefully, testing her bandaged feet. They hurt but held. Maelin rose with her, and together they moved to where bedrolls had been arranged.

But Rachel lay awake long after Maelin's breathing evened into sleep, staring at the stars through branches that never quite resolved into the patterns she remembered. The constellations here were completely foreign—not just different arrangements but different densities, different colors. One star pulsed visibly, another had a companion so close they looked like a double-yolk egg in the sky. Both moons had risen now, their overlapping light creating zones of brightness and confusing shadow.

Thinking about boundaries. About essence. About what it meant to be born into the wrong place and only discover it when the right place finally found you.

About stories told beside fires that weren't really about ancient demons at all.

About how Arathen had watched her while he spoke, like he was waiting for her to understand something he couldn't say directly.

About how she was starting to.



---

## Chapter Eighteen

The wrongness announced itself gradually, the way a headache builds from possibility into certainty. Rachel felt it first as pressure behind her eyes, then as a quality in the air that didn't translate to any sense she could name. The forest hadn't changed visibly—trees still grew with their spiral bark and scale-ridge patterns, light still filtered through blue-green leaves—but something fundamental had shifted.

The air tasted metallic. No—not metallic. That was her brain trying to categorize something that didn't have an Earth equivalent. It tasted like... displacement. Like the flavor of a gap where something should be but wasn't. Colors began saturating wrong, the greens too green, shadows too dark, everything gaining contrast that hurt to perceive.

"We're approaching the Sethril Boundary," Arathen said, as if confirming what Rachel's body already knew.

Edrin's pace slowed. His hand drifted to the strap of his pack, not quite clutching but close. "Is it stable?"

"It was two months ago," Arathen said. "But boundaries don't keep schedules."

They crested a small rise, and Rachel saw it.

Except "saw" wasn't the right word. The boundary wasn't visible the way objects were visible. It existed as wrongness made manifest, a place where reality had been folded wrong and left that way. To Rachel's eyes—if she was using her eyes, and she wasn't sure she was—it appeared as a shimmer. Not heat shimmer. Something else. Like looking at space that couldn't decide what it wanted to be.

The others had stopped. Rachel could see them squinting, heads tilted, trying to perceive what they knew should be there.

"I can't get a clear read," Ilyra said. Her usual confidence had gained an edge of frustration. "It's there, I know it's there, but—"

"But you can't quite see it," Edrin finished. "Same."

Rachel stepped forward without meaning to. The shimmer resolved as she moved closer—not into clarity exactly, but into greater complexity. She could see layers in it. Folded spaces. Places where one reality ended and another began, overlapping like pages in a book that hadn't been bound correctly.

"Rachel," Maelin said, voice careful. "Do you see something?"

"Yes," Rachel said. Then, more accurately: "No. I don't know. It's not seeing exactly."

Arathen had gone very still. "Describe it."

Rachel tried to find words for something that existed outside language. "It's like... looking at water from underneath. You can see the surface, see where air and water meet, but from this angle it looks wrong. Bent. And there are..." She gestured helplessly. "Layers. Spaces folded into other spaces. It's all wrong but also—" The realization arrived as she spoke it. "It's not wrong. It's just complicated."

"How many layers?" Arathen asked, and something in his voice made Rachel look at him.

His expression was focused in a way she'd learned to recognize. This wasn't idle curiosity. This was important.

"I don't know," she said. "Several. They keep shifting."

Edrin had pulled out his journal. "You shouldn't be able to perceive individual layers without instruments."

"But I can," Rachel said.

No one argued with her.

Arathen studied the space ahead—or studied Rachel studying it, she wasn't sure which. "The path through," he said finally. "Can you see it?"

Rachel looked again. The layers shifted and folded, spaces breathing in and out of alignment with each other. There were gaps in the wrongness. Places where the folding had left passages—narrow, unstable, but navigable.

If you could see them.

"Yes," she said quietly. "I think so."

Maelin moved closer, her presence solid and grounding. "You think so, or you know?"

Rachel watched the boundary breathe. Watched the layers slide across each other in patterns that should have been chaos but somehow weren't. There was logic in it. Rules that didn't match human physics but that were rules nonetheless.

"I know," she said.

Arathen's jaw worked. A decision being made in real-time, weighed against risks Rachel couldn't see. "Show us," he said finally.

"Arathen—" Edrin started.

"She can see it, Edrin. We can't. Not clearly." Arathen's attention hadn't left Rachel. "The alternative is backtracking three days and taking the long route through the Kirrel Pass. Which adds a week to our journey and crosses territory I'd rather avoid."

"Or we trust Rachel," Maelin said quietly.

The weight of that settled on Rachel's shoulders. Trust. The thing she'd been desperate for her entire life, now offered in the context of leading four people through a boundary collapse that could kill them all if she got it wrong.

"What if I'm wrong?" Rachel asked.

"Then we backtrack," Arathen said. "I'll know if it's too dangerous. I'll pull us out."

"You won't be able to see what I see," Rachel said.

"No," he agreed. "But I can feel when things are collapsing. You'll navigate. I'll monitor. Between us—" He left it unfinished, but the implication was clear.

Between them, they might survive.

Rachel turned back to the boundary. The layers shifted and slid, patterns resolving and dissolving. She found the gap again—narrow but stable, or as stable as anything here could be. A path that threaded between realities, touching both and neither.

"Stay close," she said. "Single file. And don't step where I don't step."

They arranged themselves into a line. Rachel first, Arathen directly behind her, then Maelin, Edrin, and Ilyra bringing up the rear. The trust implicit in that ordering made Rachel's hands shake.

She stepped forward.

The boundary responded immediately. Not attacking. Just... acknowledging. Like recognizing her presence, taking note of her intention. The shimmer pressed close on all sides, reality folding around her in ways that should have been terrifying but somehow weren't.

It felt familiar.

Rachel followed the gap between layers, feet finding solid ground where solid ground shouldn't exist. Behind her, she heard Arathen's sharp intake of breath, felt more than heard the others following. The path twisted, not in three-dimensional space but in dimensions Rachel had no names for.

"Left here," she said, though left didn't accurately describe the direction. "Step exactly where I step."

They moved in silence, breathing synchronized by necessity. The boundary pressed closer, testing. Rachel could feel it against her skin like pressure, like attention, like something aware asking questions she didn't know how to answer.

Who are you?

The thought arrived without words, without sound. Just a question impressed directly into her awareness.

Rachel didn't answer. Didn't know how to answer. Just kept walking, kept following the gap that threaded between impossibilities.

The layers shifted again. What had been a stable passage narrowed, forcing Rachel to turn sideways to continue. Behind her, Maelin made a small sound of distress.

"It's alright," Rachel said, though she had no basis for that reassurance. "Just a little farther."

Something moved in the layered space.

Not human. Not demon. Something else—something that existed native to boundary regions, feeding on the spaces between realities. Rachel felt it more than saw it: a presence that was hunger and curiosity and alien intelligence combined.

It sensed her. The unprecedented boundary walker, radiating power like a beacon.

And it attacked.

The creature came through the layers themselves—no physical form, just pressure and wrongness and teeth that existed in more dimensions than Rachel could comprehend. It struck at her like a predator lunging, trying to wrap around her consciousness, consume her boundary presence.

Rachel's instincts screamed.

She paused it.

Not the creature's physical form—it didn't have one. But its movement through boundary space, its ability to navigate the layers. She froze the rules it was using, made the gaps it needed collapse before it could reach her.

The creature recoiled, confused and angry. It circled, looking for another angle of attack.

"Rachel?" Arathen's voice, tight with concern. He couldn't see the creature, but he could feel something was wrong.

"Boundary predator," Rachel managed. "It's native here. Hunting me."

"Can you—" Arathen started.

"Yes," Rachel said.

The creature struck again, from a different layer, using rules Rachel hadn't expected. She countered desperately—not pausing this time, but redirecting. Making the boundary fold around it, trap it in space that led nowhere.

The creature thrashed, caught. Not dead—Rachel wasn't sure it could die—but contained. Imprisoned in its own hunting ground.

"Move," Rachel said urgently. "Now. While it's trapped."

They ran—as much as you could run through folded space. Rachel guided them with frantic precision, the path shifting as the creature's struggles destabilized the boundary. Behind them, she felt it breaking free, learning, adapting.

But they were faster.

The boundary pulsed one final time—a wave of disorientation that rolled through Rachel's awareness—up became sideways, forward became inward, all directions scrambling and reforming. She stumbled, caught herself against nothing, and somehow stayed upright.

Arathen's hand found her shoulder. Steadying. Grounding.

"I've got you," he said quietly.

Rachel breathed through the vertigo and found the path again. It had shifted but not closed. Still navigable. Still possible.

And behind them, the creature had stopped pursuing. They'd moved beyond its hunting range, into spaces it couldn't or wouldn't follow.

She kept moving, heart hammering, power still crackling along her boundaries.

Time stopped meaning anything. They could have been walking for minutes or hours, the boundary existing outside normal causality. Rachel focused on the gap, on the narrow thread of passage that wound between layers of folded space. Each step felt like a negotiation, like asking permission and receiving it conditionally, temporarily.

Then the pressure eased.

The shimmer pulled back. Reality settled into something that matched its own rules again. Rachel stepped forward and found herself on normal ground in a normal forest under a normal sky—or as normal as this world got. The trees here were subtly different again, bark smoother, almost like skin, and the ground cover included plants with tiny bell-shaped flowers that actually chimed when the wind moved them. Audible tiny bells, dozens of them, creating a sound like wind chimes made of glass.

She turned and watched the others emerge one by one, appearing from space that shouldn't exist but did. Maelin looked shaken. Edrin looked ill. Ilyra looked furious in the particular way of someone who'd been frightened and resented it.

Arathen stepped out last, his expression carefully neutral.

They walked several paces from the boundary before anyone spoke.

"What," Ilyra said finally, "was that?"

"A boundary crossing," Arathen said, but his attention was fixed on Rachel. "Rachel—"

"I don't want to talk about it," she said quickly. Too quickly.

"You navigated a three-layer boundary collapse without instruments," Edrin said. His voice had gone thin. "You shouldn't be able to do that. No one should be able to do that."

"But I did," Rachel said. Her hands wouldn't stop shaking. "I could see it. I could feel it. It wasn't—" She struggled for the word. "It wasn't foreign. It felt like something I should be able to do."

Maelin sat down where she stood, legs simply giving out. "That was remarkable."

"That was impossible," Edrin corrected.

Rachel looked at Arathen. Found him watching her with that same intensity from before, cataloging, theorizing, pieces clicking together in his mind like tumblers in a lock.

"What am I?" she asked. Not the first time she'd asked. But the first time she'd asked knowing he might actually have an answer.

Arathen's expression didn't change. "You're someone who can navigate boundaries the way they're meant to be navigated. By someone who belongs to them."

The words hung in the air between them.

By someone who belongs to them.

Not someone who crosses boundaries. Someone who belongs to the spaces between.

Rachel sat down next to Maelin, legs unsteady. The boundary thrummed behind them, layers shifting and folding in patterns only Rachel could perceive. Part of her wanted to turn around and look at it again. Understand it better. Learn its rules.

Part of her was terrified by how much she wanted that.

"We should keep moving," Arathen said eventually. "Put distance between us and the boundary. Camp somewhere stable."

"Define stable," Ilyra muttered, but she stood and shouldered her pack.

They walked in silence for an hour before anyone spoke again. The forest around them was blessedly ordinary, aggressively normal. Rachel tried to find comfort in the mundane reality of trees and light and ground that stayed where it should be.

It didn't work.

Because now she knew. Knew what she could do. Knew what that implied about what she was.

And she couldn't unknow it.

That evening, as they set up camp in a clearing that felt almost insulting in its normalcy, Edrin approached Rachel with his journal.

"Can you draw it?" he asked. "What you saw?"

Rachel looked at the blank page and tried to translate impossible geometry into two dimensions. The result looked like nonsense. Layers overlapping in ways that shouldn't work, spaces folded into configurations that violated every rule of perspective.

Edrin studied her drawing. "This is accurate?"

"As accurate as I can make it," Rachel said. "It's better in three dimensions. Better in more than three dimensions."

He showed it to Arathen, who looked at it for a long time without speaking.

"This is how boundary demons perceive crossings," he said finally. "Humans see boundaries as barriers. Demons see them as roads."

Rachel's breath caught.

"You're saying—" Edrin started.

"I'm not saying anything yet," Arathen interrupted. "But I'm thinking a great deal."

He handed the journal back to Edrin and walked away, leaving Rachel sitting in the firelight with her impossible drawing and her dawning, terrible understanding.

She'd always felt like she didn't fit.

Now she knew why.

Because she'd been trying to fit into the wrong shape.

The wrong world.

The wrong species.



---

## Chapter Nineteen

Morning arrived with mist and silence, the forest wrapped in quiet so complete it felt deliberate. The mist itself looked wrong—too thick, almost tangible, with a faint iridescence where it caught the early light. Rachel woke to dampness and discomfort, her body complaining about the ground, the cold, the relentless reality of travel. The bell-flowers nearby had closed for the night—or what passed for night—and sat quiet against their stems, dark pods waiting for warmth to bloom again.

She lay still and listened to the others breathe, none of them quite asleep but none quite awake either. Something called in the distance—not the almost-birdsong she'd heard before, but something deeper, resonant, like a wooden flute played in a minor key.

The boundary crossing had changed something.

Not just in how the others looked at her—though that had shifted too, gazes sliding sideways, uncertain—but in how she looked at herself. She'd navigated an impossible space with impossible ease. Had felt the boundary recognize her. Had recognized it back.

That should have been impossible.

But it wasn't. And that truth sat heavy in her chest.

They broke camp in stages, movements slow and careful. Ilyra's usual efficiency had gained an edge of fatigue. Edrin kept flexing his hands like they'd cramped and wouldn't quite uncramp. Even Maelin, steady Maelin, moved with the deliberate care of someone whose reserves had been tested.

Rachel felt fine.

Better than fine, actually. The boundary crossing had left her energized in a way that made no sense. Like she'd drawn something from the space between realities, absorbed it, made it part of herself.

She didn't mention this. Didn't want to see how that information would land on top of everything else.

Arathen watched her through breakfast. Not staring. Just... aware. Taking note. When they started walking again, he fell into step beside her.

"How are you feeling?" he asked.

"Fine."

"Rachel."

She glanced at him. Found him looking at her with that expression she'd come to recognize—the one that said he already knew the answer and was waiting for her to be honest about it.

"I feel good," she admitted. "Better than I should. Crossing the boundary—" She struggled for words. "It didn't drain me. It was more like... like it filled me up."

Arathen nodded slowly, like she'd confirmed something. "That's what I thought."

"Is that bad?"

"That depends on your definition of bad," he said. "Humans would find a boundary crossing exhausting. You found it energizing. That tells me something about what you're drawing from and how your body processes it."

Rachel's stomach tightened. "I'm not human."

It was the first time she'd said it out loud. Not as a question. As a statement.

Arathen's expression didn't change. "I think you're something more complicated than that question allows for."

They walked in silence for a while, the forest rising and falling around them. The path had become clearer—more traveled, maintained by something other than accident. Signs of habitation marked the edges: cut branches, cleared undergrowth, the particular order that came from human intervention.

"We're getting close to the next settlement," Edrin said from behind them. "Maybe another day."

Arathen nodded but didn't slow. They walked until the forest opened into a space that might once have been a clearing but had partially reclaimed itself—trees growing back in, grass tall and wild in shades that ranged from blue-green to almost purple, evidence of old fire pits now softened by moss that glowed faintly in the shadows, bioluminescent in the absence of direct light.

"Here," he said. "We'll stop."

The air in the clearing felt different, charged somehow. Insects—if they were insects—hovered in the shafts of light, their wings catching and refracting like tiny prisms.

"It's barely past midday," Ilyra protested.

"I know." Arathen's attention found Rachel. "We need to practice."

Rachel's hands went cold despite the afternoon warmth.

They set down their packs in a loose circle. The others didn't ask what they were practicing. Didn't need to. This was about Rachel's abilities, about what she could do, about quantifying something that maybe couldn't be quantified.

Arathen moved to the center of the clearing, gesturing for Rachel to join him. She did, legs steadier than her resolve.

"We've practiced stopping things," he said. "Small objects, controlled falls. You've been successful at that."

Rachel nodded.

"Now we try something harder." He looked around and found a fallen branch, mostly straight, about as long as his forearm. "I'm going to throw this. I want you to stop it and hold it. But this time—" He paused. "I want you to change its trajectory. Make it move in a different direction while it's stopped."

Rachel's throat went dry. "That's not the same as pausing."

"No," he agreed. "It's manipulation. It's imposing your will on motion itself, not just interrupting it."

"I don't know if I can do that."

"Then we'll find out." He hefted the branch, testing its weight. "Ready?"

Rachel wasn't ready. Would never be ready. But she nodded anyway.

Arathen threw the branch in a clean arc toward the tree line.

Rachel's hand came up instinctively. The warmth flared in her palm, familiar now, almost comfortable. The branch slowed—not stopped, but slowed, hanging in mid-air like it was considering its options.

Arathen nodded approval. "Good. Now change its direction. Send it... there." He pointed to a spot thirty degrees off from the branch's original trajectory.

Rachel stared at the suspended branch. Change its direction. Bend motion that was already bent, redirect force that shouldn't exist. It was impossible.

But then, she'd been doing impossible things for days now.

She focused on the branch, on the space around it, on the rules that governed motion here. The warmth in her palm spread up her arm, a pleasant heat that didn't burn so much as illuminate. She could feel the branch's momentum, paused but not gone. Potential energy waiting for release.

She wanted it to go left.

The branch went left.

Not slowly. Not uncertainly. It pivoted mid-air and shot toward the spot Arathen had indicated, slamming into a tree with force that should have shattered it. The impact echoed through the clearing.

Rachel stood frozen, hand still outstretched, warmth fading from her arm.

"Holy—" Ilyra cut herself off.

Edrin had gone very pale. Maelin's mouth had fallen open.

Arathen walked to where the branch had embedded itself in the tree bark. He pulled it free and examined it. The wood was intact. The tree bore a fresh scar.

"You didn't hesitate," he said, turning back to Rachel. "You made a decision and it happened immediately."

"I didn't mean to throw it that hard," Rachel said.

"But you could have," Arathen said. "You had the control. You chose force. Subconsciously, maybe, but you chose it."

Rachel's hands wouldn't stop shaking. "What does that mean?"

"It means you're not just pausing reality. You're manipulating it. Bending it to your will." He set the branch down carefully. "Again. But this time—" He picked up three stones from the ground. "Multiple objects at once."

"Arathen," Maelin said, worried.

"She can handle it," he said, and there was certainty in his voice that Rachel didn't feel herself.

He threw all three stones in different directions.

Rachel's awareness split without her conscious decision. She could feel all three trajectories at once, could track their paths like watching three separate screens showing different footage. The warmth spread through both arms now, through her chest, down into her core.

All three stones stopped mid-air.

The effort should have been immense. Dividing attention that way, maintaining three separate manipulations. But it wasn't hard. It was easy. Natural. Like using muscles she'd been flexing her whole life without knowing they existed.

"Hold them," Arathen said. "And then—very slowly—bring them together."

Rachel did.

The stones floated through the air like they were swimming through honey, converging on a point equidistant from their starting positions. They met with a gentle click that sounded obscenely loud in the watching silence.

"Now stop," Arathen said.

Rachel released them. All three stones dropped straight down, hitting the ground with soft thuds.

She swayed. Not from exhaustion exactly. From the strangeness of having that much control, that much power, that much impossible ease.

"You made that look effortless," Edrin said, voice thin.

"It was," Rachel admitted. "It shouldn't have been, but it was."

Arathen's expression had gone complicated. Satisfaction and concern warring for dominance. "Once more," he said. "But this time, something alive."

"Arathen—" Maelin started.

"Not dangerous," he said quickly. "Just..." He trailed off, searching.

A bird landed in a nearby tree. Small. Mostly brown, but with a crest of feathers that shifted through colors as it moved its head—blue to green to almost copper. It had four wings instead of two, the smaller pair tucked against its body. Ordinary, by this world's standards.

"That," Arathen said, pointing. "Don't hurt it. Just... pause it. See if you can."

Rachel looked at the bird. It hopped along a branch with that peculiar four-winged balance, pecking at something in the bark—insects or seeds or something else entirely—unaware it had become part of an experiment. She reached out with that sense that wasn't quite sight, that awareness that existed outside her normal senses.

She could feel the bird's motion. Not just see it—feel it. The flutter of its heart. The air in its lungs. The tiny adjustments of balance as it moved.

Rachel paused it.

The bird froze mid-peck, one foot raised, wings slightly spread for balance. It stayed that way, suspended in its own moment, unaware time had stopped.

"Let it go." Arathen's voice was gentle but firm.

Rachel tried to release it, loosening her grip on the pause the way she had with the stones.

The bird didn't move.

Rachel's breath caught. She tried again, pushing gently, then harder. The bird remained frozen, perfectly still, trapped in the moment she'd created.

"It's not—" Rachel's voice rose with panic. "It's not working. Why isn't it working? Oh god, did I kill it?"

"You didn't kill it," Arathen said, moving closer quickly. "Rachel, breathe. You're holding too tight."

"I'm not holding anything!" Rachel said desperately. "I let go! I—"

"You're still connected to it," Arathen said, his voice calm despite the urgency. "You paused it so completely that you wove yourself into the pause. You need to unweaveyourself first, then release it."

"I don't know how to do that!" Rachel's hands were shaking.

"Yes you do," Arathen said. "You've been doing it instinctively all along. Feel where you're connected. That thread between you and the bird. Now gently—very gently—pull back. Not release. Retreat."

Rachel closed her eyes, focused on the sensation Arathen described. She could feel it—a connection, almost like touching, between her awareness and the bird's suspended moment. She pulled back carefully, untangling herself.

The bird shuddered once. Then completed its peck, found whatever it was looking for, and flew away without any other sign that anything unusual had happened.

Rachel's legs gave out. She sat down hard, shaking.

The clearing went silent.

"That shouldn't be possible," Edrin said finally, voice thin. "Living things have their own temporal momentum. You can't just stop them without—" He gestured helplessly. "Without consequences. Without backlash."

"I almost couldn't restart it," Rachel said, voice barely above a whisper. "What if I hadn't been able to? What if Arathen hadn't known how to help?"

Arathen moved closer, crouching beside her. "But I did. And you learned. That's the point of practice, Rachel. To make mistakes in controlled situations so you don't make them when it matters."

"Because you're not fighting against time," Arathen said. "You're negotiating with it. Asking instead of forcing. And time is saying yes."

"Why would time say yes to me?" Rachel asked.

Arathen met her eyes. "Because you have the authority to ask. Because some part of reality recognizes you as something that belongs to it. That has the right to bend its rules."

Rachel sat down hard. Her legs had given out without her permission. "I don't want this."

"I know," Maelin said, moving close. "But you have it anyway."

Then Rachel felt it again. The same presence she'd sensed days ago during practice—watching, observing through boundaries. But closer now. More immediate.

She stood abruptly, power flaring instinctively.

"Someone's here," she said.

Arathen reacted immediately, his own awareness extending. "Where?"

"There," Rachel pointed toward the tree line. "Not physically. Through boundary space. But closer than before. They're—"

A figure materialized at the clearing's edge.

Not fully present. Half-real, existing partially in boundary space, partially in their physical reality. A demon—tall, gaunt, with features that seemed to shift and blur as Rachel looked at them. A scout. An observer who'd perfected the art of remote presence.

"Rachel, daughter of Theryn," the figure said. Their voice resonated strangely, like it was traveling through water. "You've become quite interesting."

Ilyra had a knife out instantly. Arathen moved between Rachel and the scout.

"Who sent you?" Arathen demanded.

The figure smiled—or something that might have been a smile. "Information brokers don't reveal clients. But your little heir should know—she's not unknown anymore. Not unnoticed. The unprecedented collapse repair. The boundary predator defeat. The power that draws watchers like moths to flame." The scout's attention fixed on Rachel. "You're a beacon, little heir. And beacons attract attention."

"Are you a threat?" Rachel asked, finding her voice.

"I'm an observer," the scout said. "Others will be threats. I'm just here to document." They tilted their head. "Though I'll give you one warning, free of charge: the demons who know about you now aren't all friendly. Some see opportunity. Others see threat. A few see both." The scout's form began fading. "Be careful, unprecedented one. The March remembers those who change it. Not always kindly."

They vanished completely, presence withdrawing through boundaries Rachel could feel closing behind them.

"What the hell was that?" Maelin asked.

"A scout demon," Arathen said grimly. "They spy, they document, they sell information to the highest bidder. If they're tracking Rachel..." He looked at her with worry. "Word has spread. About what you are. What you can do."

"The watcher from before," Rachel said, realization dawning. "It was them. They've been following us. Documenting."

"And now they have enough information to sell," Ilyra said. "Which means whoever's interested in Rachel will know exactly where she is, what she can do, and how vulnerable she is."

"We need to reach Thornhaven faster," Arathen said. "Get somewhere with established protections. Before whoever buys that information decides to act on it."

Rachel felt cold despite the afternoon warmth. She wasn't just being watched anymore.

She was being hunted.

And the hunters now had all the information they needed.

Rachel's voice came out flat. "Everything I touch breaks. Every rule I interact with bends. I'm dangerous."

"You're controlled," Arathen corrected. "You could have hurt that bird. You could have shattered those stones. You could have killed me with that branch. But you didn't. That's not danger. That's precision."

"Precision I shouldn't have," Rachel said.

"Perhaps," Arathen agreed. "But you have it nonetheless. And the question isn't whether you should. It's what you do with it now that you know."

Rachel looked at her hands. They looked normal. Human. Unremarkable. But she'd stopped a living creature's heart with them. Paused it mid-beat. And then let it continue like nothing had happened.

That power shouldn't exist.

But it did. In her. Part of her.

She pulled her knees to her chest. "I want to stop."

"Okay," Arathen said immediately. "We stop."

The relief was profound. Rachel stood, tested her legs, found them willing to support her again. The others had spread out, giving her space, giving themselves space. Processing what they'd seen.

What she'd done.

That evening, Ilyra helped Rachel gather firewood. They worked in silence until Ilyra finally spoke.

"You're scared of yourself."

"Yes," Rachel said.

"Good," Ilyra said. "People who aren't scared of their own power do terrible things with it."

Rachel looked at her. "Is that supposed to be comforting?"

"No," Ilyra said. "It's supposed to be true."

They carried the wood back together. Built the fire together. Sat around it together as night fell and the forest closed in.

Rachel tried not to think about how easy it had been to stop a bird's heart. Tried not to think about what that meant, what she could do, what she might be capable of if she stopped being careful.

Tried not to think about how part of her had liked it. The power. The control. The way reality bent to her will without argument.

That was the most frightening part.

Not that she could do these things.

But that they felt right.



---

## Chapter Twenty

Sleep wouldn't come.

Rachel lay in her bedroll and listened to the others breathe their way into rest—Maelin's soft sighs, Edrin's occasional snore, Ilyra's deliberate stillness that might have been sleep or might have been perfect control. The fire had burned down to coals, just enough light to suggest shapes without defining them.

Her mind wouldn't quiet.

The bird. The stones. The branch embedded in the tree. Her hands doing impossible things with impossible ease. Arathen's voice saying _You're not fighting against time. You're negotiating with it._

And time was saying yes.

Rachel sat up slowly, carefully, trying not to disturb anyone. Her body protested—sore from travel, from sleeping on ground, from existing in a world that asked more of her physically than her old life ever had. She pulled her blanket around her shoulders and stood, bare feet finding grass cold with night moisture. The bioluminescent moss around the fire pit's edge pulsed faintly with her footsteps, responding to pressure or vibration. Both moons hung overhead, the larger silver one nearly full, the smaller blue one a crescent, and their combined light made everything look layered, dimensional.

Arathen sat at the edge of camp, back against a tree, attention fixed on something Rachel couldn't see. Not sleeping. Keeping watch, maybe. Or just choosing not to rest while his mind worked through problems she could only guess at.

He turned slightly as she approached, unsurprised by her presence. Like he'd been expecting her.

"Can't sleep?" he asked quietly.

"No."

He gestured to the space beside him. Rachel sat, arranging her blanket, putting herself at an angle where they could see each other without the intensity of direct confrontation. The forest stretched before them, darkness layered with deeper darkness. Those bell-flowers had opened slightly—she could hear their faint chiming in the night breeze, a sound like distant music. Something glowed in the undergrowth, bioluminescence marking paths or territories or something else entirely. The air smelled of that persistent herbal sharpness, mixed with the clean smoke from their dying fire.

"What are you thinking about?" Arathen asked.

Rachel considered lying. Deflecting. Performing the kind of social maintenance she'd spent twenty-six years perfecting. But exhaustion had worn her defenses thin, and this was Arathen, who already knew most of her truths anyway.

"I'm thinking about how easy it was," she said. "Stopping the bird. Moving the stones. All of it. It should have been hard. Should have taken concentration, effort. But it just... happened. Like breathing."

"And that frightens you," he said. Not a question.

"Yes." She pulled the blanket tighter. "I've spent my whole life feeling like I had to work twice as hard to be half as good. At work, in relationships, just existing. Everything was effort. And now—" She gestured helplessly. "Now I have power I didn't ask for, doing things that should be impossible, and it's the easiest thing I've ever done."

Arathen was quiet for a moment. When he spoke, his voice was careful. "What if that's not a contradiction? What if it's confirmation?"

Rachel looked at him.

"You struggled," he continued, "because you were trying to be something you weren't. Fit into a shape that didn't match your edges. You worked twice as hard because you were fighting against your own nature." He paused. "And now you're not. Now you're doing what you were built to do. So it feels easy because it is easy. For you."

The words landed with uncomfortable precision.

"That's a terrifying thought," Rachel said.

"Why?"

"Because it means everything before was wrong. My whole life, every choice I made, every version of myself I tried to be—all of it was just me failing to be human correctly."

Arathen's voice softened. "Or it was you trying very hard to be something you're not. Which isn't failure. It's survival."

Rachel pressed her palms against her eyes. "What am I, Arathen?"

"I think you know."

"I want to hear you say it."

He shifted, turning to face her more fully. The coals cast just enough light to outline his features—the lines around his eyes, the silver threading through his hair, the expression that held more compassion than she'd expected.

The words came out steady, certain. "I think you're a demon. Or something very close to one. A boundary walker who was born in the wrong world. Someone who should have grown up knowing what they were, but didn't. Who spent twenty-six years trying to be human when that was never what you were meant to be."

Rachel had known he'd say it. Had been building toward this understanding for days. But hearing it spoken aloud still felt like falling.

"Demons are supposed to be evil," she said. "Dangerous."

"Who told you that?" Arathen asked. "Because it's not true. Demons are just beings who exist according to different rules. Some are dangerous. Some are kind. Most are just trying to live their lives, same as anyone else."

"But I don't know the rules," Rachel said. "I don't know what I'm supposed to be, how I'm supposed to exist. I'm just—" Her voice cracked. "I'm just making it up as I go and hoping I don't destroy something important by accident."

"That's called learning," Arathen said. "Everyone does it. You're just learning a curriculum you should have started years ago."

"What if I hurt someone?" Rachel whispered. "What if I lose control and—"

"You won't," Arathen said, certainty in his voice.

"You don't know that."

"I do, actually." He leaned back against the tree, eyes never leaving hers. "Rachel, I've been studying boundary magic and temporal manipulation for thirty years. I've seen people with a fraction of your power destroy everything around them because they couldn't control it. You have more ability than anyone I've ever encountered, and you're exercising a level of control that shouldn't be possible for someone so new to their abilities."

"That doesn't comfort me," Rachel said.

"It should," he replied. "Because it means whatever you are, whoever you are at your core—you're someone who chooses caution. Who values the people around you more than your own power. That's not a small thing."

He paused, then added more thoughtfully, "And your range is expanding. Have you noticed? The small boundary repairs you're doing instinctively—they're affecting larger areas now. Wider scope. When we first started traveling, you could sense thin places within a few feet. Now you're reading boundaries across the entire camp radius." His expression was both impressed and concerned. "That kind of growth is exponential. In another week, you might be affecting boundary stability across hundreds of yards without conscious effort."

"Is that dangerous?" Rachel asked.

Arathen chose his words with visible thought. "It's unprecedented. Which means I don't know. But it suggests your power isn't just growing—it's expanding its reach. Learning to work at scale without needing you to actively direct it." He met her eyes. "If that progression continues, you might be capable of manipulating boundaries across much larger areas than either of us anticipated."

The implications settled uneasily in Rachel's stomach. She'd been thinking of her abilities as personal, contained. The idea that they might scale up without her conscious control was both fascinating and terrifying.

Rachel looked at her hands. Ordinary hands. Human-looking hands. Hands that had stopped a bird's heart and let it resume like nothing had happened. Hands that were apparently learning to affect reality at scales she couldn't yet comprehend.

"I feel like an imposter," she said. "Even now. Even knowing what I probably am. I look at Maelin and Edrin and Ilyra and you, and you're all so clearly yourselves. And I'm just—" She struggled for the word. "Hollow. Like I'm performing existence instead of living it."

Arathen's hand found her shoulder. "That feeling is what happens when you spend twenty-six years pretending to be something you're not. It's going to take time to unlearn. To figure out who Rachel is when she's not trying to fit into a human-shaped space."

"What if I don't like who that is?"

Arathen's expression gentled. "What if you do?"

The question hung between them. Rachel turned it over, testing its weight, its implications. What if she did like who she was when she stopped pretending? What if this version—the one who could bend reality, who could see boundaries, who belonged to the spaces between worlds—what if this was the version she was supposed to be all along?

"I'm scared," she admitted.

"I know," Arathen said. "That's why you'll be careful. That's why you'll learn instead of assuming. That's why—" He paused, something shifting in his expression. "That's why you're going to be okay."

The certainty in his voice made Rachel's chest tighten. "You don't know that."
