"I know you," he said. "I've watched you. Seen how you think, how you choose. You're cautious to the point of self-sacrifice. You care more about not hurting others than you care about your own comfort. That's not going to change just because you discover you have power."

"Power changes people," Rachel said.

"Power reveals people," Arathen corrected. "And you've been revealed as someone who stops to make sure the bird is okay after using it as a test subject. Someone who apologizes for taking up space even when no one's asked you to shrink. That's who you are. That's who you'll continue to be."

Rachel found herself smiling despite everything. "You're very certain about a person you've known less than two weeks."

"I'm very good at reading people," he said. "It's my job."

"And what do you read in me?"

The question came out more vulnerable than she'd intended. More honest. More revealing.

Arathen was quiet for a long moment, gaze searching her face in the dim light. When he spoke, his voice had changed—softer, more personal than professional.

"I see someone who's been alone her entire life and is only just starting to realize she doesn't have to be anymore," he said. "Someone who's stronger than she knows and kinder than she believes. Someone who's been taught to think of herself as too much and not enough at the same time, when the truth is she's exactly who she's supposed to be." He paused. "And I see someone who's terrified that if people really knew her, they'd leave. When the reality is, we already know you. And we're still here."

Rachel's vision blurred. She blinked hard against tears she hadn't given permission to form.

"That's a very nice speech," she managed.

"It's also true," Arathen said.

They sat in silence, the space between them both comfortable and charged with something Rachel didn't have words for. Not attraction exactly—though maybe that too, she wasn't sure, couldn't parse her own feelings through the chaos of everything else. But connection. Recognition. The particular intimacy that came from being seen and choosing not to hide.

"Thank you," Rachel said finally. "For not lying to me. For not pretending this is all going to be simple."

"It won't be simple," Arathen agreed. "But that doesn't mean it has to be impossible."

Rachel pulled her knees up, wrapped her arms around them, made herself smaller in a gesture she'd probably learned in childhood and never unlearned. Arathen noticed—she could see him notice—but he didn't comment. Just shifted slightly, closing some of the physical distance between them without actually touching.

"When we get to Thornhaven," Rachel said, "when I meet Sivara—will she know? Just by looking at me?"

"Probably," Arathen said. "Demons recognize each other. It's not something you can hide, not from someone who knows what to look for."

"What if she doesn't want to help?" Rachel asked. "What if she sees me and decides I'm a threat, or a mistake, or—"

"Rachel." Arathen's hand found hers in the dark. Just resting there, warm and solid. "One problem at a time. Right now, the problem is you're exhausted and not sleeping. Everything else can wait until tomorrow."

His hand on hers felt significant in a way Rachel couldn't articulate. Not romantic—or not only romantic. Just... present. Real. An anchor point when everything else felt unstable.

She didn't pull away.

They sat like that for a while, watching the darkness, not speaking. Eventually Rachel felt her breathing even out, her body remembering how to be tired instead of wired. The fear that had been pressing against her chest eased slightly.

Not gone. Just bearable.

"You should try to sleep," Arathen said finally. "Tomorrow's another long day."

"Are you going to sleep?" Rachel asked.

"Eventually," he said. "Someone should keep watch."

"That's not why you're not sleeping," Rachel said.

He glanced at her, eyebrow raised. "No?"

"No. You're not sleeping because your mind won't shut off any more than mine will. You're thinking about what I am, what that means, what happens next. How to keep everyone safe, including me." Rachel paused. "Am I wrong?"

Arathen's mouth quirked. "You're not wrong."

"Then we can both not-sleep together," Rachel said. "It's less lonely that way."

Something in his expression softened. "Alright."

They sat together in the darkness, hands still touching, neither sleeping but neither alone. The forest breathed around them—literally breathed, the bioluminescent patterns pulsing in slow rhythm like a collective heartbeat. The coals glowed their slow fade into ash, sparks occasionally flaring green before dying. Somewhere a night bird called—that four-winged creature or something like it, a sound Rachel didn't recognize but that felt appropriate anyway. The bell-flowers chimed their gentle accompaniment. The two moons tracked across a sky full of unfamiliar constellations.

She was a demon. Probably. A being who belonged to boundaries and spaces between worlds. Someone who'd spent twenty-six years trying to be human and failing not because she was inadequate, but because she'd been trying to be the wrong thing entirely.

That should have been devastating.

But sitting here, with Arathen's hand warm against hers and the knowledge that he saw her—really saw her—and hadn't turned away... it felt less like an ending and more like a beginning.

"Arathen?"

"Mm?"

"What you said earlier. About me being exactly who I'm supposed to be." She swallowed. "Did you mean that?"

He was quiet for a moment. Then his hand squeezed hers, gentle and deliberate.

"Yes," he said. "I meant it."

Rachel closed her eyes and let herself believe him.

Just for tonight, she let herself believe she might actually be enough.



---

## Chapter Twenty-One

The wrongness started before dawn.

Rachel felt it in her sleep—a pull, a disturbance, like someone had reached into her dreams and twisted them sideways. She woke gasping, the sensation still clinging to her skin like humidity before a storm. The bioluminescent moss around camp had gone dark, all at once, leaving only the dying coals and the twin moons' unreliable light.

"Rachel?" Maelin's voice, quiet and alert.

"Something's wrong," Rachel said, sitting up. Her breath fogged in air that had suddenly gone cold, impossibly cold for the season. The bell-flowers that had been chiming softly all night had gone silent, their stems drawn tight against the ground like fists.

Arathen was already standing, his attention fixed on something Rachel couldn't see yet. "Everyone up. Now."

The urgency in his voice brought the others to wakefulness in seconds. Edrin fumbled for his pack, hands clumsy with sleep and sudden adrenaline. Ilyra had a knife out before she was fully on her feet, scanning the perimeter with hunter's focus.

"What is it?" Edrin asked.

"Boundary collapse," Arathen said. His jaw was tight. "Close. Maybe a mile ahead, maybe less. And it's spreading."

Rachel felt it then—not with her eyes or ears but with that sense she'd been learning to trust. The sensation of reality folding wrong, of spaces that shouldn't touch beginning to overlap. It was like the Sethril crossing but worse. Aggressive. Unstable in a way that made her teeth ache.

They broke camp in half the usual time, abandoning anything that wasn't essential. The cold had intensified, frost forming on the grass in patterns that looked almost deliberate—spirals within spirals, mathematical and wrong. When Rachel's breath hit the air, it hung there longer than it should, vapor refusing to dissipate properly.

The forest itself seemed to be reacting. Trees creaked and groaned despite the lack of wind, their bark shifting through colors Rachel had never seen them display—deep purples and sick yellows and a red that looked too much like blood. The four-winged birds had gone silent. Even the insects had disappeared.

They walked quickly, following the path that should have taken them around the boundary region. Should have.

"It's moving," Rachel said after twenty minutes. The sensation had grown stronger, more immediate. "The collapse. It's spreading toward us."

"I know," Arathen said. He'd pulled out a small device—brass and glass, with internal mechanisms that turned slowly—and was consulting it with increasing frequency. "It's accelerating. This shouldn't be possible."

"Define 'shouldn't,'" Ilyra said, her usual dry humor strained at the edges.

They crested a rise, and Rachel saw it.

The boundary collapse looked like someone had taken reality and shattered it. The air ahead didn't shimmer so much as fracture, broken edges catching light that came from no visible source. Through the fractures, Rachel could see other places bleeding through—a field of red grass under a green sun, a city made of black glass, an ocean that flowed upward into a sky that was somehow also ground.

Edrin's voice went thin. "Oh. That's... that's not good."

"Understatement," Maelin breathed.

The collapse was huge—maybe a quarter mile across—and growing. As Rachel watched, it pulsed outward, consuming another dozen yards of forest. The trees it touched didn't burn or fall. They just stopped making sense. Became mathematical instead of physical. Existed in too many places at once or not enough or both simultaneously.

"We need to backtrack," Arathen said. "Three days minimum. Take the Kirrel Pass like I should have insisted on in the first place—"

"We don't have three days," Rachel interrupted. She was still staring at the collapse, at the fractured spaces folding into and over each other. "It's spreading too fast. Look."

Even as she spoke, the boundary pulsed again. Another twenty yards consumed. Another section of forest reduced to impossible geometry.

"Then we run," Ilyra said. "Perpendicular to the expansion. Get out of its path—"

"It's not expanding in a line," Rachel said. "It's expanding in all directions. Like a sphere. We're already inside the danger zone."

Arathen's face had gone very pale. He looked at his device again, then at the collapse, then at Rachel. "How do you know that?"

Rachel couldn't explain it. She just knew. Could feel the shape of the collapse in her mind, could sense its boundaries—current and projected—like reading a map written in sensation instead of images.

"I just do," she said.

The collapse pulsed again. The cold intensified. Frost was forming on their packs now, on their clothes, crystallizing out of air that shouldn't be producing it.

"We have maybe an hour," Rachel said. "Maybe less. Before it reaches us."

"Then we need to move," Maelin said, already adjusting her pack. "Fast and perpendicular. Try to outrun the expansion—"

"You can't outrun it," Rachel said. The certainty settled in her chest, heavy and absolute. "It's accelerating. The more it consumes, the faster it grows. We'd need to run for hours at full sprint, through forest we don't know, and even then—" She shook her head. "We wouldn't make it."

Silence.

"So what do we do?" Edrin asked. His voice had gone thin.

Rachel looked at the collapse. At the fractures spreading through reality, spaces folding wrong, boundaries that should have been distinct bleeding into each other with catastrophic results. She could see the wrongness of it. Could feel how it violated the rules that kept worlds separate, kept realities intact.

Could feel, beneath the wrongness, what those rules should be.

Her voice came out steadier than expected. "I think I can fix it."

Four heads turned toward her in perfect synchronization.

"Rachel, no—" Maelin started.

"You can't fix a boundary collapse," Edrin said, voice rising slightly. "No one can. That's not—that's not even theoretically possible. The energy required would—"

"Kill a human, yes," Arathen interrupted. He was looking at Rachel with that intense focus again. "But Rachel isn't human. Is she?"

The question hung in the cold air between them.

"I don't know what I am," Rachel said. "But I know I can feel how it's broken. And I think—" She swallowed. "I think I know how to fix it."

"Think?" Ilyra said sharply. "That's not exactly confidence-inspiring."

"It's all I have," Rachel admitted. "But the alternative is we try to run and get caught by the collapse anyway. Or we backtrack and hope it stops expanding before it catches us, which—" She gestured at the fractured reality ahead. "Which doesn't seem likely."

Arathen studied her for a long moment. Calculating risks. Weighing impossibilities. "If you try this," he said finally, "there's no guarantee it won't kill you."

"I know."

"You could make it worse," Edrin added. "Add your power to the chaos and accelerate the collapse instead of stopping it."

"I know," Rachel said again.

"But you're going to try anyway," Maelin said. Not a question.

Rachel looked at her friends—because that's what they were now, she realized. Not kidnappers. Not strangers. Friends. People she would risk herself to protect. People she couldn't watch die if there was even the smallest chance she could prevent it.

"Yes," she said. "I'm going to try."

The collapse pulsed. Closer now. Maybe half a mile. The wrongness pressed against Rachel's awareness like pressure, like attention, like something aware of her in return.

"What do you need from us?" Arathen asked.

The question—not if she should try, but how they could help—made Rachel's throat tight.

"Stay back," she said. "Far back. If this goes wrong—"

"We're not leaving you," Maelin said immediately.

"I'm not asking you to leave," Rachel said. "Just... give me space. And if it starts to go bad, if I start to make it worse—" She looked at Arathen. "You'll know. You'll feel it. Pull me out. However you have to."

"Rachel—"

"Promise me," she said. "If I'm making it worse, you stop me. Even if—" She couldn't finish the sentence.

Arathen's expression was complicated. Concerned and determined and something else Rachel couldn't name. His voice was absolute. "I promise. But you're not going to make it worse. You're going to fix it."

"You're very certain," Rachel said, trying for levity and not quite managing it.

"I'm very certain about you," he replied.

The collapse pulsed again. The trees nearest to it were starting to dissolve, bark running like water, wood becoming something between solid and liquid and neither. The red grass from the other reality was bleeding through into this one, growing in patches that should have been impossible.

"Now or never," Ilyra said.

Rachel nodded. She handed her pack to Maelin, unnecessary weight she wouldn't need. Rolled her shoulders. Tried to quiet the fear racing through her veins.

She walked toward the collapse.

Behind her, she heard Edrin say something, heard Maelin's quiet response, heard the sound of them moving back to a safer distance. But her attention was already narrowing, focusing on the fractured space ahead.

On the boundaries that needed to be rebuilt.

On the impossible thing she was about to attempt.

Rachel stopped maybe fifty yards from the collapse's edge. Close enough to feel its pull, to sense the chaotic energy tearing reality apart. Far enough—she hoped—to not be immediately consumed if this went catastrophically wrong.

She closed her eyes.

Reached out with that sense that wasn't quite sight, wasn't quite touch, wasn't quite anything her old vocabulary had words for.

And felt the shape of the disaster unfolding before her.

It was worse than she'd thought. Not just a tear in one boundary but multiple boundaries collapsing simultaneously, realities from a dozen different worlds all trying to occupy the same space. Edges that should have been distinct had eroded, rules that kept worlds separate had failed, and now everything was bleeding together in a chaos that would only accelerate until there was nothing left but broken space and mathematical impossibility.

Rachel could see it all. Feel it all. The specific points where boundaries had failed. The cascading chain reaction as one failure led to another. The places where reality had simply given up trying to make sense and defaulted to chaos.

She could also feel—faint but present—the echo of what should be. The ghost of proper boundaries. The memory of order.

Rachel reached for it.

The warmth in her palms flared to life, spreading up her arms, through her chest, down into her core. But this time it didn't stop there. It kept building, kept growing, until her entire body felt like it was made of that heat, that power, that impossible force she'd been learning to channel.

It wasn't enough.

The collapse was too big, too chaotic, too far gone. Trying to fix it with the power she'd been using to stop rabbits and pause birds was like trying to bail out an ocean with a teaspoon.

But Rachel had more than that, didn't she? She'd been careful, controlled, cautious with her abilities because she was terrified of what might happen if she let go completely.

Now she didn't have that luxury.

Rachel stopped being careful.

The power that lived in her core—that had always lived there, waiting, dormant through twenty-six years of trying to be human—unfurled like wings she'd never known she had. It poured out of her in a wave that made the air shimmer, made the ground beneath her feet vibrate, made reality itself take notice.

And she reached for the boundaries.

Not gently. Not carefully. With authority.

With command.

The fractured edges responded.

Rachel felt them like threads, like individual strands in an impossible tapestry. She grabbed them—mentally, spiritually, with the part of her that belonged to spaces between worlds—and began to pull them back into order.

This world here. That world there. Boundaries distinct. Spaces separate. Rules enforced.

The collapse fought her.

It had momentum, chaos, the natural tendency of broken things to stay broken. It pushed back against her will with the force of physical law, with the weight of entropy itself.

Rachel pushed harder.

Heat became fire became something beyond fire. Her body was a conduit, a channel for power that had no business existing in physical form. Every cell vibrated with it. Every nerve sang.

It hurt.

Not the sharp pain of injury but the deep ache of using muscles that had atrophied, of pushing beyond limits that had been artificial all along. Rachel's knees wanted to buckle. Her breath came in gasps. Distantly, she thought she might be screaming.

But the boundaries were responding.

She felt them beginning to solidify, to separate, to remember what they should be. The red grass stopped bleeding through. The black glass city began to fade. The upward ocean retreated behind walls of reality that Rachel was rebuilding thread by thread, boundary by boundary, rule by painstaking rule.

The collapse pulsed again—but smaller this time. Weaker.

Rachel poured more of herself into the work. More power. More will. More of whatever she was at her core that made this possible.

The fractures began to seal.

Not quickly. Not easily. But definitively. Reality knitting itself back together under Rachel's hands, boundaries reforming, spaces separating into their proper configurations.

The cold began to ease. The frost on the grass started melting. The trees nearest the collapse stopped dissolving, their bark resolving back into something that made sense.

Rachel felt the moment the collapse stabilized. The momentum shift from expansion to contraction. The chaos beginning to organize itself back into order.

She gave one final push—not of power but of intention. A command impressed into the fabric of reality itself: Stay separate. Maintain boundaries. Remember order.

Then she let go.

The world snapped back into focus.

Rachel swayed, awareness returning to her body in a rush of sensation. Cold ground beneath her knees—when had she fallen? Sweat soaking her clothes despite the cold. Her hands shaking so badly she couldn't hold them still.

And silence.

The collapse had stopped.

Where there had been fractured chaos, now there was just forest. Normal forest. Trees with spiral bark and scale-ridge patterns. Grass that was blue-green and made sense. Air that tasted like air instead of like displacement.

The boundary had healed.

Rachel had fixed it.

Behind her, someone was shouting her name. Footsteps running. Hands on her shoulders.

"Rachel. Rachel, look at me."

Arathen's face swam into focus. He looked terrified and awed in equal measure.

"Did it work?" Rachel managed to ask. Her voice came out as a rasp.

"Did it—" Arathen's laugh was slightly unhinged. "Rachel, you just stabilized a multi-boundary collapse. You rewove reality itself. 'Work' doesn't begin to describe—" He cut himself off. "Are you hurt?"

"I don't think so," Rachel said. "Just... tired."

Tired was an understatement. She felt wrung out, hollowed, like every bit of energy had been poured out and what remained was just the echo of a person. But under the exhaustion was something else.

Satisfaction.

She'd done it. She'd fixed it. She'd saved them.

Maelin arrived, skidding to her knees beside Rachel. "That was the most terrifying thing I've ever watched."

"That was impossible," Edrin said. He was staring at Rachel like she'd sprouted wings. "That was—boundary mechanics don't even have theoretical frameworks for what you just did."

"I'm going to throw up," Rachel announced, and then did.

Maelin held her hair. Ilyra appeared with water. Arathen stayed close, one hand on her back, grounding.

When the nausea finally passed, Rachel sat back and tried to pretend her entire body wasn't shaking. "So," she said. "That was new."

"That was impossible," Edrin repeated. He sounded slightly in shock.

"Clearly not," Ilyra said. She was looking at Rachel with something that might have been respect or might have been fear. Possibly both.

Rachel managed to get to her feet with help, though her legs felt like water and her vision kept trying to swim sideways. Arathen kept a steadying hand on her elbow.

His tone shifted to practical. "We should move. Put distance between us and this area. Just in case."

"Just in case of what?" Rachel asked.

"Just in case whatever governing body monitors boundary collapses decides to investigate why this one suddenly repaired itself," he said. "Questions I'd rather not answer right now."

That made sense in a paranoid kind of way. Rachel nodded and immediately regretted the motion when her vision doubled.

They walked—Rachel leaning heavily on Maelin, her pack carried by Edrin—away from the healed boundary. Away from the evidence of what she'd done. The forest around them was blessedly, boringly normal. No fractures. No bleeding realities. Just trees and grass and the distant sound of bell-flowers resuming their gentle chiming.

After an hour, Arathen called a halt. "Here. We rest."

Rachel collapsed where she stood, too exhausted to care about comfort or strategy. Someone spread a blanket. Someone else pressed water into her hands. She drank without tasting it.

"Rachel," Arathen said, crouching beside her. His expression was serious. "What you did back there—"

"Was impossible, I know," Rachel interrupted. "Edrin mentioned it."

"Was incredible," Arathen corrected. "And terrifying. And absolutely, irrefutably proof that you're not human. No human could survive channeling that much power, let alone control it the way you did."

Rachel looked at her hands. They looked normal. Unremarkable. Nothing to suggest they'd just rewoven reality.

Her voice was flat with certainty. "I know. I know what I am."

Arathen's tone gentled. "Do you?"

"I'm a demon," Rachel said. The word felt strange in her mouth but not wrong. Not anymore. "A boundary walker who was born in the wrong world and spent twenty-six years trying to be something I'm not. You were right. You've been right all along."

The admission should have felt devastating.

Instead, it felt like relief.

"Yes," Arathen said simply. "And what you did today—saving us, fixing that collapse—that's what demons do. What boundary walkers do. You maintained order. Protected the separation between worlds. That's not evil or dangerous or wrong. That's your purpose."

"My purpose," Rachel echoed.

"If you choose it to be," he said.

Rachel closed her eyes. Purpose. She'd spent twenty-six years not knowing what hers was, feeling like a piece that didn't fit the puzzle she'd been given. And now—

Now she knew.

She belonged to the boundaries. To the spaces between. To the work of keeping realities separate and worlds intact.

And for the first time in her life, that felt exactly right.

"Okay," she said, opening her eyes. "Okay."

Arathen smiled. It was tired and relieved and genuinely warm. "Okay," he agreed.

Rachel let herself slump against Maelin's shoulder, exhaustion pulling at her like tide. Around them, the forest continued its normal rhythms. The moons traced their paths overhead. The bell-flowers chimed.

She'd fixed a boundary collapse.

She'd proven what she was.

And she'd survived.

For today, that was enough.



---

## Chapter Twenty-Two

Rachel slept for fourteen hours.

Not by choice. Her body simply shut down, consciousness retreating into darkness so complete she didn't dream, didn't stir, didn't register the world around her at all. When she finally surfaced, afternoon light was filtering through leaves overhead, dappled and green-gold, and someone had covered her with two blankets instead of one.

She lay still and took inventory. Her body ached—deep, bone-level exhaustion that came from pushing too hard, too far. But nothing felt broken. Nothing felt damaged beyond repair.

Just used. Thoroughly, completely used.

"She's awake," Ilyra's voice, from somewhere nearby.

Movement. Footsteps on grass. Then Maelin's face appeared above her, concerned and relieved in equal measure.

"How do you feel?" Maelin asked.

Rachel considered the question. "Like I got hit by a truck. Then the truck backed up and hit me again. For good measure."

"That's fair," Maelin said. "You did reweave reality. That probably counts as truck-equivalent."

Rachel managed a weak laugh. Her throat felt raw, like she'd been screaming. Maybe she had. The memory of the boundary collapse was already starting to blur at the edges, her mind protecting itself from the full weight of what she'd done.

Maelin helped her sit up slowly. The world swayed but held. Rachel's stomach protested but didn't rebel. Small victories.

The camp around her was more established than she remembered. Someone had clearly decided they'd be staying a while—proper fire pit, organized supplies, a perimeter that suggested Ilyra had spent time securing the area. The sun's angle suggested late afternoon, maybe early evening.

"How long was I asleep?" Rachel asked.

"Fourteen hours, give or take," Edrin said. He was sitting nearby with his journal open, pen in hand. "We took turns keeping watch. You were—" He paused, searching for words. "You were very deeply asleep. It was concerning."

"Recovery mode," Arathen said, approaching with a cup of something that smelled herbal and slightly bitter. "Your body needed time to process what you put it through. Here. Drink this."

Rachel accepted the cup. The liquid inside was warm and tasted like medicine—not unpleasant exactly, just definitively medicinal. "What is it?"

"Restorative tea," Arathen said. "Helps with magical exhaustion. Also tastes terrible, so you'll know it's working."

He wasn't wrong. Rachel drank it anyway, grateful for the warmth if nothing else.

"You scared us," Maelin said quietly. "When you collapsed, we thought—" She didn't finish the sentence.

"I'm sorry," Rachel said.

"Don't apologize for saving our lives," Ilyra said. She'd moved closer, arms folded, her usual guarded expression softened slightly. "That's not how that works."

"Still," Rachel said. "I scared myself too, for what it's worth."

"How much do you remember?" Arathen asked. He'd settled into what Rachel thought of as his scholar mode—focused, analytical, already cataloging data.

Rachel tried to pull the memory into focus. "Most of it, I think. The collapse. The fractures. Reaching for the boundaries." She paused. "It hurt. Not physically exactly, but—"

"But the effort of it," Arathen supplied. "Like using muscles you didn't know you had."

"Yes," Rachel said. "Exactly like that."

"And yet you did it anyway," Edrin said. He was watching her with a mix of awe and something that might have been fear. "You stabilized a collapse that should have been impossible to stop. Do you understand how significant that is?"

Rachel looked at him. "I understand we'd all be dead if I hadn't tried."

"It's more than that," Edrin insisted. He flipped back several pages in his journal, finding notes he'd made earlier. "I've been reading about boundary collapses for years. They're catastrophic. Unstoppable once they reach a certain threshold. The accepted response is evacuation and containment, not repair. Because repair isn't possible. Not by humans. Not by any magic we understand."

"But I'm not human," Rachel said quietly.

The words settled in the space between them. Not a question anymore. Not even really a revelation. Just a fact that everyone present had accepted.

"No," Arathen agreed. "You're not. And what you did yesterday—that was demon work. Boundary walker work. Maintaining the separation between realities, enforcing the rules that keep worlds intact. That's what your kind does. What you were made to do."

"Made," Rachel echoed. The word sat uncomfortably. "That makes it sound like I was manufactured. Like I'm a tool."

"Not manufactured," Arathen corrected gently. "Born. Into a purpose, yes, but that's not the same as being a tool. Humans are born with purposes too—we just call them talents or callings and pretend they're choices we made."

Rachel turned the cup in her hands, watching the liquid inside catch the light. It had a faint golden quality, not quite natural. More evidence that nothing in this world worked quite the way Earth's rules suggested it should.

"So I'm a demon," she said. "A boundary walker. Something that exists to maintain order between worlds."

"Something that can," Maelin said. "Not something that must. There's a difference."

"Is there?" Rachel asked. "Because that collapse yesterday—I didn't feel like I had a choice. I had to fix it. Had to try, even if it killed me. That doesn't feel like choice. That feels like compulsion."

"Or," Ilyra said, "it feels like being a decent person who doesn't want to watch their friends die when they have the power to prevent it."

Rachel looked at her. "Is that what you think it was?"

"I think you made a choice," Ilyra said. "A brave, stupid, necessary choice. Same as anyone would make in that situation if they had your abilities. Being a demon doesn't make that automatic. It just makes it possible."

The distinction mattered more than Rachel had expected. Not programmed to save them. Chose to save them. With abilities that happened to be demon abilities instead of human ones.

"Okay," Rachel said. "Okay."

She finished the tea—it didn't get less bitter with familiarity—and handed the cup back to Arathen. Her body was already starting to feel marginally less wrecked. Whatever was in that tea, it worked.

"What happens now?" she asked.

"Now," Arathen said, "we continue to Thornhaven. To Sivara. She'll have answers we don't. Context. History." He paused. "And she'll recognize you for what you are immediately. No more speculation. Just truth."

Rachel's stomach tightened. "What if the truth is worse than the speculation?"

"What if it's better?" Maelin countered.

"What if it just is?" Edrin added. "Neither better nor worse. Just... information."

Information. Rachel could work with information. She'd spent her career managing information, organizing it, making sense of chaos. This was just a different kind of chaos.

A more personal kind.

"When can we leave?" she asked.

"Tomorrow morning," Arathen said. "You need more rest. One night's sleep, even fourteen hours of it, isn't enough after what you did."

Rachel wanted to argue. Wanted to insist she was fine, that they should keep moving, that every day they delayed was another day of not knowing. But her body betrayed her—exhaustion pulling at her limbs, making even sitting upright feel like work.

"Tomorrow," she agreed.

The rest of the day passed in strange limbo. Rachel dozed intermittently, never quite sleeping but never fully awake either. The others moved through camp routines around her—Ilyra scouting, Edrin making notes, Maelin preparing food that smelled better than Rachel's stomach was ready for.

Arathen stayed close. Not hovering, exactly. Just present. Reading from a small book, consulting that brass-and-glass device occasionally, keeping watch in his particular way.

As the afternoon dimmed toward evening, he set his book aside and moved to sit near Rachel's bedroll.

"Question," he said.

Rachel shifted to face him. "Answer. Maybe."

His mouth quirked. "When you were fixing the collapse—did you feel anything unusual? Beyond the obvious strain, I mean."

Rachel thought about it. "The boundaries responded to me," she said slowly. "Like they recognized me. Like they were waiting for someone with authority to tell them what to do." She paused. "It didn't feel like I was forcing them. It felt like I was reminding them. Of what they should be."

Arathen nodded, like she'd confirmed something. "That's what I thought. You weren't imposing your will on reality. You were helping reality remember its own rules."

"Is there a difference?"

"A significant one," he said. "Imposing will takes constant energy. It's a battle you have to keep fighting. Helping reality remember itself—that takes initial effort, but once the pattern's reestablished, it holds on its own. That's why you were able to fix such a massive collapse without killing yourself. You weren't holding it together with raw power. You were teaching it how to hold itself together."

The explanation made sense in a way that felt fundamentally right. Rachel hadn't felt like she was wrestling the collapse into submission. She'd felt like she was showing it the correct pattern, and once the boundaries understood, they'd snapped into place on their own.

"So I'm not just powerful," she said. "I'm... what? A teacher? A guide?"

"A walker," Arathen said. "Someone who knows the paths between worlds and can show others—or show reality itself—how to navigate them correctly."

Walker. The word resonated in a way that made Rachel's chest tight. Not a destroyer. Not a force of chaos. A guide through spaces that needed guiding.

"I think I can live with that," she said quietly.

"Good," Arathen said. "Because you don't really have a choice. That's what you are."

"Demon," Rachel said, testing the word again.

"Boundary walker," Arathen corrected. "Demon is just the common term. It doesn't capture the nuance."

"What nuance?"

He considered his answer. "Demons are beings who exist outside normal reality's constraints. But within that category, there are types. Boundary walkers maintain the spaces between worlds. Shadow demons work with darkness and absence. Flame demons embody transformation and consumption. Water demons flow between states. You're not just 'a demon.' You're specifically a boundary walker. Someone whose purpose is order and separation and the maintenance of cosmic rules."

"That's a lot of purpose for one person," Rachel said.

"It's a lot of purpose for one demon," Arathen agreed. "For a human, it would be impossible. But you're not human. You never were."

Rachel looked at her hands again. They still looked human. Ordinary. Nothing about her physical form suggested demon anything.

"Do I look human to you?" she asked. "Physically?"

Arathen studied her. "Mostly. But there are tells, if you know what to look for. The way light catches in your eyes sometimes. The fact that you haven't gotten sick once, haven't gotten seriously injured despite everything we've put you through. The way temperature doesn't seem to bother you as much as it should—you were working in freezing air yesterday and barely shivering."

Rachel hadn't noticed that. But he was right. The cold had registered, but it hadn't affected her the way it clearly had the others.

"So I look human but don't quite act human," she said.

"You look like a demon who was raised human," Arathen said. "Which is exactly what you are."

The truth of it settled over Rachel like a weight. Not oppressive. Just present. Undeniable.

"I'm never going back, am I?" she asked quietly. "To my world. Even if we figure out how, even if it's possible—I can't go back to being human. To pretending to be human."

"No," Arathen said gently. "You can't. But Rachel—" He waited until she looked at him. "Did you ever really fit there? Were you happy?"

Rachel thought about her apartment. Her job. Her relationship with Evan. The carefully constructed life she'd built around the hollow feeling that nothing ever quite made sense.

"No," she admitted. "I wasn't happy. I was functional. I was fine. But happy?" She shook her head. "I don't think I knew what that felt like."

"And now?" Arathen asked.

Rachel looked around the camp. At Maelin tending the fire, Edrin absorbed in his notes, Ilyra returning from another perimeter check. At Arathen beside her, patient and present and accepting of exactly what she was.

"Now I'm terrified," she said honestly. "And exhausted. And confused about what comes next. But underneath all of that—" She paused, finding the word. "I feel real. Like I'm actually living instead of performing. Even when I'm scared, even when I'm pushing myself too hard, it feels more honest than anything I did in my old life."

"That's because you're being yourself," Arathen said. "Finally. After twenty-six years of being what you thought you should be."

Rachel let that sink in. Being herself. Her actual self. Not the version she'd crafted to fit human expectations, but the demon she'd been all along without knowing it.

"It's going to take time," she said. "To figure out what that means. Who I am when I'm not trying to be someone else."

"You have time," Arathen said. "We'll make sure of it."

The promise in his voice made Rachel's chest tighten. Not just him—all of them. Her found family who'd seen her at her most impossible and hadn't run. Who'd watched her reweave reality and then covered her with blankets and let her sleep.

"Thank you," she said. "For not being afraid of me."

"We are afraid of you," Arathen said with a slight smile. "We're just more committed to you than we are afraid. There's a difference."

Rachel laughed despite herself. "That's almost romantic."

"It's practical," he corrected, but something in his expression suggested he didn't entirely disagree with her assessment.

The evening deepened into night. The two moons rose—large silver, small blue—casting their overlapping shadows. The bell-flowers in the surrounding forest began their gentle chiming. Bioluminescent moss lit up in patterns that might have been random or might have been language Rachel didn't yet speak.

Rachel ate what she could of dinner, drank more of that bitter medicinal tea, and let herself be cared for by people who knew what she was and chose to stay anyway.

Tomorrow they'd continue toward Thornhaven.

Toward Sivara.

Toward final answers about what Rachel was and what that meant.

But tonight, she let herself rest. Let herself exist in this liminal space between knowing and not knowing, between human and demon, between her old life and whatever came next.

And for the first time since the summoning, she felt something like peace.



---

## Chapter Twenty-Three

They left at dawn, moving through forest that had changed again. The trees here had bark that looked almost like birch—white with dark markings—but the pattern wasn't random. Up close, Rachel could see deliberate designs, almost like writing, carved into the surface by something other than human hands. The leaves were a pale silver-green, catching morning light and reflecting it back in ways that made the whole forest shimmer.

Rachel felt better. Not fully recovered—her body still carried the deep ache of over-exertion—but functional. Capable of walking, of carrying her pack, of existing in her own skin without every movement feeling like punishment.

The medicinal tea helped. So did fourteen hours of sleep. But mostly, Rachel suspected, it was acceptance. Knowing what she was, what she'd done, what she could do. The weight of

 it was still there, but it sat differently now. Less like burden, more like responsibility.

They walked in comfortable silence, the kind that came from people who'd been through something together and didn't need to fill every moment with words. Edrin led, consulting the map periodically. Ilyra ranged ahead and to the sides, scouting by habit. Maelin stayed close to Rachel, her presence quietly supportive. Arathen brought up the rear, attention divided between their back trail and Rachel herself.

He'd been watching her all morning. Not obviously. But Rachel could feel it—awareness, assessment, concern masked as casual observation.

Finally, as they paused beside a stream for water, he pulled her aside.

His voice dropped low enough the others wouldn't hear. "We need to talk. About what comes next."

Rachel nodded. She'd been expecting this conversation. "Okay."

"What you did at the boundary collapse—" Arathen paused, choosing words carefully. "That's going to have consequences. Someone will have noticed. Collapses that size don't just stop. They certainly don't repair themselves."

"You think someone's looking for me," Rachel said.

"I think someone's going to be very interested in what fixed that collapse," Arathen said. "And while the Thornhaven region is relatively independent, it's not invisible. Questions will be asked."

"What kind of questions?"

"The kind that lead to attention you don't want," Arathen said. "Scholars who want to study you. Officials who want to understand the threat level. Other demons who might see you as competition or opportunity."

Rachel's stomach tightened. She'd been so focused on surviving the immediate crisis that she hadn't thought about longer-term implications. "What do we do?"

"We get to Sivara quickly," Arathen said. "She has influence in Thornhaven. Protection. And she'll know how to navigate this better than I do. Demon politics aren't my area of expertise."

"Demon politics," Rachel repeated. "That's a thing?"

"Every group has politics," Arathen said. "Demons are no exception. And a boundary walker powerful enough to stabilize a major collapse—" He shook his head. "You're going to be significant whether you want to be or not."

The thought made Rachel want to crawl out of her own skin. She'd spent twenty-six years trying to be invisible, to not take up too much space, to be small and manageable. And now she was significant. Powerful. Impossible to ignore.

"I don't want to be significant," she said. "I just want to understand what I am. Figure out how to exist without breaking things."

Arathen's expression gentled. "I know. But what you want and what you are don't always align. That's something you'll need to make peace with."

Rachel looked at the stream. The water was clearer than Earth water should be, almost crystalline, with a faint mineral taste that was pleasant despite being unfamiliar. Small fish—if they were fish—darted through the shallows. They had too many fins and eyes that reflected like mirrors, but they moved with the same fluid grace fish should have.

"I'm scared," she admitted quietly.

"Good," Arathen said. "You should be. But Rachel—" He waited until she looked at him. "You're not alone in this. Whatever comes next, you have us. You have me."

The emphasis on that last part made something in Rachel's chest tighten. Us. Me. The distinction felt significant even if she wasn't ready to examine why.

"Thank you," she said.

"Don't thank me," he replied. "Just trust that we're not going to let you face this alone."

They rejoined the others, who had spread out along the stream bank to refill water skins and eat quick rations. Maelin caught Rachel's eye and raised an eyebrow—a silent question. Rachel nodded slightly. I'm okay. As okay as I can be.

Maelin seemed satisfied with that.

They continued walking. The forest shifted again—the birch-bark trees giving way to something darker, with deep purple wood and leaves that were almost black. The undergrowth here had a wild quality, plants growing in patterns that suggested intelligence more than randomness. Vines spiraled up trunks with mathematical precision. Flowers opened and closed in synchronized waves.

"We're entering someone's territory," Ilyra said, voice low. She'd stopped ahead, hand resting on her knife hilt.

"Whose?" Edrin asked.

"Not sure yet," Ilyra said. "But the forest has been shaped. Deliberately. Someone lives here and has been... gardening."

As if in response, something moved in the canopy above. Rachel caught a flash of movement—too large to be a bird, too deliberate to be falling. She tensed, power rising instinctively to her fingertips.

Arathen's hand found her arm. "Easy. Let's not start a conflict we don't need."

The movement resolved into a figure dropping from the branches with impossible grace. They—Rachel couldn't determine gender immediately—landed without sound, rising to full height with fluid precision.

Not human. Definitely not human.

The figure was tall, maybe seven feet, with skin that had an iridescent quality like oil on water. Their features were sharp, angular in ways that suggested beauty but not human beauty. Eyes too large, too dark, pupils that might have been slitted if Rachel looked directly but seemed normal when she didn't.

Demon. Rachel knew it instinctively. Recognized it the way you recognized your own language being spoken.

"You're far from the usual paths," the demon said. Their voice had a quality Rachel couldn't describe—layered, like multiple pitches speaking simultaneously. "And you—" Those too-large eyes fixed on Rachel. "You're recent."

"Recent?" Rachel asked.

"Newly awakened," the demon clarified. "I can smell the fresh magic on you. The boundary work. Very impressive." It wasn't clear if they meant that as compliment or threat.

Arathen stepped forward slightly, positioning himself between Rachel and the newcomer without making it obvious. "We're traveling to Thornhaven. We mean no disrespect to your territory."

"Thornhaven," the demon repeated, attention still fixed on Rachel. "To see Sivara, I'd wager. Another boundary walker seeking answers." They tilted their head, bird-like. "Though usually they don't arrive having already stabilized a major collapse. That's unusual."

"You felt it," Rachel said.

"Everyone felt it," the demon replied. "A collapse that size doesn't stop itself. And then suddenly—" They snapped their fingers, the sound sharp as a gunshot. "Stability. Order reimposed. Very dramatic. Very noticeable." A pause. "Very you, I'm guessing."

Rachel didn't know how to answer that. Yes felt like admitting too much. No felt like lying.

"It was necessary," she said finally.

The demon's mouth curved—not quite a smile, but similar. "Necessary. How pragmatic." They looked at Arathen. "She always this direct?"

"Usually." Arathen's tone stayed neutral.

"I like it," the demon decided. "Most boundary walkers are all mystique and ceremony. Tedious." They gestured ahead. "You're two days from Thornhaven at your pace. The paths are safe enough. Relatively. Don't stray from the marked routes and don't accept invitations from the pale folk. They're terrible hosts."

"Pale folk?" Edrin asked, then looked like he regretted speaking.

"Forest spirits," the demon said dismissively. "Harmless until they're not. Just decline politely and keep moving." They looked back at Rachel. "Sivara will be interested in you. Very interested. A new walker with your level of power? That's rare."

"Is that a problem?" Rachel asked.

The demon's laugh was like wind chimes made of bone. "Problem? No. Complicated? Absolutely. But that's not my concern." They stepped back, already beginning to fade into the shadows despite it being midday. "Travel safely. And Walker—" They paused. "Learn quickly. Power without knowledge is dangerous. Especially yours."

Then they were gone. Not walking away. Just... gone. Like they'd been made of shadow and had simply stopped bothering to hold shape.

The silence that followed was heavy.

"Well," Ilyra said finally. "That was unsettling."

"That was a demon," Edrin said. He'd gone slightly pale. "An actual demon. I've read about them but never—"

"That was a shadow demon," Arathen corrected. "Specifically. They work with absence and darkness. And they were being relatively friendly, all things considered."

"That was friendly?" Maelin asked.

"They didn't attack us or trap us in riddles," Arathen said. "So yes. Friendly."

Rachel was still staring at the place where the shadow demon had vanished. Her voice came out hollow. "They knew. They looked at me and knew immediately what I was."

"Yes," Arathen said. "Demons recognize each other. You'll need to get used to that."

"And they said I was powerful," Rachel continued. "Unusually powerful. Is that true?"

Arathen exchanged a glance with Edrin. "Based on what you did at the collapse? Yes. That level of boundary work isn't common even among experienced walkers."

"So I'm not just a demon," Rachel said. "I'm a powerful demon. Which means more attention. More complications."

"Yes," Arathen said simply.

Rachel closed her eyes briefly. Of course. Of course she couldn't just be a normal demon with normal abilities living a normal demon life. She had to be powerful and noticeable and significant.

"I hate this," she said.

"I know," Maelin said, moving close. "But hating it doesn't make it less true."

"Doesn't make it easier either," Rachel muttered.

They continued walking, the forest growing darker as those purple-wood trees became denser. The path was still clear though—marked with stones that had been deliberately placed, white against dark earth. The shadow demon's directions, Rachel assumed.

Don't stray from marked routes. Don't accept invitations from pale folk.

Wonderful. More rules. More things to remember. More ways to die or get trapped or make terrible mistakes.

Rachel was so tired of being out of her depth.

They made camp that evening in a clearing that felt carefully neutral—not claimed by anyone, not dangerous, just a space that existed between territories. Arathen confirmed it with that brass-and-glass device, which apparently detected magical boundaries as well as physical ones.

Dinner was quiet. Everyone processing the day's encounter in their own way. Edrin kept making notes. Ilyra sharpened her knives with more force than necessary. Maelin organized and reorganized supplies, finding comfort in order.

Rachel sat and stared at the fire, watching wood burn in colors that still didn't match Earth fire. Green sparks. Blue undertones in the flames. Smoke that smelled medicinal.

"You're thinking too loud," Ilyra said from across the fire.

"Sorry," Rachel said automatically.

"Don't apologize," Ilyra said. "Just talk. Whatever it is."

Rachel struggled to find words. "I keep thinking about what that shadow demon said. About learning quickly. About power without knowledge being dangerous."

"They were right," Arathen said. "You have significant abilities but no training. No context. That's why we're going to Sivara. She can teach you what you need to know."

"And if she can't?" Rachel asked. "Or won't?"

"Then we figure it out ourselves," Maelin said firmly. "We've gotten this far. We'll keep going."

"You make it sound simple," Rachel said.

"It's not simple," Maelin agreed. "But it's possible. There's a difference."

Rachel looked at her friends gathered around the fire. Edrin with his journal and his endless curiosity. Ilyra with her knives and her pragmatic wisdom. Maelin with her steady presence and quiet strength. Arathen with his knowledge and his careful concern.

They'd chosen her. Chosen to help her, to travel with her, to face unknown dangers on her behalf. Not because they had to. Because they wanted to.

Because somehow, impossibly, she'd become someone worth choosing.

"Thank you," Rachel said. "I know I keep saying that, but I mean it. Thank you for not giving up on me."

"We're not going to," Edrin said. "Give up, I mean. You're stuck with us now."

"Stuck is right," Ilyra added with a faint smile. "We've invested too much effort in keeping you alive. Can't quit now."

Despite everything—the fear, the exhaustion, the overwhelming uncertainty—Rachel found herself smiling.

"Okay," she said. "Okay."

The moment shattered when Ilyra stood abruptly, hand going to her knife. "We have company."

Rachel felt it a heartbeat later—that sensation of being noticed, of boundaries shifting as something powerful moved through them. From the tree line, a figure emerged.

This demon was different from the shadow walker they'd met earlier. Taller, with skin that had an opalescent quality like mother-of-pearl, and eyes that were solid white without pupils. They wore robes that seemed to shift color with each movement, never quite settling on one hue.

"Peace," the demon said, hands raised to show they held no weapons. Their voice had a resonant quality that suggested multiple tones speaking in harmony. "I mean no harm. I'm simply... curious."

"About?" Arathen asked carefully, positioning himself slightly in front of Rachel.

"About the boundary collapse two days north of here," the demon said. Their white eyes fixed on Rachel with uncomfortable precision. "Three-layer failure. Catastrophic expansion. Should have consumed everything within ten miles." They paused. "And then it stopped. Repaired itself. Impossible, except it happened."

"We heard about that," Arathen said neutrally. "Boundary work."

"Boundary work requires walkers," the demon said. "Experienced walkers. Teams of them, coordinated, with Council authorization." Those white eyes hadn't left Rachel. "Yet there were no teams dispatched. No authorization granted. Just a collapse that suddenly... wasn't."

"Lucky," Ilyra said flatly.

"Convenient," the demon corrected. They took a step closer. Rachel felt power radiating from them—not threatening exactly, but present. Undeniable. "I felt it from fifty miles away. Felt the moment the chaos shifted to order. Felt the signature of whoever wielded that much power." Another step. "And I followed that signature here. To her."

"I don't know what you're talking about," Rachel said, trying to keep her voice steady.

"Don't you?" The demon tilted their head. "You reek of boundary work. Fresh work. Massive expenditure. The kind that leaves marks for days." They smiled, though it didn't reach those white eyes. "You're the one who fixed it. A new walker. Untrained. Impossibly powerful."

Silence stretched taut as wire.

"What do you want?" Arathen asked quietly.

"Nothing," the demon said, surprising Rachel. "I'm not Council. Not enforcement. Just a scholar who felt something unprecedented and wanted to confirm what I suspected." They looked at Rachel with something that might have been respect or might have been concern. "Word will spread, little walker. What you did was too large to hide. Others will come asking questions. Some friendly. Some not."

"Are you threatening her?" Maelin asked, voice hard.

"Warning her," the demon corrected. "There are demons who will want to know who you are, where you came from, why you're so powerful without training. Some will offer help. Some will offer problems." They stepped back, already beginning to fade into shadows. "Get to Thornhaven quickly. Find Sivara. She can protect you from the worst of the attention."

"How do you know we're going to Sivara?" Edrin demanded.

"Where else would an untrained walker go?" the demon said. "She's the best teacher within three weeks' travel. Of course you're going to her." They were almost gone now, form blurring. "Move fast. You have maybe a week before the wrong people start looking."

Then they were gone, leaving only disturbed air and the faint scent of ozone.

Rachel's legs wouldn't hold her. She sat down hard where she'd been standing.

Ilyra crouched beside her. "That was bad."

"That was a problem," Arathen agreed. He looked at Rachel. "They weren't hostile, but they were right. What you did was too visible. We need to reach Thornhaven faster than I'd planned."

"How much faster?" Maelin asked.

"Tomorrow we push hard," Arathen said. "Cover two days' travel in one if we can manage it. I want Rachel behind Sivara's wards before anyone else comes looking."

That night, as the two moons rose and the forest settled into its bioluminescent patterns, Rachel lay awake and thought about power. About knowledge. About becoming something she'd never been prepared to be.

And about the people who'd decided to walk that path with her, even knowing where it led.

Two days to Thornhaven.

Two days to answers.

Two days to find out what came next.

Rachel closed her eyes and tried to believe it would be enough.



---

## Chapter Twenty-Four

Rachel dreamed of her old apartment.

Not the summoning—for once, not that. Just her apartment on a random Tuesday afternoon, light coming through windows that faced the wrong direction, traffic sounds muffled by double-pane glass that actually worked the way glass should. Her laptop open on the kitchen table. Coffee in a mug that said something supposedly inspirational that she'd always found vaguely condescending.

Normal. Aggressively, mundanely normal.

In the dream, she was packing. Moving boxes sat half-filled around the living room. She was folding clothes—the same clothes she'd been wearing when the summoning happened, which her dream brain found extremely significant though she couldn't articulate why.

Someone knocked on the door.

Rachel knew, with dream logic, that it was Evan. That he was there to help her move, or to talk her out of moving, or maybe just to be there because that's what partners did. She walked to the door. Put her hand on the knob.

Couldn't remember what he looked like.

The realization jolted her awake.

Rachel lay in her bedroll staring at unfamiliar stars, heart pounding, the dream already fragmenting but that one detail sharp and terrible: she couldn't remember Evan's face.

She tried now, fully awake. Tried to picture him clearly. His eyes had been... what color? Blue? No, brown. Maybe hazel? He'd had hair that did that thing where it flipped up in back no matter what product he used. Or was that someone else?

The specifics wouldn't come. She could remember facts—physical therapist, kind, conflict-averse, reliably present but rarely exciting. She could remember feelings—comfortable, safe, slightly suffocating. She could remember the shape of their relationship.

But his face. The specific arrangement of features that made him Evan instead of anyone else. That was gone.

Rachel's chest constricted. She'd been with him for two years. Two years of shared meals and shared space and shared life. And now, less than two weeks after leaving—after being torn away—she couldn't picture him clearly.

What did that say about her?

What did that say about them?

She sat up carefully, trying not to disturb Maelin sleeping nearby. The camp was quiet. Both moons hung low on the horizon, their light creating long shadows through the purple-wood trees. The bioluminescent patterns in the moss pulsed slowly, a heartbeat rhythm that Rachel's own pulse kept trying to match.

Arathen was on watch, sitting at the camp's edge with that same patient awareness he always carried. He noticed Rachel moving and turned slightly, a silent question.

Rachel stood and walked over to him, blanket wrapped around her shoulders against the night chill. He made space beside him without needing to be asked.

They sat in silence for a while. Comfortable silence, the kind that didn't demand filling.

Finally, Rachel spoke. "I can't remember what he looked like."

Arathen didn't need clarification about who "he" was. "Evan," he said quietly.

"Yes." Rachel pulled the blanket tighter. "I was just dreaming about him—about my old life—and I realized I couldn't see his face clearly anymore. I know facts about him. I know how I felt. But the actual him—" She shook her head. "It's fading."

"That's normal," Arathen said gently.

"Is it?" Rachel asked. "Normal for who? Humans? Demons? People who get kidnapped into other dimensions?"

"All of them," Arathen said. "Memory works differently when you're not reinforcing it. When you're not seeing someone daily, not actively maintaining that connection. Two weeks is long enough for details to start blurring, especially when you've been through the kind of trauma and change you have."

"It feels wrong," Rachel said. "Like I'm betraying him by forgetting. He's probably looking for me. Probably terrified about what happened. And I'm here, in another world, forgetting what his face looks like."

"You're surviving," Arathen said. "That's not betrayal. That's necessary."

Rachel looked at her hands. They looked the same as they always had, but she knew now they weren't. That they'd never been human hands, just hands that looked human because she'd been born in a human world and had adapted accordingly.

"I think I'm letting him go," she said quietly. "And I don't know if that's healthy or terrible or just practical."

"Why can't it be all three?" Arathen asked.

Rachel considered that. "I guess it can be."

"Do you want to remember him?" Arathen asked. "Clearly, I mean. We could work on it. Reconstruct details. I have techniques for memory preservation."

Rachel opened her mouth to say yes. Of course yes. She'd loved Evan—or thought she had, or had tried to, or whatever approximation of love she'd been capable of while pretending to be human. She owed him at least the courtesy of remembering his face.

But the word that came out was: "No."

Arathen didn't react with surprise. Just nodded slowly, like she'd confirmed something he'd suspected.

"No," Rachel repeated, testing the word. "I don't want to work to remember him. Is that awful?"

"It's honest," Arathen said. "And honesty is rarely awful, even when it's uncomfortable."

Rachel pulled her knees up, resting her chin on them. "I think I'm realizing I didn't love him the way I thought I did. Or the way I was supposed to. He was... convenient. Safe. Proof that I could have a normal relationship like normal people do." She paused. "But I wasn't normal. And trying to be normal made everything feel hollow."

"Including him," Arathen said.

"Including him," Rachel agreed. "Which isn't fair to him. He deserved someone who loved him fully, not someone who was using him as evidence of normalcy."

"You didn't know you were doing that," Arathen pointed out.

"Does that make it better?" Rachel asked.

"It makes it understandable," he said. "You can't be blamed for trying to fit into the only world you knew. You were doing your best with the information you had."

Rachel thought about Evan. About his earnest attempts to understand her, to connect with her, to build a life together. About her equally earnest attempts to meet him halfway while never quite feeling like she was succeeding.

"He deserves to know I'm not coming back," she said. "Even if I could find a way, even if it were possible—I'm not going back to that life. To pretending. He deserves someone who isn't pretending."

"Yes," Arathen said simply.

They sat with that truth. Rachel waited for guilt, for grief, for the emotional devastation that should come with realizing you're letting go of someone who'd been central to your life for two years.

What she felt instead was relief.

And beneath that: freedom.

"I'm a terrible person," she said.

"You're not," Arathen said firmly. "You're someone who's finally being honest with herself. That's brave, not terrible."

"It doesn't feel brave," Rachel said. "It feels like I'm abandoning him. Like I'm taking the easy way out."

"There's nothing easy about what you're doing," Arathen said. "Nothing easy about any of this. You're just making the choice that's true instead of the choice that looks right from the outside."

Rachel turned that over in her mind. True versus right. They weren't the same thing, were they? Right was what you were supposed to do. True was what actually aligned with reality.

"I wish I could tell him," she said. "That I'm okay. That this isn't his fault. That he should move on and find someone who can love him the way he deserves."

"Maybe you can," Arathen said. "Someday. When we have more answers about how boundaries work, about what's possible. But Rachel—" He waited until she looked at him. "You don't owe him your life. You don't owe anyone that. Not even out of guilt."

"I know," Rachel said. "Intellectually, I know. But the feeling—"

"Will take time to catch up," Arathen finished. "That's normal too."

They sat in silence again. Above them, the smaller blue moon had set, leaving only the large silver one to light the forest. The shadows shifted, became deeper. Somewhere in the distance, one of those four-winged birds called—a sound that was becoming familiar now, almost comforting in its strangeness.

"Can I ask you something?" Rachel said.

"Always," Arathen replied.

"If I had stayed human—if I'd somehow actually been human instead of a demon who didn't know it—would you have looked at me the same way?"

The question hung between them. Rachel wasn't entirely sure what she was asking. Wasn't entirely sure she wanted the answer.

Arathen was quiet for a long moment. "I don't know," he said finally. "Because I never saw you as human. From the first moment, from before I even met you—Edrin's message said you were manifesting abilities you shouldn't have. I came expecting someone extraordinary. And you were."

"Are," Rachel corrected quietly. "I am."

"Yes," Arathen agreed. "You are."

Something in his tone made Rachel's chest tighten. Not romantic exactly—or not only romantic. Just... significant. Weighty. The kind of moment that would look different in retrospect than it felt in real-time.

"I should try to sleep," Rachel said, though she didn't move.

"You should," Arathen agreed, though he didn't suggest she leave.

Rachel looked at the forest, at the unfamiliar trees and the impossible light and the creatures that moved in shadows cast by moons that weren't her moons. Looked at the life she'd stumbled into, been dragged into, been summoned into without choice or preparation.

Looked at the person beside her who'd seen her at her most impossible and had chosen understanding over fear.

"Arathen?" she said.

"Mm?"

"The resonance effect," she said. "The one you warned me about. The timer that meant the longer I stayed here, the harder it would be to return." She paused. "I can feel it. It's not pulling anymore. It's settled. Like this world has decided I belong here and stopped trying to integrate me."

"Because you are a demon," Arathen said quietly. "Boundary walkers don't need to integrate. We're already part of the spaces between. The resonance wasn't trying to trap you. It was trying to wake you up."

"And now I'm awake," Rachel said.

"Now you're awake," he agreed. "Which means the timer is gone. You could try to return, theoretically, though I don't know if it's possible. But the pressure to stay, the resonance pulling you deeper—that's done. You're stabilized."

The information should have brought relief. Instead it just confirmed what Rachel already knew in her bones.

"Thank you. For not making this easier than it is. For not pretending I should feel something I don't."

"You're welcome," he said simply.

Rachel stood, blanket still wrapped around her shoulders. Arathen's hand brushed hers as she turned—so briefly it might have been accident—and then she was walking back to her bedroll, back to the space between waking and sleeping where Evan's face had faded and her real life had finally begun.

She lay down. Closed her eyes. Tried to summon guilt or grief or any of the emotions that should accompany letting someone go.

Found only relief.

Only freedom.

Only the truth that she'd been alone in that relationship from the beginning, and leaving it—even accidentally, even unwillingly—was the kindest thing she could have done for both of them.

Rachel's last thought before sleep was this: She'd spent twenty-six years building a life that fit wrong.

Now she was building one that fit right.

And if that meant letting go of everything she'd thought she was, everyone she'd thought she loved, every future she'd imagined—

Well.

Some things were worth letting go of.

Some things were necessary to release before you could hold onto what actually mattered.

Rachel slept.

The nightmare came swiftly.

Not Evan this time. Not her apartment. This was different—darker, more invasive. She stood in boundary space, the layered reality of the Sethril crossing, but wrong. Corrupted. Something moved through the layers toward her, hunting.

A presence. Powerful. Ancient. Hungry.

It reached for her through the dream, trying to locate her physical form, map her location through the connection between sleeping mind and waking world. Boundary magic turned weapon.

Rachel felt it touch her awareness and recoiled.

"Found you," a voice said. Not spoken. Impressed directly into her mind. Vex. She recognized his power signature from the watcher weeks ago.

He was trying to track her through dreams.

Rachel's instincts flared. In the dream, her power responded—boundaries snapping shut around her consciousness, creating shields that shouldn't be possible in sleep. She pushed back against Vex's intrusion, forcing him out of her dream space.

"Interesting," Vex's voice echoed as he withdrew. "You're stronger than expected. Even asleep, you defend. We'll meet soon, little heir. And when we do—"

Rachel woke gasping.

Her power was crackling along her boundaries, visible to anyone looking—blue-white light flickering across her hands. Maelin was awake instantly, alarmed.

"Rachel? What's wrong?"

Rachel tried to calm her breathing, to pull her power back under control. "Someone tried to track me. Through my dream. Demon nightmare magic."

Arathen was there in seconds, still on watch. "Describe it."

Rachel told him. About the dream, the presence, the attempt to locate her physically through the sleeping connection.

"That's advanced technique," Arathen said grimly. "And illegal in most territories. Dream invasion requires the target to be vulnerable, unguarded." He looked at Rachel with mixed concern and pride. "You defended yourself. While unconscious. That's remarkable."

"That's terrifying," Rachel corrected. "I was asleep and still had to fight. When am I supposed to be safe?"

"Not until we reach Thornhaven," Ilyra said. She'd woken too, knife already in hand. "Whoever's tracking you—they're escalating. First observation, then boundary creatures as distraction, now active dream assault. They're testing your defenses, learning your capabilities."

"And confirming your location," Edrin added quietly. He'd joined them, looking worried. "Dream tracking has limited range. Whoever did this knows roughly where we are."

Maelin moved closer to Rachel. "Did you recognize them?"

Rachel nodded slowly. "The power signature. It felt like... the watcher. The one who's been following us. But stronger. Like he was showing me what he's really capable of."

"Vex," Arathen said with certainty. "He's done being subtle. He wants you to know he's coming."

Rachel pulled her blanket tighter despite the crackling power still warming her hands. "How much farther to Thornhaven?"

"Two days," Arathen said. "Maybe three. Depends on whether we're tracked again, whether they try another assault."

"They will," Ilyra said flatly. "They're committed now."

Rachel looked around at her found family—all awake now, all alert, all ready to protect her from threats that came even in sleep.

"I'm sorry," she said. "For bringing this down on you. For—"

"Stop," Maelin said firmly. "We chose to come with you. We choose to stay. That's not your fault."

"Get some rest," Arathen said gently. "I'll keep watch. If anything tries to reach you again through dreams, I'll wake you immediately."

Rachel lay back down, but sleep felt dangerous now. Like giving Vex another opening. Another chance to find her.

But exhaustion won eventually.

And for the first time since the summoning, she didn't dream of home.

She dreamed of nothing at all.

And that, at least, felt safe.



---

## Chapter Twenty-Five

Morning came with birdsong that was almost right—four-winged creatures calling to each other in patterns that were starting to sound like music instead of noise. Rachel woke feeling lighter than she had in days, which was strange considering she'd spent half the night realizing she was letting go of her former life without regret.

Maybe that was why she felt lighter.

They broke camp efficiently. The rhythm of it had become second nature—rolling bedrolls, distributing supplies, checking that nothing was left behind. Rachel found herself moving through the routine without thought, muscle memory adapting to this life faster than her conscious mind had.

The forest today was different again. The purple-wood trees had given way to something that looked almost like ash—pale gray bark, silver leaves that made a sound like paper rustling when the wind moved through them. The undergrowth was sparse here, just silvery moss and clusters of white flowers that closed when anyone got too near.

The path remained clear though. Those deliberate white stones marking the safe route through territory Rachel didn't fully understand but was learning to respect.

They walked for hours. Not rushed, but steady. The kind of pace that ate distance without exhausting. Rachel's body had adapted to travel in ways that surprised her—she no longer got blisters, her muscles no longer screamed after full days of walking, her breathing stayed even on inclines that should have left her winded.

Demon benefits, she supposed. A body that wasn't quite human, wasn't quite bound by human limitations.

It should have felt wrong. Alien. Proof that she wasn't who she'd thought she was.

Instead, it felt like finally working correctly after years of glitching.

They stopped for midday rest in a clearing where the silver-leaved trees created a natural canopy, light filtering through in patterns that almost looked intentional. Rachel sat with her back against smooth gray bark and ate rations that were becoming familiar—dried fruit that tasted like apples mixed with something citrus, bread that stayed fresh longer than bread should, and water from a stream that ran clearer than physics suggested was possible.

Maelin settled beside her, close enough that their shoulders brushed.

They ate in comfortable silence for a while. Around them, the others spread out—Edrin immediately absorbed in his journal, Ilyra doing her systematic scan of the perimeter, Arathen consulting that brass-and-glass device and making small adjustments to their planned route.

"You seem different today," Maelin said finally.

Rachel glanced at her. "Different how?"

"Lighter," Maelin said. "Less like you're carrying something heavy. Or like you set something down."

Rachel considered denying it. Then remembered she was done pretending. "I let someone go last night. In my head. Someone I left behind."

"The boyfriend," Maelin said. Not a question.

"Yes," Rachel confirmed. "I realized I couldn't remember what he looked like anymore. And when I tried to feel guilty about that, I just... couldn't. I felt relieved instead."

Maelin nodded slowly. "That makes sense."

"Does it?" Rachel asked. "Because it feels selfish. He's probably terrified about what happened to me, and I'm here thinking 'well, that's over, time to move on.'"

"You're not thinking that," Maelin said gently. "You're thinking 'I was never happy there, and I'm not going back to unhappiness just because it's familiar.'"

The accuracy of it made Rachel's chest tight. "Yes," she admitted. "Exactly that."

They sat with that for a moment. The silver leaves rustled overhead, creating patterns of light and shadow that moved across the ground like breathing. One of those white flowers had opened near Rachel's feet—either she'd sat still long enough that it no longer registered her as a threat, or it was curiosity overriding caution.

"Can I ask you something?" Maelin said. Her voice had changed slightly, gone careful in a way that suggested the question mattered.

"Always," Rachel said.

"Do you want to go home?"

The question landed heavier than Rachel had expected. Not because it was surprising—she'd been dancing around it for days—but because Maelin was asking it directly, without qualification or softening.

Do you want to go home.

Rachel looked at the forest around her. At the silver leaves and gray bark and white flowers that closed when you got too close. At Edrin absorbed in scholarship that mattered, Ilyra moving with purpose through a world she understood, Arathen studying problems that were actually solvable instead of just endurable.

At Maelin beside her, patient and present and accepting of exactly who Rachel was.

"No," Rachel said. The word came out quieter than she'd intended but no less certain for it. "No, I don't want to go home."

Maelin didn't react with surprise. Just nodded once, like Rachel had confirmed something instead of revealing it. "Why not?"

Rachel tried to find words for feelings she'd been avoiding examining too closely. "Because home wasn't home. It was just where I was. Where I was trying very hard to fit into a shape that didn't match my edges." She paused. "Every day there felt like work. Like I was maintaining a performance. And I was so tired, Maelin. So tired of pretending to be someone I wasn't."

"And here?" Maelin asked.

"Here I'm terrified half the time," Rachel admitted. "I don't understand most of what's happening. I have powers I don't know how to control and responsibilities I never asked for. I'm constantly reminded that I don't belong to the species I thought I did."

"But?" Maelin prompted gently.

"But I feel real," Rachel said. "Like I'm actually living instead of just existing. Like the things I do matter, the choices I make have weight. Even when I'm scared, even when I'm exhausted—it feels honest. True. Like I'm finally being myself after twenty-six years of being a very convincing imitation."

Maelin's hand found Rachel's, warm and solid. "That's not selfish," she said firmly. "That's survival. That's choosing life over existence. No one can blame you for that."

"I can," Rachel said. "I do. Because what kind of person leaves behind everyone they knew, everything they built, and just... doesn't miss it? Doesn't want to go back?"

"The kind of person who was never happy there," Maelin said. "The kind who was drowning slowly and didn't even know it until someone pulled them out." She squeezed Rachel's hand. "You're allowed to not want to go back to drowning, Rachel. Even if the water was familiar."

Rachel's vision blurred. She blinked hard against tears she hadn't given permission to form. "What if we figure out how to send me back? What if there's a way?"

"Then you'll have a choice," Maelin said simply. "And you'll make whatever choice is right for you. But Rachel—" She waited until Rachel looked at her. "You don't have to choose suffering just because it's what you're used to. You're allowed to choose happiness. You're allowed to choose belonging."
